# Strange And Weird Tales: H.P. Lovecraft And Horror In American History - Scholars Crossing

                                                                                                                       i 

                                                         LIBERTY UNIVERSITY  

DEPARTMENT OF HISTORY 

STRANGE AND WEIRD TALES: H.P. LOVECRAFT AND HORROR IN AMERICAN 

HISTORY 

A Dissertation Submitted 

by 

Serafin H. Matthews

Degree of Doctor of Philosophy in History 

Presented to the Department of History in the 

College of Arts and Sciences at 

Liberty University 

Lynchburg, Virginia

                                                                                                                       ii 

## Abstract

 Author Howard Phillips (H.P.) Lovecraft (1890-1937) of Rhode Island was a 

transformative figure in American fiction.  Hailed by one modern writer as “the twentieth-century 

horror story’s dark and baroque prince,” Lovecraft wrote over sixty-five works of fiction 

representing—alongside Clark Ashton Smith, Robert Bloch, and David H. Keller—“the golden 

age of weird fiction.”1  Lovecraft, however, enjoyed only modest commercial success during his 

lifetime, writing for amateur journals and pulp magazines such as The Vagrant, Amazing Stories, 

and Weird Tales. Most of his stories, then, were not published in hardcover or paperback form 

until after his death, including what many literary critics and historians consider his “finest novel,” 

The Case of Charles Dexter Ward (1941).2  Overall, Lovecraft’s macabre tales—characterized by 

their cosmic worldview, marked a stark break from late 19th century horror writings, making him 

the most original horror writer since Edgar Allan Poe, a writer Lovecraft considered the “God of 

Fiction.” Indeed, through the late 19th century, the horror genre was marked by vampire stories 

and other elements of folkloric tradition. By the early 20th century, however—with the emergence 

of the First World War (1914-1919), industrialization, and modernization—gothic fiction of this 

nature became less prevalent for a time. Instead, there were new horrors—loneliness, isolation, 

massive destruction, loss of religious beliefs and the new uncertainty of man’s place in an ever-

expanding universe. Inspired by these new horrors, Lovecraft tackled both the gothic elements of 

the past and the creeping uncertainty of an ever more cosmic worldview in his literary works. 

 1 Stephen King, Danse Macabre. New York: Everest House, 1981, 41.  2 Lee Daniels, Living in Fear: A History of Horror in the Mass Media. New York: DeCapo Press, 1975, 121. 

                                                                                                                       iii 

## Acknowledgements And Dedication

Many acknowledgements must be made to those who have contributed to the creation of 

this work. While this work may have taken a year to write, the mindset behind it has been 

influenced over a lifetime. 

First, I must give thanks to institutions of higher learning, such as Brown University who 

opened their archives to me to research not only Lovecraft but less known yet significant 

contemporaries of Lovecraft such as R.H. Barlow who would remain nearly unknown if not for 

his various letters and manuscripts that are kept at the John Hay Library at Brown University. 

Much thanks must also be given to scholars such as S.T. Joshi who has kept interest in H.P. 

Lovecraft and “weird literature” alive and without whom the Lovecraft scholar would be lost 

completely.  

I must thank the Liberty University History Department for allowing me to complete this 

dissertation and for preparing me for such an exploit throughout the years. 

I must thank my paternal and maternal family members. My great uncles Timothy and 

Michael Smith for opening the arts to me and for giving me intellectual stimulation throughout my 

youth. My numerous uncles and aunts who have inspired me in various ways include Mary and 

David Arroyo, Jill Matthews, Markous Matthews, Antonio Martinez, Nick and Hector Nieves and 

Frank Martinez. My grandmother Wanda Martinez who gave me my first serious literature and 

who has been my rock throughout a lifetime. My love and gratitude are also given to my 

grandfather and grandmother Diane and Hector Nieves for also making Sundays the best dinners 

anyone can ask for.  

Gratitude to Samuel Levy, my seventh-grade mathematics teacher, for being a friend and a 

teacher over the past eighteen years. 

                                                                                                                       iv 

I give my thanks and love to Ron Rhodus along with their family without whom part of 

who I am would not exist and for long endless conservations on theology, baseball and everything 

in between without which my life would not be nearly as interesting.  

My love and gratitude to my friend and advisor Dr. Michael Davis and his spouse Holly 

Davis for opening their hearts and their home. Without Dr. Michael Davis this dissertation would 

not exist. Thank you for giving me the gall to follow my interest and for listening to me. You have 

taught me more than words can express.  

My love and gratitude to Sandra Coplin-Neill for accepting me as a son. I am lucky to have 

you as a mother.  

Thank you to my mother and father, Dyanne Nieves and Serafin Matthews. Without you 

there would be no literature, no history, no words, and most of all no music. My love for you 

cannot be described and can only be felt. I feel it every day. 

Most of all love and gratitude must be given to my wife Shannon Neill-Matthews and our 

two cats Jack and Mickey. You have heard me philosophize; you have read what I wrote. You are 

always there and my life without you is unimaginable. I can only hope that I can show the unlimited 

love you have shown for me back to you. And to Jack and Mickey, even though you do not talk 

back I know you are always listening. Without all of you, what is there to write about? I love you. 

This work is dedicated to my grandmother Mary Matthews who taught me to hunger for 

education, to my great grandmother Ana Martinez, to my great uncle Norberto Rivera, to my 

grandfather Serafin Matthews, to my great grandparents Mary and William Smith, to my father in 

law Jerry Neill, and to Jane French and Jim Rhodus. Your love is felt every day.

## Introduction

Author Howard Phillips (H.P.) Lovecraft (1890-1937) of Rhode Island was a 

transformative figure in American fiction.  Hailed by one modern writer as “the twentieth-century 

horror story’s dark and baroque prince,” Lovecraft wrote over sixty-five works of fiction 

representing—alongside Clark Ashton Smith, Robert Bloch, and David H. Keller—“the golden 

age of weird fiction.”1  Lovecraft, however, enjoyed only modest commercial success during his 

lifetime, writing for amateur journals and pulp magazines such as The Vagrant, Amazing Stories, 

and Weird Tales.  Most of his stories, then, were not published in hardcover or paperback form 

until after his death, including what many literary critics and historians consider his “finest novel,” 

The Case of Charles Dexter Ward (1941).2  Overall, Lovecraft’s macabre tales marked a stark 

break from late 19th century horror writings, making him the most original horror writer since 

Edgar Allan Poe, a writer Lovecraft considered the “God of Fiction.”3  

Prior to Lovecraft, the horror genre was mostly “gothic” in nature and thus comprised of 

vampire and ghost stories. The emergence of the Industrial Revolution in the first half of the 

nineteenth century, however, unleashed “new technological effects,” including  “electromagnetic 

signals, brilliant light shows, musical innovations, clattering city streets, mesmeric emanations, 

[and] machine-oriented words.”4  Meanwhile, an intellectual tide—marked by the publication of 

Charles Darwin’s On the Origin of Species (1859),  Friedrich Nietzsche’s The Twilight of the Idols 

 1 Stephen King, Danse Macabre. (New York: Everest House, 1981), 41.  2 Lee Daniels, Living in Fear: A History of Horror in the Mass Media. (New York: DeCapo Press, 1975), 121. 3 Lovecraft quoted in Sean Moreland, ed., The Lovecraftian Poe: Essays on Influence, Reception, Interpretation and Transformation (Bethlehem, Pennsylvania: Lehigh University Press, 2017), xv.  4 John Tresch, The Reason For The Darkness of The Night: Edgar Allan Poe and the Forging of American Science. (New York: Picador, 2021), 8. 

                                                                                                                       2 

(1888),  and Sigmund Freud’s The Interpretation of Dreams (1899)—ushered in new ideas about 

Man, and Man’s place in the world.  Indeed, a leading philosophical position by the end of the 

century was nihilism.  Associated with several late nineteenth century literary figures, including 

Russian novelist Ivan Turgenev, and his Fathers and Sons (1862), nihilism challenged tradition-- 

especially religion and morality—insisting that it was meaningless.  Indeed, Nietzsche wrote in 

his The Will to Power (1906) nihilism was an “absolute repudiation of worth, purpose, 

desirability”—an insistence that “‘nothing has any purpose’… [and] that all explanations may 

perhaps be false.”5  

By the early 20th century—with the emergence of a complex, modern industrial and urban 

order, and the outbreak of the First World War (1914-1919)—gothic fiction became less prevalent. 

Instead, there were new horrors—isolation, loneliness, trench warfare, economic collapse, political 

oppression, religious disillusionment, and the new uncertainty of man’s place in an ever-expanding 

universe. Inspired by these “new horrors,” Lovecraft tackled both the gothic elements of the past 

and the creeping uncertainty of an ever more cosmic worldview in his literary works. Ultimately, 

then, Lovecraft was ahead of his time and yet could not escape the trends of his time. His 

conflicting ideals show contradictions that were major attributes and aspects of his work as well 

as his worldview and personality.  

Horror emerged as a distinct genre in Europe and the United States by the end of the 18th 

century.  Most of these early works were characterized by their gothic sensibilities, which focused 

on medieval landscapes, evil settings, and the supernatural.6 Many works—not unlike Grimm’s 

Fairytales (1812)-- contained moral lessons.  One of the first popular and trendsetting, gothic 

 5 Friedrich Nietzsche, The Will To Power (New York: Barnes and Noble, 2006), 3.   6 Eino Ralio, The Haunted Castle. (London: Routledge, 1927), 1-5.  

                                                                                                                       3 

novels was The Castle of Otranto (1764), by the English writer Horace Walpole (1717-1797), 

whom Lovecraft insisted was the “the actual founder of the literary horror-story as a permanent 

form.”7  Meanwhile, in the United States, in 1798, Charles Brocken Brown (1771-1810) , launched 

gothic literature with his novel, Wieland (1798)—a work that predicted the emergence of the horror 

tales Edgar Allan Poe and Nathainel Hawthrone with its focus on puritanism and the macabre. 

Ultimately, then, many of their works became cornerstones of not only the horror genre but also 

nineteenth century literature generally.  

In the nineteenth century, the “weird tale”-- a new subgenre of horror combining the 

various elements of speculative fiction (including horror, mystery, suspense, science fiction, and 

the supernatural)-- began to find acclaim through the works of young writers. A “weird tale,” one 

modern scholar noted was less of a genre and more of worldview centered around the idea of a 

tale that suspends belief and has a feeling of “dread of outside sources.”8 Lovecraft himself 

described “weirdness” in literature as “a strong impression of the suspension of natural laws or the 

presence of unseen worlds or forces close at hand.”9  Such a tale, then, moved beyond the “patently 

physical” horrors of gothic literature.10 A key figure in the creation and popularization of the 

“weird tale” was Edgar Allan Poe (1809-1849). Indeed, his short stories, including “The Black 

Cat” (1843), “The Tell-Tale Heart” (1843), “The Masque of the Red Death” (1842) and “The Fall 

of The House of Usher” (1839), were among the building blocks of the modern horror genre— 

including the use of both “weird fiction” and the gothic tradition. Poe, one 20th century scholar 

 7 H.P. Lovecraft, The Annotated Supernatural Horror In Literature. Ed. S.T. Joshi. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2000), 8. 8 S.T. Joshi, The Weird Tale. (Austin: University of Texas Press, 1990), 6. 9 H.P. Lovecraft to Wilfred Blanch Talman, August 24, 1926, in August Derleth and Donald Wandrei, eds., H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1925-1929 (Saux City, Wisconsin: Arkham House, 1968), 69.  10 Ibid. 

                                                                                                                       4 

observed, was “devoted to the tale of terror in America,” and in his hands it “became a fine art.”  

11 

Another 19th century American author with a wide influence on the horror genre was 

Nathaniel Hawthorne (1804-1864). Like Poe, Hawthorne brought gothic traditions into the new 

sub-genre of “weird literature.” Overall, he focused not just general the Puritanical origins of New 

England, but also his own family’s role in those origins, including the Salem Witch Trials and his 

guilt surrounding them. While many of Hawthorne’s works, including The Scarlet Letter (1850) 

and Twice-Told Tales (1837), have some horror themes, it was with The House of Seven Gables 

(1851) that-- according to Lovecraft-- Hawthorne created “New England’s greatest contribution to 

weird literature.”12  By the end of the century, “respectable” works such as The Strange Case of 

Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde (1886), by Robert Louis Stevenson, and The Picture of Dorian Gray 

(1890), by Oscar Wilde added further acceptability to the genre.  This was especially true with the 

publication of Bram Stoker’s enormously popular and influential Dracula in 1897.  Reminiscent 

in style to Willkie Collins’ 1859 “sensation novel,” The Woman in White, Stoker’s Dracula took 

the trends of gothic horror and placed them in the context of Victorian England. The result was a 

shocking, frightening, and alluring tale that was ultimately adapted into theatre productions and 

films, and that influenced a whole generation of young writers, including Lovecraft, Algernon 

Blackwood, Clark Ashton Smith, Robert E. Howard, Arthur Machen, and M.R. James. 

However, one of the most consequential forms of nineteenth century “horror” came outside 

of literary fiction in the form of “penny dreadfuls” and, later, pulp magazines.  “Penny dreadfuls” 

were serial stories—usually only around ten or fifteen pages in length—that were printed on cheap, 

 11 Edith Birkhead, The Tale of Terror (London: Constable, 1921), v.  12 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror in Literature, 30. 

                                                                                                                       5 

pulp paper, and cost one penny.  They first emerged in Great Britain in the 1820s and consisted of 

both original stories and classics divided into serial form.  One early “penny dreadful” was Varney 

the Vampire; or, the Feast of Blood (1845-1847), by James Malcom Rymer and Thomas Peckett 

Prest, which introduced some popular themes regarding vampires, including fangs, superhuman 

strength, and hypnotic powers.  Because of their cheap price, “penny dreadfuls” gained wide 

appeal among working class Europeans.  By the 1890s, especially in the United States, they were 

supplanted by pulp magazines.  The pulps were the American counterparts to the penny dreadfuls.  

The very first one was Argosy, founded by Frank Munsey of Maine, in 1896.13  By the early 

twentieth century, the rapidly expanding pulp industry was catering to more off-beat tastes, and 

were sometimes criticized as lurid, sadistic, and grotesque.14  

H.P. Lovecraft was born in Providence, Rhode Island on August 20, 1890, the only son of 

Winfield Scott Lovecraft and Sarah Susan (“Susie”) Philips. Lovecraft’s family was wealthy, and 

his early years were spent in relative comfort.  Soon, however, tragedy struck, and Lovecraft’s 

youth ultimately became one of crippling loss and sadness.   In 1893, his father—possibly suffering 

from tertiary syphilis—experienced a nervous breakdown and was institutionalized at Butler 

Hospital, a psychiatric facility, in Providence.  He remained there until his death in 1898.  

Meanwhile, Lovecraft’s mother became “permanently stricken with grief,” and slowly deteriorated 

both physically and mentally. Lovecraft was a bright and pampered child, who quickly learned the 

alphabet at the age of two and was reading by the time he was three years old.  Following his 

maternal grandfather’s death in 1904, the family’s wealth quickly evaporated, and Lovecraft and 

his mother were forced to move into a small apartment.  Lovecraft—now in high school-- found 

 13 The Argosy began in 1882 as a children’s weekly, The Golden Argosy, and then rebranded in 1888, as a boys’ adventure magazine.  In 1896, Munsey began issuing it as a monthly printed on cheap, pulp paper. 14 Bradford W. Wright, Comic Book Nation: The Transformation of Youth Culture in America. (Baltimore: Johns Hopkins University Press), 2001. 

                                                                                                                       6 

solace in reading ancient works of Roman history, astronomy, chemistry and most importantly the 

gothic works of authors such as Edgar Allan Poe.  

In 1905, Lovecraft, aged fifteen, wrote his first short story, “The Beast in the Cave.” It was 

not his first foray into writing, however.  In the late nineteenth century, amateur journalism— 

coinciding with inexpensive printing costs-- became a popular movement among American youth.  

Works of amateur journalism included not only news items, but also poetry and short fiction.  

Lovecraft was an enthusiastic and prolific amateur journalist.  Indeed, between 1903 and 1909, he 

produced sixty-nine issues of his “self-published” Rhode Island Journal of Astronomy. He also 

published several other scientific journals, including Planet (1903), a daily Scientific Gazette 

(1899), and a two-volume collection of poetry, Poemata Minora (1902).  Unsurprisingly, his 

expected life path—from an early age-- was an education from Brown University and a career in 

journalism.  In 1908, however, Lovecraft suffered a “nervous collapse,” and thus never even 

graduated high school.  “I was and am prey to intense headaches, insomnia, and general nervous 

weakness which prevents my continuous application to any thing [sic],” Lovecraft later wrote.15  

In a letter from 1908, he confessed that he “could hardly bear to see or speak to anyone, & liked 

to shut out the world by pulling down dark shades & using artificial light.”16  For the next several 

years, Lovecraft lived in relative seclusion. 

Then, in 1914, he wrote in poetic verse to the pulp magazine Argosy to complain about 

the stories of popular author Fred Jackson (1886-1953), which opened a lively debate in the 

Letter Column of the pulp for the next several months, and attracted the attention of Edward F. 

Daas, president of the United Amateur Press Association.  Daas invited Lovecraft to join the 

 15 Lovecraft to R.H. Barlow, April 10, 1934, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., O Fortunate Floridian: H.P. Lovecraft’s Letters to R.H. Barlow. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2016), 125. 16Ibid.,  125. 

                                                                                                                       7 

association, which fueled the productivity of the young amateur journalist.  In 1915, Lovecraft 

began publication of The Conservative, an amateur journal which ran for thirteen issues, 

spanning the period from 1915 to 1923.  It was during this early period, then, that Lovecraft’s 

worldview was shaped, and articulated.  Without question, the young Rhode Island writer was 

influenced by the intellectual tides of the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, as well as 

his own personality. 

For example, Lovecraft adhered to a post-Civil War, “Lost Cause” romanticism that 

informed his views on race.  Indeed, this pro-South interpretation of the Antebellum South, the 

origins of the Civil War, and Reconstruction was prominent across the country by the early 

twentieth century.  Writing in his five-volumed History of the American People (1902), 

Woodrow Wilson expressed the largely accepted view of both the war and slavery among white 

scholars.17  According to Wilson, black slaves—uncapable of either independent thought or 

moral action—were “faithful and steady at their accustomed tasks… devoted in the service of 

their masters.”  After the war, he added, “The government would find land for them, would feed 

them and give them clothes… They had the easy faith, the simplicity, the idle hopes, the 

inexperience of children. Their masterless, homeless freedom had made them more pitiable, the 

more dependent, because under slavery they had been shielded, the weak and the incompetent 

with the strong and capable.”18 

  Lovecraft’s writings in The Conservative in the 1910s reflected these racial attitudes.  

Indeed, in his essay “In A Major Key” (1915), Lovecraft’s defended the newly released 

Hollywood blockbuster film, The Birth of a Nation (1915).  The film, based upon the 1905 novel, 

 17 Historian W.E.B. Du Bois, however, boldly resisted this “Lost Cause” romanticism in an essay published in the American Historical Review in 1910 and entitled “Reconstruction and Its Benefits.” 18 Woodrow Wilson, History of the American People, Volume V. (New York: Harper and Brothers, 1902), 18.  

                                                                                                                       8 

The Clansman, by Thomas Dixon, Jr., portrayed the Ku Klux Klan as the heroic defender of 

white, Southern, and female virtue.  While Lovecraft admitted the film was “crude and 

melodramatic,” he nevertheless insisted it promoted “honour [sic], chivalry and patriotism.”19  

Lovecraft, then, not only embraced the film’s positive portrayal of the Klan, but also its 

disparagement of African Americans.  “The negro” he wrote, “is fundamentally the biological 

inferior of all White and even Mongolian races, and the Northern people must occasionally be 

reminded of the danger they incur by admitting him to freely to the privileges of society and 

government…Race prejudice is a gift of Nature, intended to preserve in purity the various 

divisions of mankind which the ages have evolved.”20 

Lovecraft was also heavily influenced by Friedrich Nietzsche (1844-1900), and to a 

lesser degree, Sigmund Freud (1856-1939)—both of whom advanced materialist and nihilistic, 

philosophical positions.  “With Nietzsche,” Lovecraft wrote in 1919, “I have been forced to 

confess that mankind as a whole has no goal or purpose whatsoever, but is a mere superfluous 

speck in the unfathomable vortices of infinity and eternity.”21   Writing to a friend in early 1921, 

Lovecraft insisted that the specific importance of Nietzsche was his call “to remove morbid 

erotic interest by removing the prejudices and inhabitations which make it doubly strong.  It is 

quite possible that the net amount of vulgarism would thus be decreased rather than increased; 

surely the decrease in hypocrisy would make for a gain in wholesomeness… Thus have I 

changed my views… What is inherent in the majority cannot be… molded to an ideal society and 

literature; society and literature must conform to man as he is.”  As to the overall condition of 

 19 H.P. Lovecraft, “In A Major Key.” In Joshi. Collected Essays: Volume 1: Amateur Journalism. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004), 6. 20 Ibid., 6. 21 H.P. Lovecraft to Reinhardt Kleiner, September 14, 1919, in August Derleth and Donald Wandrei, eds., H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1911-1924 (Sauk City, Wisconsin: Arkham House, 1965), 86. 

                                                                                                                       9 

Man, Lovecraft was pessimistic. “I expect nothing of man, and disown the race.  The only folly 

is in expecting what is never attained; man is most contemptible when compared with his own 

pretensions.  It is better to laugh at man from outside the universe, than to weep for him 

within…”22      

While Lovecraft described Nietzsche’s writings as “a tonic to the intellect,” the German 

philosopher was not above criticism.  “Lest you fancy that I am making an idol of Nietzsche,” 

Lovecraft wrote in 1921, “…let me state clearly that I do not swallow him whole.  His ethical 

system is a joke—or a poet’s dream, which amounts to the same thing.”23  Furthermore, he did 

not believe Nietzsche stood alone in cutting through “the artificial fog of empty, resonant, 

mystical words.”24  He also highly regarded the works of Arthur Schopenhauer, as well as Hugh 

Elliot’s Modern Science and Materialism (1919).  Still, Lovecraft believed that it was in 

Nietzsche’s “method, and his account of the basic origin and actual relation of existing ideas and 

standards” that made “him the master figure of the modern age and founder of unvarnished 

sincerity in philosophic thought.”25  When a “voluble friend” once insisted that was “egotistical 

from reading Nietzsche,” Lovecraft replied that it “disturbeth [sic] me not in the least.”26  As to 

Freud—who was only rarely mentioned in Lovecraft’s letters--  Lovecraft noted early in his 

writing career that Freud’s “system of psycho-analysis… will probably prove the end of 

idealistic thought.”  Like Nietzsche, though, Freud too had limitations.  “I am inclined to accept 

the modifications of [psychologist Alfred] Adler,” Lovecraft wrote, “who in placing the ego 

 22 H.P. Lovecraft to Reinhardt Kleiner, April 13, 1921, in Ibid., 130. 23 Lovecraft to Anne Tillery Renshaw, June 1, 1921, in Ibid., 134. 24 Ibid. 25 Ibid. 26 Lovecraft to Reinhardt Kleiner, August 30, 1921, in Ibid., 149 

                                                                                                                       10 

above the eros makes a scientific return to the position which Nietzsche assumed for wholly 

philosophical reasons.”27   

The influence of Nietzsche and nihilism was evident—as was Lovecraft’s own personally 

(and drift toward seclusion and pessimism).  “I have not much interest in anything now unless it 

is wild and weird,” Lovecraft wrote a friend in 1921.  “I am so beastly tired of mankind and the 

world that nothing can interest me unless it contains a couple of murders on each page or deals 

with horrors unnamable and unaccountable that leer down from the external universes.”28   While 

he had, he wrote friend Reinhardt Kleiner in 1919, “‘nothing to live for,’ I certainly have just as 

much as any other insignificant bacteria called human beings.  I have thus been content to 

observe the phenomena about me with something like objective interest, and to feel a certain 

tranquility which comes from perfect acceptance of my place as an inconsequential atom.”29  

Indeed, he added, “Utter happiness… is in most cases an unattainable impossibility… Consider 

yourself an impersonal observer without emotions, and have as your aim in life the tranquil 

observation and classification of facts about you.  I am sure that I, who hardly knows what an 

emotion is like (outside of a few bursts of honest anger once in a while!), am far less vexed than 

he who is constantly straining after new sensations…”  In the end, he advised aspiring writers, 

“Be a scientist instead of a litterateur.”30   

Lovecraft later elaborated on his worldview in a 1922 essay entitled “A Confession of 

Unfaith.”  According to Lovecraft, “The [Versailles] Peace Conference, Friedrich Nietzsche, 

Samuel Butler (the modern), H.L. Mencken and other influences have perfected my cynicism; a 

 27 Lovecraft to Anne Tillery Renshaw, June 1, 1921, in Ibid., 134. 28 Lovecraft to Reinhardt Kleiner, April 13, 1921, in Ibid., 128. 29 Lovecraft to Reinhardt Kleiner, September 14, 1921, in Ibid., 86. 30 Ibid., 87. 

                                                                                                                       11 

quality which grows more intense as the advent of middle life removes the blind prejudice whereby 

youth clings to the vapid ‘all’s right with the world’ hallucination from sheer force of desire to 

have it so.  As I near thirty-two I have no particular wishes, save to perceive the facts as they are.”31  

In Lovecraft’s stories, then, one modern scholar observed, “life has no meaning. But neither does 

death. And this is another thing that curdles the blood when one discovers Lovecraft’s universe. 

The deaths of his heroes have no meaning. Death brings no appeasement. It in no way allows the 

story to conclude.”32  

Lovecraft’s literary career began in earnest in 1916, after his short story, “The Alchemist,” 

was published in the United Amateur Press Association journal.  Lovecraft subsequently 

concentrated more on short fiction, launching a career in pulp magazines such as Weird Tales and 

Astounding Stories.  Tragedy soon struck again, however.  In 1919, his mother, Susie—suffering 

from an “abnormality—was admitted to Butler Hospital, where she resided until her death in May 

1921.  That same year, Lovecraft met Sonia Green, a fellow amateur journalist and writer who 

deeply appreciated Lovecraft’s writing. The couple spent much of 1922 and 1923 visiting and 

corresponding with each other. In 1924, Lovecraft and Greene were married leading Lovecraft to 

relocate to the fashionable Flatbush neighborhood in Brooklyn, New York where Greene had 

resided. Lovecraft hoped that in New York he could gain reasonable employment as a writer or 

editor.  Ultimately, Lovecraft, who disliked New York, wrote very little during his time there, and 

suffered financially, physically, and emotionally. Indeed, due to financial difficulties, Lovecraft 

relocated to what was then the slums of Brooklyn Heights where-- except for meetings with a few 

 31 H.P. Lovecraft, “A Confession of Unfaith.” In Joshi. Collected Essays: Volume 5: Philosophy: Autobiography and Miscellany. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004), 147. 32 Michel Houellbecq, H.P. Lovecraft: Against The World. Against Life. (Paris: Cernunnos, 2018), 41. 

                                                                                                                       12 

friends-- he spent much of his time dreaming of New England. By 1926, Lovecraft’s marriage was 

disintegrating, and he decided to return home to Providence without his wife’s company.  

Lovecraft’s return to Providence in 1926 left him rejuvenated by familiar surroundings that 

led to him writing more than ten stories or fragments in four years, including “The Call of the 

Cthulhu” (1928) and “The Colour Out of Space” (1927), and his only novel, The Case of Charles 

Dexter Ward (written in 1927 but not published until 1941).  However, after the rejection and cold 

reception of his masterpiece, the novella At the Mountains of Madness (1936), his writing career 

slowed to a near crawl.  During this time, however, he maintained a rich correspondence with other 

pulp and “weird” writers, including Robert Hayward Barlow, August Derleth, Clark Ashton Smith, 

and Donald Wandrei, that numbered—according to Lovecraft biographer S.T. Joshi—over 

100,000 letters.33  Ultimately, Lovecraft’s career never become financially viable, and he died— 

of cancer at the age of forty-six--  a relatively obscure writer in March 1937.  Still, he had 

assembled a dedicated, if small fanbase, and influenced a rising generation of horror writers, 

including Robert Bloch—author of Psycho (1959) and American Gothic (1974)—whom Lovecraft 

personally encouraged.  “The news of Lovecraft’s death seems incredible and nightmarish, and I 

cannot adjust myself to it,” Derleth wrote to Smith only a few weeks after his death.34 

Over the course of his short life, Lovecraft wrote over sixty short stories and novellas, and 

one novel—many of which were published posthumously.  Most of these stories, August Derleth 

later observed, fell into two general categories:  tales of the “fantastic” and the “weird and terrible 

tales of cosmic outsideness.”35  Indeed, Lovecraft, an atheist, possessed a “cosmic” worldview that 

 33 S.T. Joshi, A Subtler Magick: The Writings and Philosophy of H.P. Lovecraft. (Berkeley Heights, New Jersey: Wildside Press, 1996), 236-242. 34 S.T. Joshi, I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013, 1009. 35 August Derleth, “H.P. Lovecraft and His Work” in The Dunwich Horror and Others. (Sauk City, Wisconsin: Arkham House, 1963), xiii. 

                                                                                                                       13 

he himself developed. Cosmicism—as expressed in Lovecraft’s writings-- was a literary 

philosophy grounded in the belief that there was no divine presence or plan, and that Man was 

generally insignificant in relation to the larger universe.  For Lovecraft, then, “nothing was so 

essentially terrifying to the human spirit as a dislocation in space and time.”36  Still, his works— 

especially those connected to his Cthulhu Mythos developed in the 1920s—reflected a struggle 

between Good and Evil.  “All my stories, unconnected as they may be,” Lovecraft once wrote, 

“are based on the fundamental lore or legend that this world was inhabited at one time by another 

race who, in practicing black magic, lost their foothold and were expelled, yet live on outside ever 

ready to take possession of this earth again.”37   

Interest in Lovecraft’s macabre tales exploded after his death.  In 1939, Derleth and Donald 

Wandrei, two friends of Lovecraft, created the publishing company Arkham House for the primary 

reason of publishing Lovecraft’s works in hardcover editions. In 1939, its first publication, The 

Outsider and other Stories was released followed by Beyond the Wall of Sleep in 1943. Arkham 

House quickly became one of the most popular small presses of the time, publishing works by 

various renowned horror, fantasy and science fiction writers, including Clark Ashton Smith, 

Ramsey Cambell, Ray Bradbury, and Robert E. Howard. However, controversy surrounded 

Arkham House due to Derleth’s editing of Lovecraft’s short story volumes and selected letters 

which was rumored to be quite free in rewriting some of Lovecraft’s works or creating new works 

based on his ideas. Other Lovecraft editions published by Arkham House included The Shuttered 

Room and other Pieces (1959), The Dunwich Horror and Others (1963), and At the Mountains of 

 36 Ibid., xvi. 37 Lovecraft quoted in Derleth., xiii. 

                                                                                                                       14 

Madness and other Novels (1964).  By the end of the twentieth century, he was the subject of major 

biographies by Frank Belknap Long, L. Sprague de Camp, Michel Houellebecq and S.T. Joshi. 

Overall, H.P. Lovecraft is important to the history of literature and the horror genre for 

three reasons. First, he contributed to the transformation of “weird literature” from its gothic roots 

to a new style of literature that with its merging of scientific fiction and horror would come to be 

known as “cosmic horror.”  Prior to Lovecraft, horror fiction—rooted in an age of lamps and 

candlelight—was characterized by ghosts, vampires, and mysterious occurrences.  The twentieth 

century, however, ushered in a noisy and fast generation powered by electricity, leading one 

English writer to conclude in the mid-1930s that “ghosts went out when electricity came in.”38  For 

Lovecraft, horror was beyond ghosts or vampires.  It was rooted instead in meaninglessness and 

nothingness. “It is most sensible just to accept the universe as it is and be done with it,” Lovecraft 

wrote in his 1926 essay, “The Materialist Today.”  “All is an illusion, hollowness, and nothingness-

but what does that matter? Illusions are all we have, so let us pretend to cling to them; they lend 

dramatic values and comforting sensations of purpose to things that are really valueless and 

purposeless.”39  

Lovecraft felt that he understood the valuelessness and meaninglessness of society on both 

the cosmic and psychological level and so used the “comforting sensation” of fiction to discuss 

these thoughts in a quite fitting theme: the horror story. Sometimes—as in his short story “The 

Outsider” (1921)—horror was loneliness and rejection by society.  More often, his horror—as 

expressed in stories like “The Dunwich Horror” (1929) and “The Colour Out of Space” (1927)— 

was grounded in an “outside evil”—i.e. evil “coming from outside like a stroke of lightening.”  

 38 Osbert Sitwell quoted in Edith Wharton, Ghosts. (New York: D. Appleton-Century Company, 1937), x. 39 H.P. Lovecraft, “The Materialist Today.” In Joshi. Collected Essays: Volume 5: Philosophy: Autobiography and Miscellany. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004), 76. 

                                                                                                                       15 

Indeed, modern horror writer Stephen King observed in 1981, Lovecraft’s stories “make us feel 

the size of the universe we hang suspended in, and suggest shadowy forces that could destroy us 

all if they so much as grunted in their sleep.” In the end, King added, “what is the paltry inside evil 

of the A-bomb when compared to [Lovecraft’s] Nyarlathotep, the Crawling Chaos, or Yog-

Sothoth, the Goat with a Thousand Young?”40 

Second, Lovecraft elevated the “weird tale” into the realm of serious literary fiction.  

Lovecraft himself certainly believed the genre to be worthy of serious study.  In a 1927 essay 

entitled “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” he insisted that “The oldest and strongest emotion of 

mankind is fear, and the oldest and strongest kind of fear is fear of the unknown.  These facts few 

psychologists will dispute, and their admitted truth must establish for all time the genuineness  and 

dignity  of the weirdly horrible tale as a literary form.”41  While initially dismissed as a mere “pulp 

writer,” some scholars—as early as 1945-- acknowledged Lovecraft’s literary importance.42  

Indeed, mid-twentieth century Edgar Allan Poe scholar, Thomas Ollive Mabbott, even insisted 

that Lovecraft possessed an “honorable place” in American fiction.43  Certainly, Lovecraft 

partisans, such as Derleth at Arkham House, promoted Lovecraft as “an American master of the 

macabre of the same calibre [sic] as Poe, Bierce, Hawthorne, Mary E. Wilkins-Freeman, F. Marion 

Crawford, Edith Wharton and a very few others.”44  In 1979, a scholarly journal named Lovecraft 

Studies was created to discuss his works, showing that his oeuvre was starting to be taken seriously 

 40 Stephen King, Danse Macabre.  (New York: Everest House, 1981), 71-72. 41Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror in Literature, 21. 42 See Marjorie Farber, “Subjectivity in Modern Fiction,” Kenyon Review (Autumn 1945) quoted in Derleth, “H.P. Lovecraft and His Works,” xviii-xix. 43 Mabbott quoted in Ibid., xvii. 44 Dustjacket, H.P. Lovecraft, The Dunwich Horror and Others. (Sauk City, Wisconsin: Arkham House), 1963. 

                                                                                                                       16 

by the literary establishment.  Then, in 2005, Lovecraft’s Tales, edited by Peter Straub, was added 

to the distinguished Library of America literary series. 

And third, Lovecraft left a legacy as perhaps the most important intellectual, and artistic 

horror writer to emerge from the pulp magazines of the early twentieth century, influencing 

generations of modern horror writers.  In addition to Robert Bloch, modern authors such as Ray 

Bradbury, Peter Straub, Clive Barker, Michel Houellebecq, and Stephen King all expressed 

reference for Lovecraft.   King, who first came across a copy of Lovecraft’s The Lurking Fear and 

Other Stories (1947) in the early 1960s, later insisted that “Lovecraft… opened the way for me as 

he had done for others before me… it is his shadow, so long and gaunt, and his eyes, so dark and 

puritanical, which overlie almost all of the important horror fiction that has come since.”45  In the 

dedication of his 2014 novel, Revival, King included Lovecraft—alongside Mary Shelley, Bram 

Stoker, Arthur Machen, Shirley Jackson, August Derleth, Donald Wandrei, and Clark Ashton 

Smith—as “some of the people who built my house.”46 

Ultimately, then, Lovecraft—fusing the various trends in horror writing from the 

eighteenth and nineteenth centuries—established a “new horror” that often-mimicked Gothic form 

while providing no moral or ethical substance.  Indeed, for Lovecraft horror was not rooted in 

death, grief, dissatisfaction, or a struggle between good and evil.  There was no sense of ethical or 

moral dilemma, then, in Lovecraft’s writing.  Instead, Lovecraft’s horror—steeped in twentieth 

century nihilism and a cosmic worldview—advanced a new fear—i.e. that life was meaningless 

and the individual insignificant.  Lovecraft’s protagonists—often disoriented (and unsure if even 

what they were saying or experiencing was true)-- did not know their “place” or purpose, and 

 45 Stephen King quoted in Curt Wohleber, “The Man Who Can Scare Stephen King,” American Heritage (December 1995). https://www.americanheritage.com/man-who-can-scare-stephen-king 46 Stephen King, Revival. (New York: Scribner, 2014). 

                                                                                                                       17 

neither—for that matter-- did his “monsters.”  For Lovecraft, then, there was no point to 

existence—only madness.    

The themes and content of horror writing—like other artistic forms—often reflect the times 

(i.e. concerns, values, and experiences) of the individual horror writer.  In the early twentieth 

century—against the backdrop of world war, economic collapse, and a developed industrial and 

urban order—Lovecraft’s stories resonated with a “lost generation” that felt empty, isolated, and 

adrift.  The tumult and course of the rest of the twentieth century—especially with the dawn of the 

atomic age—only reinforced an affinity for Lovecraft’s stories.  Furthermore, Stephen King 

recently observed, Lovecraft’s “lumbering poetry” and “unearthly range of imaginative vision,” 

only added to his appeal.  “His screams of horror,” King insisted, “are lucid.”47  Ultimately, then, 

according to King, Lovecraft’s literary significance transcended mere and continued popularity 

among “generation after generation of maturing readers.”  Instead, it was grounded in that he was 

“viscerally important to an imaginative core group that goes on to write that generation’s fantasy 

and weird tales… and, by doing so, to chart that generation’s deepest fears.”48 

Unsurprisingly, then, Lovecraft is the subject of voluminous literature. While he never 

wrote an autobiography or memoir, Lovecraft did leave behind several important essays on a 

variety of subjects, including science, philosophy, and horror writing. His most important non-

fiction work as it related to literature was his lengthy essay “Supernatural Horror in Literature” 

(1927), which provided a broad survey of horror literature, with particular focus on key early 

figures such as Horace Walpole, Mary Shelley, Washington Irving, Nathaniel Hawthorne, and 

Edgar Allan Poe, as well as “modern masters” such as Arthur Machen, Algernon Blackwood, Lord 

 47 Stephen King, “Lovecraft’s Pillow,” in Michel Houellbecq, H.P. Lovecraft: Against the World. Against Life. (Paris: Cernunnos, 2019), 15. 48 Ibid., 16-17. 

                                                                                                                       18 

Dunsany, and M.R. James.   Lovecraft was also a prolific letter writer.  Many of these letters— 

numbering in the thousands-- were written to friends and associates, including fellow writers, and 

can be found in the extensive H.P. Lovecraft Collection at Brown University.  Ultimately, his vast 

correspondence and numerous manuscripts have given scholars much material with which to work. 

Lovecraft historiography began with August Derleth and the publishing company 

Arkham House, which provided some the first book collections of Lovecraft’s stories.  Derleth 

also included lengthy introductions to the collections, including the essay “H.P. Lovecraft and 

His Work” for The Dunwich Horrors and Others (1963).  In 1971, L. Sprague de Camp, 

published the first major biography of Lovecraft, simply entitled H.P. Lovecraft: A Biography.  

The work-- based upon Lovecraft’s letters, and the author’s own interviews with Lovecraft 

associates-- was grounded heavily in psychoanalysis.  Four years later, Howard Philips 

Lovecraft: Dreamer on the Night Side (1975), was published by Frank Belknap Long, who had 

“exchanged numerous letters with… [Lovecraft], and met and talked with him at length at least 

500 times—in New York, in Providence, and in the New England seacoast towns great historical 

or antiquarian interest.”49  Then, in 1996, S.T. Joshi’s monumental H.P. Lovecraft: A Life was 

released, surpassing all previous biographies. Joshi expanded this work into a two-volume study 

in 2013 entitled I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft, an innovative biography 

that studied Lovecraft on a day-by-day basis, and that established Joshi as the leading Lovecraft 

scholar. Joshi also published several essays on Lovecraft, including “Time, Space, and Natural 

Law: Science and Pseudo-Science in Lovecraft” (2010), which explored the importance of 

 49 Frank Belknap Long, Howard Philips Lovecraft: Dreamer on The Nightshade. (Cabin John, MD: Wildside Press, 2016). 

                                                                                                                       19 

science to Lovecraft and his work, and “Poe, Lovecraft, and the Revolution in Weird Fiction” 

(2012), which examined how Poe transformed the gothic horror genre. 

Then there was Michel Houellebecq’s H.P. Lovecraft: Against the World, Against Life 

(2019)—originally published in French in 1991.  Overall, Houellebecq argued “Lovecraft was one 

of the most important American writers on the twentieth century.”50  Specifically, he at focused 

on Lovecraft’s writing process—particularly during his time in New York City in the mid-1920s.    

That process, Houellebecq insisted, was connected to Lovecraft’s materialism (i.e. the belief that 

physical matter is the primary substance of nature, and that all things could be explained through 

physical interactions) and racism. According to Houellebecq, Lovecraft’s body of work was 

comprised of “purely imaginary creations” and “few concessions to the real.”51 Despite his 

boundless and “horrific” imagination, Lovecraft was a strident materialist who “exploded the 

casings of the traditional narrative through his systematic use of scientific terms and concepts,” 

combining materialism (which rejected “the supernatural”) with fantasy.52 This fusion of 

materialism and fantasy in some of Lovecraft’s “great texts”—including  “The Call of Cthulhu”, 

The Colour Out of Space” and “At the Mountains of Madness”-- lead to a “founding mythology” 

which, in turn, inspired mid-twentieth century writers like Robert Bloch and August Derleth to 

“develop and enrich the myths created by HPL.”53 Racism, Houellebecq added, was another 

important aspect of Lovecraft’s work.  “[A]s an author of horror fiction (and one of the finest),” 

Houellebecq insisted, “he brutally takes racism back to its essential and most profound core: fear. 

His own life is an illustration of this. A country gentleman, convinced of the superiority of his 

 50 Stephen King, “Lovecraft’s Pillow” in in Michel Houellebecq, H.P. Lovecraft: Against the World. Against Life. (Paris: Cernunnos, 2019), 16. 51 Michel Houellebecq, H.P. Lovecraft: Against the World. Against Life. (Paris: Cernunnos, 2019), 31. 52 Ibid. 53 Ibid., 44. 

                                                                                                                       20 

Anglo-Saxon origins, he felt only a remote disdain for other races. His stay in New York’s 

underbelly, in its slums, would change all that. The foreign creatures became competitors, enemies, 

who were close by and whose brute strength far surpassed his. It was then, in a progressive delirium 

of masochism and terror, that came his calls to massacre.”54  

Ultimately, Houellebecq observed, Lovecraft’s worldview led to a “technical assault” on 

the reader in that he employed scientific, architectural, and psychological substance and imagery 

to convey an “absolute hatred of the world in general, aggravated by an aversion to the modern 

world in particular.”55 Thus, Lovecraft’s nihilistic body of work revealed a man “against the world 

and against life.”56 Despite the pessimism of Lovecraft’s writings, Houellebecq concluded,  “It’s 

clear why reading Lovecraft is paradoxically comforting to those souls who are weary of life. In 

fact, it should perhaps be prescribed to all who, for one reason or other, have come to feel a true 

aversion to life in all its forms. In some cases, the jolt to the nerves upon a first reading is immense. 

One may find oneself smiling all alone or humming a tune from a musical. One’s outlook on 

existence is, in a word, modified.”57    

Lovecraft’s posthumous popularity, then, was rooted in his identity as an outsider. In 

general, “weird fiction, fiction of horror and the supernatural, utters a resounding ‘No!’ to the 

world as it is and reality as the world insists it must be.”58  Furthermore, Houellebecq wrote, “More 

so today than ever before Lovecraft would have been a misfit and a recluse. Born in 1890, he 

already appeared to his contemporaries, in the years of his youth, to be an obsolete reactionary. 

It’s not hard to imagine what he would have thought of our society today. Since his death, it has 

 54 Ibid., 31. 55 Ibid., 71. 56 Stephen King, “Lovecraft’s Pillow” in Ibid., 17. 57 Ibid., 42-43. 58 Stephen King, “Lovecraft’s Pillow” in Ibid., 17. 

                                                                                                                       21 

not ceased evolving in a direction which could only have led him to hate it more. Mechanization 

and modernization have ineluctably destroyed the lifestyle he was attached to with his every fiber 

(it is not as if he harbored any delusions about humanity’s ability to influence events; as he wrote 

in a letter, ‘Everything in modern existence is a direct & absolute corollary of the discoveries of 

applied steam power & of large-scale applications of electrical energy.’ The ideals of liberty and 

of democracy that he so abhorred have spread all over the planet. The man who declared: “What 

we detest is simply change itself” could only have bristled at the degree to which the idea of 

progress has come to be an indisputable and almost unconscious credo. The reach of liberal 

capitalism has extended over minds; in step and hand in hand with it are mercantilism, publicity, 

the absurd and sneering cult of economic efficiency, the exclusive and immoderate appetite for 

material riches. Worse still, liberalism has spread from the domain of economics to the domain of 

sexuality. Every sentimental fiction has been eradicated. Purity, chastity, fidelity, and decency are 

ridiculous stigmas. The value of a human being today is measured in terms of his economic 

efficiency and his erotic potential—that is to say, in terms of the two things that Lovecraft most 

despised.”59  

 There are a number works that deal with specific elements of Lovecraft’s life and career.  

For example, in “A Weird Modernist Archive: Pulp Fiction, Pseudobiblia, H.P. Lovecraft” (2010), 

Leif Sorensen explored Lovecraft’s affiliation with the modernists, arguing that Lovecraft’s work 

in the pulp magazines had much in common with works of literary fiction and was not confined— 

either  stylistically or substantively—to pulp literature.60 Meanwhile, in Bibliographic Citation in 

H.P. Lovecraft’s The Case of Charles Dexter Ward (2015), Emil Hardman examined Lovecraft’s 

 59 Ibid., 135. 60 Leif Sorensen, “A Weird Modernist Archive: Pulp Fiction, Pseudobiblia, H.P. Lovecraft.” Modernism/Modernity 17, no.3 (2010): 502, https://muse.jhu.edu/article/406818 

                                                                                                                       22 

obsession with the history of literature.  That same year, Christoper Sharett’s “The Haunter of the 

Dark: H.P. Lovecraft and Modern Horror Cinema” (2015) detailed Lovecraft’s influence on films, 

including Howard Hawks’s seminal horror-science fiction film The Thing from Another World 

(1951)-- based loosely off At The Mountain of Madness-- and John Carpenter’s 1982 remake of 

The Thing, as well as his 1994 film, In the Mouth of Madness.61  More recently,  Jose Luis Arroyo 

Barriguete, in “Sentiment Analysis of Lovecraft’s Fiction Writings” (2023) organized Lovecraft’s 

fiction into three distinct periods or classifications: the Macabre (grounded in Poe), the Dream 

Cycle (steeped in the fantasy influences of Lord Dunsany), and the Cthulu Mythos (representing 

Lovecraft at his most innovative).62  The most recent book on Lovecraft is Midnight Rambles: H.P. 

Lovecraft in Gotham (2023), by David J. Goodwins, which, as the titles suggests, focuses on 

Lovecraft’s relationship with New York City—and specifically his xenophobia. 

There are also many works on the horror genre itself.   One of the first was Eino Ralio’s 

The Haunted Castle (1927), which provided a historical and literary analysis of gothic literature in 

connection with the Romantic Movement of the nineteenth century, arguing that the genre 

spearheaded a revolution in an artistic field while creating its own specific themes. Similarly, The 

Tale of Horror (1920), by Edith Birkhead detailed the development of the genre as a serious art. 

Specifically, Birkhead examined the transition from the gothic novel to the horror one, like Ralio, 

ended with Poe and Hawthrone who, she insisted, transformed horror into a fine art.63 

By the late twentieth century, scholarship emerged on “penny dreadfuls” and pulp 

magazines.  For example, Patrick A. Dunae’s “Penny Dreadfuls: Late Nineteenth-Century Boys’ 

 61 Christoper Sharrett, “The Haunter of the Dark: H.P. Lovecraft and Modern Horror Cinema” Cineaste Vol 41, No. 1 (Winter 2015), 24. 62 Jose Luis Arroyo Barriguete, “Sentiment Analysis of Lovecraft’s Fiction Writings.” Hellyon 9, no.1 (2023): 1, https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2405844022039615 63 Edith Birkhead, The Tale of Terror. (London: Constable, 1921), 1, 

                                                                                                                       23 

Literature and Crime” (1979) examined the rise of the “Penny Dreadfuls”, a popular literature that 

was originally published in serial periodicals and included works as colorfully named as Varney 

the Vampire which became incredibly popular. Dunae also described how the Penny Dreadfuls 

transformed from serial periodicals read by adults of all ages to-- by the end of the nineteenth 

century-- primarily juvenile literature.64 Meanwhile, Robert Weinberg’s masterful The Weird 

Tales Story (1977) detailed the development of the most important pulp magazine to publish horror 

fiction, Weird Tales (founded in 1922).65  According to Weinberg,  Weird Tales had a unique roster 

of writers that were well respected not only in the horror field but in general fiction (such as Ray 

Bradbury).  Then, in Hard-Boiled: Working Class Reader and Pulp Magazines (2000), Erin A. 

Smith surveyed the rise of and importance of pulp magazines in mass culture. Although Smith 

study focused on pulp mystery magazines, it included important information about the importance 

of pulp magazines to publishers, writers, and readers.66 Much of the Arkham House story itself 

can be found in Sixty Years of Arkham House (1999) by leading Lovecraft scholar, S.T. Joshi.   

More recently, John Tresch’s The Reasons for the Darkness of the Night: Edgar Allan Poe 

and the Forging of American Science (2021) examined—as its title suggested-- the birth of science 

in America and its influence on Poe. Overall, Tresch’s work was both a biography of Poe and a 

primer on early nineteenth century science, specifically the 1840s – the decade in which Poe 

published his both scientific and metaphysical essay Eureka: An Essay on the Material and 

Spiritual Universe.   Specifically, Tresch told “the full story of Edgar Allan Poe’s life-but it does 

so from a new angle. It returns Poe’s cosmology to its place at the summit of his life and thought, 

 64 Patrick A. Dunae. “Boys’ Literature and the Idea of Empire, 1870-1914.” Victorian Studies 24, no. 1 (1980): 105, http://www.jstor.org/stable/3826881. 65 Robert Weinberg, The Weird Tale Story, (West Lin, OR: FAX Collector’s Editions, 1977), xi. 66 Erin A. Smith, Hard-Boiled: Working Class Reader and Pulp Magazines. (Philadelphia: Temple University Press: 2000), 49. 

                                                                                                                       24 

showing his work as a singular expression of the tumultuous ideas and passions of his age, 

thoroughly bound up with the emergence of modern science.”67 Tresch also analyzed how “Poe 

explored the exhalating prospects and treacherous blind spots of Science’s powerful new ways of 

assembling the world” and that “sheds new light on the obsessions and controverses of early 

nineteenth century science as it unfolded in America. His work embodies its defining tensions: 

between popular diffusion and elite control, between inspired empathy and icy materialism.”68.  

Ultimately, Tresch concluded, Poe was not only a writer but also an artist.  “Despite the 

myths and cliches about him, Poe was not a morbid, melancholy dreamer, prone to slip at the 

slightest suggestion into an earthshaking alternate reality – or rather, he was that, and something 

more, Poe experienced great misfortune, much of which he brought upon himself. But as his 

portrait suggests, he faced up to his life with dignity, kindness, a sense of duty, and a sense of fun. 

One of the first Americans to earn his living by literature alone, he forced himself through 

unprecedented contortions to capture readers and fame. But he aimed higher. Glancing 

compulsively up to the skies, he was an analyst, a philosopher, and a detective, seeking to crack 

the code of the universe. With exceptionally keen eyes, Poe followed the dazzling trail of the 

strange and contradictory nation that produced him and the sublime, terrifying modernity it was 

bringing down to earth.”69 It was only after Poe’s death that the modernity which Tresch 

highlighted emerged in full bloom.  It was Lovecraft, then, who applied Poe to the modern world. 

 67 John Tresch, The Reason for the Darkness of The Night: Edgar Allan Poe and the Forging of American Science. (New York: Picador, 2021), 9. 68 Ibid. The influence of Poe’s scientific interests can then be seen in some of Poes works such as the poem “Al Aaraaf ” (1829) and stories such as “The Murders in the Rue Morgue” (1841) “Mesmeric Revelation” (1844) and “The Unparalleled Adventure of One Hans Pfaall.” (1835) 69 Ibid.,  12. 

                                                                                                                       25 

- H.P. Lovecraft was a key literary figure of the early twentieth century, transforming

- traditional, gothic horror literature into a “cosmic horror,” and providing the new genre literary

- heft.  While some of his contemporaries were moving toward that same goal, it was Lovecraft--

- whose writing style had more in common with the later, modernist writers of the twentieth

- century—who had the most influence on future literature.  Certainly, his art reflected both the

- negative and positive qualities of his time. He focused on new advancements in science while

- showing how science also seemed to make human beings more isolated and unsure of their place

- in the universe.  Yet, Lovecraft was a traditional moralist in most aspects of his life. In personality

- he had little in common with the “lost generation” of the 1920s, but in literary trends shared the

- same disaffection with the modern world.  Ultimately, his life and career provide a convenient lens

- to consider the past, present, and future of American literature.  Indeed, Lovecraft remains an

- important yet unheralded historical subject in the emergence of the modern world and presents the

- horror genre as an important part of not just literature but popular American culture.

This dissertation is not a biography of Lovecraft, but primarily a cultural and intellectual 

history of horror writing—and specifically the development of Lovecraft’s distinct and new brand 

of horror.  Critical to this new horror was Lovecraft’s interactions with Gothic literature, Edgar 

Allan Poe, “the pulps,” and the late nineteenth century European writers he regarded as “the 

modern masters.” As such, Chapters One and Two examine the early literary influences on 

Lovecraft, including Horace Walpole and Edgar Allan Poe. Chapters Three and Four, meanwhile, 

explore the rise of the pulp magazines, as well as the general state of horror fiction, in the late 

nineteenth and early twentieth centuries.  Chapters Five, Six, and Seven detail Lovecraft’s writing 

career and works against the backdrop of world war, economic collapse, and modern 

disillusionment. In The Modern Temper: American Society and Culture in the 1920s (1995), 

                                                                                                                       26 

historian Lynn Dumenil observed that the transformations of the 1920s—including “the alienated, 

lost generation of intellectuals,” “the new woman,” and  hedonism—did not stem from the First 

World War (1914-1919), but actually predated the war.70 Lovecraft was both influenced and 

abhorred by many of these cultural changes.  Indeed, an understanding of the cultural changes 

during this period is pivotal to an understanding of the worldview of Lovecraft. Finally, the 

Conclusion considers Lovecraft’s literary legacy.   

 70 Lynn Dumenil, The Modern Temper: American Culture and Society in the 1920s. (New York: Hill & Wang, 1995), 3. 

27 

## Chapter 1: The Development of Horror

Horror developed as a distinct genre in European literature over the course of the eighteenth 

and nineteenth centuries.  Horror, of course, existed in works prior to that time, but only as a mere 

component of a larger narrative. By the mid-eighteenth century, however, classic rationalism gave 

way to what Lovecraft later described as a “revival of romantic feeling—the era of new joy in 

Nature, and in the radiance of past time, strange scenes, bold deeds, and incredible marvels.”1  The 

eighteenth century, then, witnessed the emergence of several pioneers in horror writing, including 

Horace Walpole, Sophia Lee, Anne Radcliffe, Matthew Lewis, and the Marquis de Sade.  There 

were two key and enduring elements that these pioneers introduced to literature.  One was the use 

of horror as the main intent of (and not just a passing aspect of) a story.  The other was the 

employment of “supernatural” occurrences without natural explanation.  Then, in the early 

nineteenth century, Mary Shelley, with her monumental Frankenstein (1818), introduced a third 

element, science fiction.  In Shelley’s wake came Guy de Maupassant, perhaps the last great 

European horror writer of the nineteenth century, whose horror tales—often rooted in fantasy-- 

reflected his own descent into madness.  However, by mid-century—with works such as Wuthering 

Heights (1847), by Charlotte Bronte—gothic writing once again experienced change, becoming 

more romantic and less horrific. 

While horrific elements had been used in literature since Greco-Roman times and even in 

the works of some Judeo-Christan authors, there was no genre associated with the primary 

intention of creating fear in its readers. The only genres to contain such ideals were fairytales or 

 1 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror in Literature, 26. 

                                                                                                                       28 

folklore. The first “modern” horror writer was Horace Walpole (1717-1797) whose novel The 

Castle of Otranto (1764) introduced the basic elements of the horror genre—i.e. the supernatural 

manifesting of ghosts, the traditional gothic setting of the haunted house, and a general 

connection to the unknown.  It was Walpole, then, Lovecraft wrote in 1927, who “was the actual 

founder of the literary horror-story as a permanent form.”  While The Castle of Otranto was-- 

according to Lovecraft-- “tedious, artificial, and melodramatic… and altogether devoid of the 

true cosmic horror which makes weird literature,” it nevertheless reflected “touches of 

strangeness and spectral antiquity… that stimulated the growth of an imitative Gothic school 

which in turn inspired the real weavers of cosmic terror [such as Poe].”2 

Walpole was born in 1717, the son of British Prime Minister Sir Robert Walpole. As an 

adult Walpole spent much of his career as a member of parliament. While Walpole grew up as an 

Anglican, early on in his education he became skeptical of some superstition and religious 

doctrine while under the influence of the famed and controversial English clergyman Conyers 

Middleton.3 This may have led to his ability to look at some superstition such as ghosts in a less 

theological way than say his ancestors only a few decades before with for example such subjects 

as witchcraft.   Walpole’s early life coincided with the rise of “Gothic culture” and a “Gothic 

imagination” in both England and Europe as a whole.  The Gothic context, of course, was deep, 

dating back to the fourth century A.D., when the Germanic Goths invaded the Roman Empire 

along the Danube River in central Europe.  By the seventeenth century, the Goths were viewed— 

especially in England—as more than “mere barbarians.”  Gothicism, then, became associated 

with a “politics of freedom,” and “Gothic culture,” including architecture, became fashionable.4    

 2 Ibid.,27. 3 Robert Wyndham Ketton-Cremer, Horace Walpole: A Biography. (Ithaca: Cornell University Press, 1966), 48-49. 4 Nick Groom, “Introduction,” in Horace Walpole, The Castle of Otranto: A Gothic Story (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2014), xix. 

                                                                                                                       29 

According to one modern scholar, “Gothic thinking inevitably identified monumental medieval 

architecture with the strange and sinister traces left centuries earlier by the Ancient Britons in the 

empty wilderness of antiquity; the mysterious burial mounds and colossal megaliths that were 

thought to be tombs and temples of sacrifice of a forgotten age.”5  In literature, meanwhile, 

“Gothic” became associated with melancholy, “savage primitivism, and weird supernaturalism.”6  

Thus, by the time Walpole began writing in the early seventeenth century, the Gothic was 

“already perceived to be the portal to a realm of nightmare.”7 

Ultimately, Walpole did not write many works in the gothic horror genre that he helped to 

popularize. Most of his works consisted of historical works with titles such as Anecdotes Of 

Painting in England (1762-71) and Historic Doubts On The Life And Reign Of King Richard The 

Third (1768). However, his most famous and most influential work was The Castle of Otranto 

(1764).  Ironically enough, Walpole did not consider his work a horror tale at its basis but a 

romance—one that blended what he called “the two kinds of romance, the ancient and the 

modern.”8  According to Walpole, there was a difference between “the ancient” and “the modern” 

in that “In the former, all was imagination and improbability: in the latter, nature is always intended 

to be, and sometimes has been, copied with success.”9  For Walpole, then, “the ancient” was 

connected with the unknown—creating the atmosphere of both the gothic and the horrific.  

Meanwhile, “the modern” was connected with rationalism and the realistic. When both approaches 

were combined, they created a new type of horrific theme—one in which the modern man is 

 5 Ibid., xiii. 6 Ibid., xxi. 7 Ibid. 8 Horace Walpole, The Castle of Otranto, A Gothic Story and the Mysterious Mother, A Tragedy. (Petersborough, ON: Broadview Press, 2003), 65. 9 Ibid.,66. 

                                                                                                                       30 

bombarded with and into the unknown. It is from this worldview that the gothic style in literature 

was born. 

Overall, The Castle of Otranto—originally published anonymously-- was a story of 

familial struggle. The main character was Manfred, who as lord of his castle, Otranto, was 

attempting to avert an ancient prophecy “that the castle and lordship of Otranto should pass from 

the present family, whenever the real owner should be grown too large to inhabit it.”10 Walpole’s 

tale began on the wedding day of Manfred’s son, Conrad, to a princess named Isabella.  Before the 

marriage ceremony, however, a large helmet from a statue atop the church fell and killed Conrad.  

Fearful that Conrad’s death marked the initiation of the prophecy (and the end of his family line), 

Manfred attempted to force Isabella to marry himself in the hope of producing another male heir. 

The princess, with the help of a peasant named Theodore, fled from Manfred and his plan.  Manfred 

became obsessed with his goal, and in turn, became more vulgar and malicious.  Ultimately, a 

dynastic struggle developed over Isabella, who, meanwhile, had fallen in love with the peasant 

Theodore (who was revealed to the reader a being born of noble birth).  In the end, Manfred 

accidentally killed his own daughter (thinking she was Isabella), and thus inadvertently ended his 

family line.  A ghost then appeared and declared the prophecy fulfilled, and Theodore—now 

married to Isabella—assumed authority over Otranto.  At its most basic level, the novel’s 

discussion of prophecies and the deterioration of the soul was as old as Shakespeare. However, 

elements of horror were added as Manfred’s castle began to show supernatural manifesting, 

including as ghosts, moving pictures and closing doors.  Thus, Walpole, inadvertently created the 

stereotype of the Gothic novel—i.e. the haunted house. 

 10 Horace Walpole, The Castle of Otranto: A Gothic Story. (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2014), 17. 

                                                                                                                       31 

Much of Walpole’s inspiration for his work came of his love of architecture and history. 

Indeed, he transformed his Twickenham estate, which he purchased in 1747, to a Gothic home— 

in both its exterior and interior-- called Strawberry Hill.  Walpole and his contemporaries were 

influenced by the medieval gothic buildings which connected with the sense of horror and terror 

that many attributed to this period in history. Naturally this type of setting made sense in 

connection with the historical and horrific attributes of this new genre that kept the attributes of 

the architectural settings that much of the genre’s early work used. Walpole’s Strawberry Hill 

reflected that of the one in The Castle of Otranto and became a well-known tourist attraction.   

Walpole also—as he noted in his preface to the 1765 edition of the novel (which included 

the subtitle “A Gothic Story”)-- took inspiration from William Shakespeare, whom he called “That 

great master of Nature.”11  Indeed, allusions to Hamlet were scattered throughout Walpole’s novel, 

including the use some of Shakespeare’s dialogue used verbatim—especially as it related to the 

ghost of Hamlet’s father.12 While in Shakespeare play, the horror was used to show the effects on 

Hamlet himself, Walpole used supernatural devices to show a menacing influence that caused 

terror.13  Thus, Walpole established a key distinction between horror as a literary device and horror 

as a genre--- i.e. he did not employ horror to add elements to the story but made horror the main 

focus of his tale. With Walpole, Lovecraft observed in the 1920s, “the Gothic novel was now 

settled as a literary form.”14 

A second pioneer in horror writing was the English novelist Sophia Lee (1750-1824).  In 

her most famous novel, The Recess; Or a Tale Of Other Times (1783-85), Lee added the “historic 

 11 Ibid., xxi. 12 Robert B. Hamm. “‘Hamlet’ and Horace Walpole’s ‘The Castle of Otranto.’” Studies in English Literature, 1500-1900 49, no. 3 (2009): 674-675. http://www.jstor.org/stable/40467317. 13 Ibid., 674–675.   14 Joshi, ed. The Annotated Supernatural Horror in Literature, 28. 

                                                                                                                       32 

element,” creating, then, the Gothic historical novel and centered on the story of the fictional, 

illegitimate twin daughters of Mary Queen of Scotts.  Lee also centered her story around women, 

describing—as one modern scholar recently observed, a “nightmare cycle of violence, madness 

and death, culminating in the erasure of the maternal line.”15 Despite lacking the supernatural 

elements that characterized the horror genre, Lee’s novel nevertheless “employ[ed] the Walpole 

scenery and mechanism with great dexterity.”16 

A third pioneer was Anne Radcliffe (1764-1823), an English novelist and one of most 

popular—and even respected-- writers of the late eighteenth century.   Her great Gothic novel was 

The Mysteries of Udolpho (1794), which significantly broadened “the familiar Gothic trappings of 

her predecessors.”  Indeed, Lovecraft wrote in 1927, “Radcliffe added a genuine sense of the 

unearthly in scene and incident which closely approached genius; every touch of setting and action 

contributing artistically to the impressions of illimitable frightfulness which she wished to 

convey.”17   In all, the novel was a “Gothic romance” centered around Emily St. Albert, a young, 

French woman who came from a landed and noble family.  After her mother and father’s death, 

Emily was forced to live with her aunt, Madame Cheron, whose husband, the corrupt Italian 

nobleman Signor Montoni, schemed to control his wife’s family estate.  Ultimately, Montoni—a 

classic “Gothic villain”-- imprisoned Emily in the Castle Udolpho, where she encountered 

hauntings and terrors.  In the end, Emily escaped and was reunited with her lover, the dashing 

Valancourt. Walpole may have created Gothic literature, but it is Radcliffe who gave Gothic and 

 15 Diana Wallace. Female Gothic Histories: Gender, History, and the Gothic. (La Vergne: Gwasg Prifysgol Cyrmu/ University of Wales Press, 2013), 41. 16 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror of Literature, 10. 17 Ibid. 

                                                                                                                       33 

therefore horror literature popularity and an audience, and influenced writers such as Henry James, 

Edgar Allan Poe, and Jane Austen. 18  

 A fourth horror pioneer was Matthew Lewis (1775-1818), another English novelist, who 

created a new type of horror more violent than any of the works before him.  While only authoring 

three books, his influence over horror literature was great.  Like Walpole, Lewis was connected to 

the British government, becoming Chief Clerk of the War Office in 1772, and Deputy Secretary 

of War in 1775. Lewis’s most famous work, The Monk (1796), was inspired, as Lewis himself 

explained the novel’s Introduction, by both Radcliffe and Walpole.19  Overall, the plot centered on 

Ambrosio, a monk, and his interactions with a young girl named Matilda who stole into the 

monastery disguised as a boy.  The novel—characterized later by Lovecraft as “a masterpiece of 

active nightmare whose general Gothic cast is spiced with added stores of ghoulishness”-- was 

shocking and violent, complete with stories of  incest, rape, and sorcery.20  The significance of The 

Monk was that for the first time in a Gothic novel the supernatural was not given a realistic 

explanation at the end of the work.21  The story ended, then, with the reader uncertain as to what 

happened and why.  

Early reviews of The Monk were generally favorable.  One of the most notable reviews was 

written by English poet and literary critic Samuel Taylor Coleridge, who—while admitting its 

content far-fetched and unnatural-- found the work overall to be imaginative and its structure 

clever.22   Still, Coleridge felt that the novel lacked moral truth and that the work would cause a 

 18 Joan Passey. “Ann Radcliffe’s Influences and Legacies” in The Palgrave Handbook of Gothic Origins. Ed. C. Bloom. (Hull: Hull University, 2021), 127. 19 Christopher MacLachian. “Introduction to The Monk.” In Matthew Lewis, The Monk. (New York: Penguin Books, 1998), ix. 20 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror of Literature, 13. 21 Christopher MacLachian. “Introduction to The Monk.” In Matthew Lewis, The Monk. (New York: Penguin Books, 1998), xxiii. 22 Ibid., ix-x. 

                                                                                                                       34 

parent to turn pale if it was observed in a child’s hands.23  Others, however, including Thomas 

Matthis, were more blunt in their criticisms.  Indeed, Matthis insisted the novel’s handling of the 

Bible should be considered blasphemous and open to prosecution.24  

A fifth pioneer in European horror was the Marquis de Sade (1740-1814).  While there 

were no supernatural events in his stories, there was “horror” in terms of graphic descriptions of 

rape, murder, and torture. Indeed, according to one modern scholar, gothic works were 

characterized by “the disintegration of the body at the behest of uncontrollable and all-powerful
