forces.”25  Sometimes those forces were supernatural, but other times they were psychological or 

even physical.  Sade was born in Paris into a noble family related to the French monarch.  However, 

his sexual peculiarities—including orgies and violent sex-- enveloped him in many scandals, and 

ultimately resulted in his imprisonment in the Bastille. It was while in prison that Sade began to 

write The Hundred and Twenty Days of Sodom (1785), which chronicled the activities of four 

wealthy libertines—i.e. those indulging in heavy drinking and eating, and sexual promiscuity— 

who kidnapped men and women (both young and old), and debauched and kept them locked in 

their castle.  26 The events in the castle mirrored those in The Monk-- just more violent and sexual-

-  and unlike other gothic novels, including The Monk,  there was no escape from the castle for the 

victims and no real resolution.  One modern scholar described Sade’s work as “one of the greatest 

gothic novels, unpublished in the age of gothic ascendency.”27 Sade’s materialist philosophy was 

 23 Andre Parreaux. The Publication of The Monk: A Literary Event: 1796-1798. (Paris: Librairie Marcel Didier, 1960), 90. 24 Christopher MacLachlan “Introduction to The Monk.” In Matthew Lewis, The Monk. (New York: Penguin Books, 1998), x. 25 Clive Bloom. “From Horace Walpole to the Divine Marquis De Sade in The Palgrave Handbook of Gothic Origins. Ed. C. Bloom. (Hull: Hull University, 2021), 15. 26 The novel was not published until 1903. 27 Clive Bloom. “From Horace Walpole to the Divine Marquis De Sade in The Palgrave Handbook of Gothic Origins. Ed. C. Bloom. (Hull: Hull University, 2021), 15. 

                                                                                                                       35 

quite vulgar and shocking for the time and Sade himself—both imprisoned and institutionalized— 

was an outcast among most of his contemporaries. He may have been one of the first writers to 

publicly describe himself as an atheist.  Sade believed, then, that a world without a god meant that 

any kind of moral situation was possible.  

A sixth key figure in the development of European horror was Mary Shelley (1797-1851). 

Born in London as Mary Wollstonecraft Godwin, Shelley came from a prominent family.  Indeed, 

both her parents were well-known writers.  Her mother was a leading advocate for women’s rights, 

and in 1792, published her most famous work, A Vindication of the Rights of Woman, which 

insisted women were not inferior to men, but uneducated. Mary’s father, meanwhile, was a 

philosophical anarchist, and author of An Enquiry Concerning Political Justice (1793), as well as 

Things as They Are; or, The Adventures of Caleb Willaims (1794), a three-volume novel about the 

abuse of government power. In 1816, Mary married the famous poet Percy Shelly (1792-1822) 

who wrote such monumental works as Prometheus Unbound (1820), “The Necessity of Atheism” 

(1811) and the popular romantic poems “Ozymandias” (1818) and “To a Skylark. (1820).” Mary 

herself published her first book, a travel narrative, in 1817.  The following year she published her 

first novel, the monumental Frankenstein; or, The Modern Prometheus (1818). 

Shelley was inspired by an evening gathering in Switzerland in 1816, of her and her 

husband, and some of their neighbors, including the poet, Lord Byron.  In the 1831 edition of 

Frankenstein, Shelley wrote the Introduction, and insisted that the idea for Frankenstein stemmed 

from the fact that Lord Byron wanted everyone to write a ghost story almost as a friendly contest.28 

She began to think about the reanimation of a corpse and thought how “supremely frightful would 

 28 Mary Shelly. Frankenstein: Or The Modern Prometheus. (London: Henry Colburn and Richard Bentley, 1831), Project Guttenberg. 

                                                                                                                       36 

be the effect of any human endeavor to mock the stupendous mechanism of the world” and then 

that “the success would terrify the artist.”29 What would it be to create an artificial life?  From 

these fractured ideas the plot of Frankenstein was born.  

The plot of Frankenstein has been retold so many times since its publication that much of 

what made it unique has become part of international culture. The basic plot followed the exploits 

of the scientist Victor Frankenstein who reanimated a man made of various parts of other people 

and animals. Victor recounted his story to an Artic explorer named Captain Walton, who, at the 

start of the novel, encountered Victor chasing a gigantic man in the ice and snow. In his hubris, 

Victor created a man (the “creature” or “monster”), and then, in his horror, abandoned him.  After 

his abandonment the “creature”—hideous in form—hid himself away and observed the different 

aspects of life and humankind.  Lonely, smart (he read Milton’s Paradise Lost), and now angry, 

the creature eventually sought to find his maker, and destroy everything he loved.  Following the 

creature’s murder of Victor’s new wife, Elizabeth, Victor pursued the creature into the deep Arctic. 

It was there that Victor died and that the “monster” committed suicide, acknowledging that 

Victor’s death has done nothing to stop the loneliness of existence. Among other things, the novel 

was a commentary on ambition and guilt. Victor’s ambition (and pride) caused his destruction, 

and the revenge of the monster created nothing but guilt in its path. In the end, the lesson was that 

humanity created its own monsters through its own decisions. 

Frankenstein—arguably the first true work of the horror genre-- was much darker than its 

gothic forebears and had more in common with the works of Lewis and Sade than Radcliffe and 

Walpole.  Lovecraft himself admired the work deeply, insisting that it was “unforgettable” and 

 29 Ibid. 

                                                                                                                       37 

“one of the horror classics of all time.”30 The novel was indeed significant.  Certainly, it paved the 

way for the future of horror, and inspired generations of future writers.  Specifically, though, Shelly 

infused into gothic writing a mixture of both past and future.  While looking to the past-- and works 

such as Milton’s Paradise Lost (1667)-- she also looked toward futuristic scenarios (via the dead 

being brought back to life through scientific means). The work, then, precured the science fiction 

literary genre in its themes and inspired Lovecraft to riff off the story in one of his most playful 

works “Herbert West--Reanimator” (1922).  

Lovecraft’s tale—like Shelley’s Frankenstein-- involved a scientist, Herbert West, 

reanimating the dead.31  Originally serialized in the amateur magazine Home Brew, “Herbert 

West--Reanimator,” recounted the adventures of an unnamed narrator (a fellow scientist) and the 

“missing” title character, West, whom the narrator could “speak only with extreme terror.”  This 

terror, the narrator insisted, was grounded not only in “the sinister manner” of West’s recent 

disappearance (of which the narrator understood what happened), but also “the whole nature of 

his life-work.”  The narrator’s concerns about West originated “more than seventeen years ago, 

when we were in the third year of our course at Misatonic Medical School in Arkham. While he 

was with me, the wonder and diabolism of his experiments fascinated me utterly, and I was his 

closest companion. Now that he is gone and the spell is broken, the actual fear is greater. 

Memories and possibilities are even more hideous than realities.”32  Over the years, West—with 

the narrator’s assistance—reanimated dead corpses with a special serum concocted by West.  

With each new experiment, a corpse was reanimated with a new serum which “improved” the 

 30 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror of Literature, 35. 31 In Shelley’s novel, Frankenstein’s monster was comprised of different body parts while in Lovecraft’s story, whole corpses were reanimated.    32 Lovecraft, “Herbert West-Reanimator,” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction (New York: Barnes & Noble, 2008), 181. 

                                                                                                                       38 

mental and physical state of the reanimated corpse.  At the end of the story these reanimated 

“zombies” sieged West’s home through an ancient system of catacombs, and West was 

beheaded. The narrator witnessed the events, but did not tell authorities what happened to West, 

confiding his tale only to the story’s text.  “They imply that I am a madman or a murderer,” the 

narrator concluded.  “[P]robably I am mad. But I might not be mad if those accused tomb-legions 

had not been so silent.”33  

       Ultimately, Lovecraft did not like “Herbert West—Reanimator,” and considered the tale 

a mere monetary effort. “I am become a Grub street hack…,” Lovecraft wrote a friend in late 

1921.  Tasked with “a series of six daemoniack [sic] tales with the same hero, for… [a] proposed 

new professional magazine,” Lovecraft had written two, “From the Dark, and The Plague-

Daemone; the whole set being entitled Herbert West—Reanimator. In this enforced, laboured 

[sic], and artificial sort of composition there is nothing of art or natural gracefulness; for of 

necessity there must be a superfluity of straining and repetitions in order to make each history 

complete. My sole inducement is the monetary reward, which is a guinea per tale—a lowness of 

price shewing that the breezy St. Julian can drive as hard a bargain as Osborn or Curll, though 

with an infinitely greater deal of honour. [sic].”34  The following year, Lovecraft confided to 

writer Clark Ashton Smith, that “Herbert West--Reanimator represents my poorest work-stuff 

done to order for a vulgar magazine & written down to the herd’s letter. This is a good way to 

pick up an odd dollar now and then.”35  

 33 Ibid., 181. 34 Lovecraft to Reinhardt Kleiner, October 7, 1921, in Durleth and Wandrei, eds., Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1911-1924, 98. 35 Lovecraft to Clark Ashton Smith, November 12, 1922, in Ibid., 122. 

                                                                                                                       39 

In the end, gothic literature—with its troupes of the haunted house, romance, and the fight 

between good and evil—ultimately cast a long shadow over the emerging horror genre.  Indeed, 

Lovecraft himself was heavily influenced by it as late as the early twentieth century.  Writing to a 

friend in 1927, Lovecraft emphasized the importance of environment in his own work.  “I think an 

author strongly reflects his surroundings, & that he does best in founding his elements of incident 

& colour [sic] on a life & background to which he has a real & deep-seated relation.”36  For 

Lovecraft, of course, that environment was the old city of Providence, Rhode Island (founded in 

1636).  “I have found from experience,” Lovecraft added, “that Providence is the only place where 

I can be content… The old town dreams on seven green hills at the head of its lovely blue bay, 

crowned by great domes & venerable steeples, & preserving unchanged a vast portion of the 

narrow, winding hill streets, the peaked colonial gables & gambrel roofs… & the stately Georgian 

mansions in their archaic walled gardens…”  Ultimately, he observed, “The old families & the old 

ideals still dominate, & there are little ways & customs on every hand which attest an unbroken 

evolution from colonial times, & an absolute, vital identity with the Gothic town of Roger 

Williams…”37 

Unsurprisingly, Lovecraft possessed a romantic view of the past (and particularly the 

seventeenth and eighteenth centuries).  Place and temperament, then, helped generate gothic 

elements in Lovecraft’s own writings.  “No one loves the memory of old moss-covered abbeys 

and the sweet chimes of Gothic belfries at evening more than I,” he wrote in March 1927.38   In 

1928, Lovecraft wrote detective novelist and pulp horror and fantasy writer Vincent Starrett, 

commending him on a poem from his 1922 collection, Ebony Flame.  “It has the music of true 

 36 Lovecraft to Donald Wandrei, February 10, 1927, in August Derleth and Donald Wandrei, eds., H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1925-1929 (Sauk City, Wisconsin: Arkham House, 1968), 100. 37 Ibid.,102.  38 Lovecraft to Bernard Austin Dwyer, March 26, 1927, in Ibid., 119. 

                                                                                                                       40 

poetry, & at the same time the stealthy atmospheric convincingness of first-rate Gothic horror; a 

combination not often found in such felicitous completeness.”39   Still, Lovecraft was often critical 

of gothic writers, including Walpole.  “Have you read The Castle of Otranto itself?” Lovecraft 

asked a friend in early 1928.  “If not, don’t!”  The novel failed to offer a sufficient “kick,” he 

insisted.  “Walpole was too steeped in the classical tradition of the early 18th century to catch the 

Gothic spirit of the latter half.  His choice of words and rhythms is the brisk, cheerful… one; and 

his nonchalant and atmosphericless [sic] way of describing the most prodigious horrors is enough 

to empty them of their potency.”40  

Lovecraft elaborated on gothic “horror” in his 1927 essay “The Supernatural Horror in 

Literature,” and clearly admired “The shadow-haunted landscapes… the grotesque witch dances… 

the sinister daemonism [sic]… [and] ghostly charm” of gothic works.41  While gothic tales were 

“flat, stilted, and altogether devoid of true cosmic horror,” they reflected the era’s interest in 

“strangeness and spectral antiquity,” and raised—“in spite of its intrinsic ineptness”-- this early 

form of horror “to a pedestal of lofty importance in literary history.”   Above all, he noted, it 

created “a novel type of scene, puppet characters, and incidents; which handled to better advantage 

by writers more naturally adapted to weird creation, stimulated the growth of… [a later] innovative 

Gothic school which in turn inspired the real weavers of cosmic terror—the line of actual artists 

 39 Lovecraft to Vincent Starrett, January 10, 1928, in Ibid., 221-222.  Starrett (1886-1974) was a Canadian author known for his novel, The Private Life of Sherlock Holmes (1933).  He also wrote for pulp magazines, including Weird Tales.  A collection of his pulp short stories was published in 1927 as The Moon Terror.  In 1928—reflecting his similar interests to Lovecraft—Starrett published Seaports in the Moon: A Fantasia on Romantic Themes.   40 Lovecraft to Bernard Austin Dwyer, February 14, 1928, in Ibid., 231. 41 Lovecraft specifically mentioned Robert Burns’s poem, “Tam O’Shanter,” Samuel Taylor Coleridge’s “Christabel” and Ancient Mariner, and James Hoag’s Kilmeny. S.T. Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror in Literature (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2000), 26.    

                                                                                                                       41 

beginning with Poe.”42   It was in the new British colonies in America (and late the United States), 

then, that gothic literature was transformed. 

In addition to early writers like Walpole, and later ones, like Shelley, Lovecraft highlighted  

Clara Reeves’s The Old English Baron (1777), Sophia Lee’s The Recess (1785), and Anne 

Radcliffe The Mysteries of Udolpho (1794), which he insisted was “the early gothic tale at its 

best.”43  Lovecraft was especially fond of Radcliffe, arguing that her “famous novels made terror 

and suspense a fashion, and who set new and higher standards in the domain of macabre.”44  

Lovecraft also examined two authors specifically as the “the apex of gothic romance”: Matthew 

Lewis (1775-1818) and Charles Robert Mauturin (1780-1824).45  According to Lovecraft, the 

English Lewis – with his “gothic romance,” The Monk (1796)-- “turned to terror more violent than 

his gentle predecessor[s] had ever dared to think of; and produced as a result a masterpiece of 

active nightmare whose general gothic cast is spiced with added stores of ghoulishness.”46  

Published in three volumes, The Monk included explicit sexual content—including rape and 

incest—witchcraft, satanic bargaining, and eternal damnation.   Similarly, Mauturin’s Melmoth the 

Wanderer (1820) provided “daemonic fright” via the title character, Melmoth, who sold his soul 

for immortality, and then wandered tortuously through the centuries.47  Lovecraft called Mauturin, 

an Irish clergyman, “the greatest as well as the last of the Goth’s.”48  

A hallmark of Lovecraft’s own horror was his fusion of various trends in “horror” writing 

across the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries.  Gothic literature, then, was an important element 

 42 Ibid., 27. 43 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror In Literature,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1049. 44 Ibid. 45 Ibid., 1051. 46 Ibid., 1052. 47 Ibid., 1049. 48 Ibid., 1055. 

                                                                                                                       42 

in that fusion. While Lovecraft’s stories rarely included “romance,” gothic elements such as 

setting, substance, and style were often employed.  Indeed, Lovecraft was enamored with 

architecture and believed setting and environment—alongside an authors own “psychological 

tastes & requirements”--  to be key components of horror writing.49  “I think an author strongly 

reflects his surroundings,” Lovecraft wrote in 1927, “& that he does best in founding his elements 

of incident & color on a life & background to which he has a real & deep-seated relation.”50  For 

Lovecraft, then, that often meant New England generally and Rhode Island specifically.  “All 

genuine art,” he later wrote, “… is local and rooted in the soil; for even when one sings of 

incredible twilight lands he is merely singing of his homeland in some gorgeous and exotic 

mantle.”51  While setting (or environment) was most definitely connected to mood, Lovecraft 

often—as with “The Shunned House”  and “The Picture in the House”—was inspired by specific 

and familiar structures (the gothic “haunted house”), and included them into his stories.  On one 

occasion, Lovecraft complained to a friend about the tearing down of a particular district of 

interest.  “I imagine the building inspectors must have found those ancient houses as sinister as I 

did, albeit with a different sort of perception.  That is the perennial grief of an architectural 

antiquarian-- … something quant is always being demolished in the interest of alleged progress.”52   

In addition, Lovecraft employed gothic substance (i.e. imagery and metaphor)—but in 

reverse.  Gothic tales were rooted in a Christian worldview and often involved moral responsibility 

within the context of that worldview.  Whether with the monk Ambrosio’s fornication and satanic 

bargaining in The Monk, or Victor Frankenstein’s creation of his own Man/Monster in 

 49 Lovecraft to Donald Wandrei, February 10, 1927, in Derleth and Wandrei, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1925-1929, 100 50 Ibid. 51 Lovecraft to Bernard Austin Dwyer, June 1927, in Ibid., 131. 52 Lovecraft to Zealia Reed, September 22, 1927, in Ibid., 170. 

                                                                                                                       43 

Frankenstein, Christian themes and imagery were prominent.  Lovecraft, of course, was not a 

Christian, and thus there was no ethical dimension to his stories.  The substance of the horror for 

him was not Man’s inability to attain (or maintain) a lofty, Christian ideal, but Man’s insignificance 

and lack of purpose.  As to style, Lovecraft mimicked the language and writing techniques of 

eighteenth-century European writers.  Ultimately, Lovecraft employed key Gothic tools-- language 

and setting—and then heightened them.  Unlike traditional Gothic tales, then, his sole purpose was 

to cause fear.  There was no romance, no deep understanding of the protagonist, and no morality.  

Just fear, and the story itself.  “I believe that art should not teach, but simply picture.” Lovecraft 

wrote in 1921.  “It should deal with images more than ideas; should thrill rather than convince.”53 

  After the publication of Frankenstein, European gothic literature continued to give birth 

to some classics of the genre though it was a century before a horror classic with the influence or 

reputation of Frankenstein. The next great horror literature came from the United States where a 

young Southerner began making the horror genre an art form. It was at this point that the gothic 

horror genre began to transform. While there were still works of horror being written, they tended 

to be more romantic in nature.54 Indeed, these new gothic romances of the mid-nineteenth 

century—including Emily Bronte’s classic Wuthering Heights (1847)--  were more focused on the 

romantic situations that took place in the work and less on causing fear. 

 53 Lovecraft to Frank Belknap Long, June 4, 1921, in Derleth and Wandrei, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1911-1924, 136. 54 These works included Charles Robert Maturin’s work Melmoth the Wanderer (1820) and Willim Beckford’s Vathek (1816). Both works included religious imagery and are considered classics of the genre. These works are amongst the last embers of this period of the traditional gothic horror that existed before Shelly’s work.  

                                                                                                                       44 

## Chapter 2:  The American Horror Story

The horror genre emerged as a popular force in American literature in the nineteenth 

century.  In the first half of the century, most American novels reflected the attitudes of the 

Romantic Movement in literature, which emphasized independence and rural living.  The works 

of James Fenimore Cooper, including The Last of the Mohicans (1826) and The Deerslayer (1841), 

perhaps best represented that trend. Little about these works were “horrific.”  Indeed, many 

American novelists, such as Cooper, aimed to establish a unique American fiction-- distinct from 

that of Europe.  Still, elements of European gothic works made their way into American writing— 

even if inadvertently.  Indeed, seventeenth century American literature was rich with non-fiction 

works such as Day of Doom (1662), The Wonders of the Invisible World (1693), Memorable 

Provinces Relating to Witchcraft and Possessions (1689), and The Kingdom of Darkness (1688)— 

all of which, modern novelist Joyce Carol Oates observed, “might be the titles of lurid works of 

gothic fiction.”1   In his masterful World of Wonder, Days of Judgement: Popular Religious Belief 

in Early New England (1989), historian David D. Hall noted that “The people of seventeenth-

century New England lived in an enchanted universe.  Theirs was a world of wonders.  Ghosts 

came to people in the night, and trumpets blared, though no one saw the trumpeters…”2  

Unsurprisingly, early English settlers in North America—living in small, candlelit homes on the 

edge of a vast and mysterious wilderness (complete with “the hordes of coppery Indians whose 

 1 Joyce Carol Oates, “The King of Weird,” The New York Review (October 31, 1996). https://www.nybooks.com/articles/1996/10/31/the-king-of-weird/ 2 David D. Hall, World of Wonder, Days of Judgement: Popular Religious Belief in Early New England. (Cambridge, Massachusetts: Harvard University Press, 1989), 71. 

                                                                                                                       45 

strange, saturnine visages and violent customs hinted strongly at traces of infernal origin”)—were 

attracted to and influenced by tales of ghosts, witches, and devils.3 

By the end of the eighteenth century, the ideas of the Enlightenment were well-entrenched 

in North America, and the United States had emerged as an independent nation—characterized by 

national development (including steamboats and canals, bustling urban communities, and 

emerging manufacturing centers), and greater literary sophistication.  Despite a new era of liberty 

and freedom, however, mortality still defined the human condition, and fear—fear of the unknown 

and fear of death—remained a common human emotion. Indeed, mortality rates in the nineteenth 

century were still high—about twenty-five per thousand annually.4   Mortality rates were higher in 

urban areas, and among women of childbearing years.  As late as 1900, “for every thousand live 

births, six to nine women died from pregnancy-related complications, and in some U.S. cities… a 

third of infants died before their first birthday.”5  The lack of modern medicines to combat diseases 

such as measles, scarlet fever, and “consumption” made death—even death of the young— a 

commonplace occurrence.   In Charles Dickens’s classic English novel, Oliver Twist (1838), young 

Oliver-- concerned about his dangerously ill friend, the beautiful maiden, Rose-- reflected “that 

graves were for cold and cheerless winter; not for sunlight and fragrance.”  Just then, though, a 

church-bell rang, and “A group of humble mourners entered the [cemetery] gate, wearing white 

 3 Lovecraft “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction (New York: Barnes & Noble, 2008), 1070. 4 Michael R. Haines, “The Urban Mortality Transition in the United States, 1800-1840,” Historical Working Paper (July 2001), National Bureau of Economic Research. https://www.nber.org/papers/h0134#:~:text=A%20national%20crude%20death%20rate,urban%20share%20of%20th e%20population. 5 Centers for Disease Control, “Achievements in Public Health,1900-1999: Healthier Mothers and Babies,” Morbidity and Mortality Weekly Report, October 1, 1999, quoted in Edward F. O’Keefe, The Loves of Theodore Roosevelt: The Women Who Created a President (New York: Simon & Schuster, 2024), 144. 

                                                                                                                       46 

favors, for the corpse was young.  They stood uncovered by a grave… But the sun shone brightly, 

and the birds sang on.”6   

It was at the end of the eighteenth century, then, that the United States began its foray into 

serious gothic literature. Indeed, with his 1798 novel Wieland, or the Transformation, Charles 

Broden Brown (1771-1810) initiated “the Gothic preoccupation with the psychological and 

historical meaning of America.”7 Brown was a lawyer and magazine editor from Philadelphia, and 

the first native-born American to make writing his profession and livelihood.8  Overall, Wieland 

was a dark tale of religious fanaticism and psychological manipulation that offered “an 

extraordinary narrative dramatization of one of the most crucial issues of the early national period: 

the conflicting claims of authority and liberty.”9  Set in rural Pennsylvania in the period between 

the French and Indian War (1754-1763) and the American Revolution (1775-1783), the story was 

narrated (via the form of a letter) by Clara Wieland, the sister of the main character, Theodore.  

The two had recently lost their fanatical father—who had founded his own religion—in a 

mysterious explosion. Like his father, Theodore was a religious zealot. He had married his 

childhood friend, Catherine Pleyel, and the two had four children. Clara lived next door to her 

brother and his family, and was hopeful of marrying Catherine’s brother, Henry. All seemingly 

lived in happiness until the arrival one day of a mysterious stranger named Carwin.  Soon the group 

was besieged by supernatural occurrences, including the hearing of strange voices. Theodore— 

increasingly fanatical-- claimed that these voices told him to commit murder, and ultimately, he 

killed his wife and children. Clara was convinced that Carwin—who had sabotaged her 

relationship with Henry—was responsible for the voices.  After Theodore was imprisoned for 

 6 Charles Dickens, The Adventures of Oliver Twist (Boston: Mershon Company Press, Undated), 249. 7 Jay Fliegelman, ed., Wieland and Memoirs of Carwin Biloquist. (New York: Penguin Books, 1991), vii. 8 Ibid. 9Ibid. 

                                                                                                                       47 

murder, Clara confirmed that Carwin was a biloquist, and that he had indeed been responsible for 

most of the strange voices.  Carwin insisted, however, that he did not tell Theodore to kill anyone.  

After he learned the truth about the voices, Theodore committed suicide.  Clara, meanwhile, moved 

to Europe, married Henry, and then wrote the story of her brother’s tragedy.  

Most early American “horror” stories were really more “weird” in substance than 

frightening. This was especially true with the tales of Washington Irving (1783-1859).10 Often 

regarded as the “Father of American Literature” and “architect of America’s first mythology,” 

Irving had a tremendous influence on later American, gothic writers such as Hawthorne and Poe.11  

Many of the eighteenth-century horror works in Europe used relics of the past as the setting for 

their plots. Irving did the same thing but with a focus on American history and folklore, particularly 

with the Dutch residents of New York—the state’s last connection to its first European inhabitants.  

His first major work—written under the pseudonym Diedrich Knickerbocker-- was the satirical A 

History of New York: From the Beginning of the World to the End of the Dutch Dynasty (1809). 

Shortly thereafter, he became the editor of the Analectic Magazine in Philadelphia, which included 

“Selections from Foreign Reviews and Magazines Together with Original Miscellaneous 

Compositions.”12  Then, in 1815, Irving traveled to Europe in an attempt to salvage his family’s 

trading company.  Ultimately, he resided there for nearly seventeen years. However, he was able 

to continue writing and it is in Europe that he published much of his major works.  

The first and most important work connected to Irving published while in Europe was The 

Sketch Book of Geoffrey Crayon Gent. (1819). Most of the works in the collection were not weird 

 10 Before Irving, horror could be found in supposed non-fiction accounts in works like Cotton Mathers The Wonders of the Invisible World (1693) which discussed witchcraft.  11 Elizabeth L. Bradley. “Introduction to The Legend of Sleepy Hollow and Other Stories.” In Washinton Irving, The Legend of Sleepy Hollow and Other Stories. (New York: Penguin Books, 2014), ix. 12 Front Cover, The Analectic Magazine, https://archive.org/details/analecticmagazin06irvi/page/n7/mode/2up 

                                                                                                                       48 

or horrific in nature. Indeed, the collection included some Christmas stories which led to 

popularization of the Christmas holiday in the United States, and even influenced the Christmas 

tales of Charles Dickens later that century. 13 The two most well-known stories from the collection, 

however, happened to be the “weirdest”: “Rip Van Winkle” and “The Legend of Sleepy Hollow.”   

“Rip Van Winkle” was an early American “weird” story.  It described a Dutch American 

villager named Rip Van Winkle who lived in the Catskill Mountains of New York colony before 

the American Revolution. After meeting a group of men in the woods carrying a keg, Van Winkle, 

who took a drink from the keg, fell fast asleep. He awoke later to a changed world—one where he 

was an old man with a long beard.  Upon returning to his village, he found it too had changed.  

Indeed, he soon learned that twenty-one years had passed since he was last in the village.  In that 

time, his wife had died, his children had grown into adulthood, and the Revolutionary War had 

taken place.  Seeking an answer for his long sleep, Van Winkle was told by a village elder that the 

“men” he had met in the woods were actually ghosts of the crew from a Dutch East India   

Company ship, the Halve Maen.14  While the story contained elements of the supernatural, it was 

more reminiscent of a fairy tale.  In the end, then, the most “horrifying” part of the story was Van 

Winkle waking up to another time and world that was unrecognizable.15  Not unlike the European 

gothic stories from a century earlier, Irving’s tale was one of lostness—in this case, time and space. 

It was with his “The Legend of Sleepy Hollow,” however, that Irving forever secured his 

place in American literature.   Indeed, it was one of the nation’s first ghost stories, introducing 

 13 Penne L. Restad. Christmas In America: A History. (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 1996), 137.  14 Washington Irving. “Rip Van Winkle” The Legend of Sleepy Hollow and Other Stories. (New York: Penguin Books, 2014), 46. 15 Jeffrey Rubin-Dorsky. “The Value of Storytelling: ‘Rip Van Winkle’ and ‘The Legend of Sleepy Hollow’ in the context of ‘The Sketch Book.’” Modern Philology 82, no.4, 1985, 399.  

                                                                                                                       49 

readers to the “mysterious and appalling” Headless Horseman.16  Overall, it was a tale of Ichabod 

Crane, a mild-mannered schoolteacher in Sleepy Hollow, a small community near Tarrytown, New 

York.  The lanky, nervous, and superstitious—if not comical-- Crane hoped to marry Katrina Van 

Tassel, the daughter of a wealthy farmer. His rival for Katrina’s hand in marriage was the brutish 

Abraham (“Brom Bones”) Van Brunt.  During a harvest party on a “fine autumn day,” Van Brunt 

told the story about a “Headless Horseman”-- a Hessian soldier decapitated during the 

Revolutionary War, who returned from the dead on horseback each night in search of his head. 

Van Brunt boasted that he had seen the Horseman and had raced him to a bridge just outside the 

village.  According to Van Brunt, the Horseman could not cross the bridge.  Later that evening, 

Crane proposed to Katrina and was rejected. He then began his trek home-- his mind enraptured 

by Van Brunt’s ghost stories—when he encountered the Headless Horseman.  Crane raced toward 

the bridge at Sleepy Hollow, where the Horseman threw his severed head at Crane, knocking him 

from his horse. The next day there was no trace of Crane—except his hat (and a nearby shattered 

pumpkin)-- and rumors abounded on whether he had left the town in humiliation after the rejection 

of the marriage proposal or whether the Headless Horseman really did exist, and that Crane was 

“spirted away by supernatural means.”17 The story, then, ended ambiguously, and with a heavy 

suggestion from Irving that Van Brunt was the “Horseman” and had run off his rival.   

Strangely enough, Lovecraft, in his 1927 essay on horror in literature, mentioned neither 

“Rip Van Winkle” nor “The Legend of Sleepy Hollow.” Instead, he focused on a lesser-known 

Irving story, “The Adventure of the German Student,” published in Irving’s 1824 collection of 

stories, Tales of a Traveler.   The story was dark, and ambiguous as to what really happened— 

 16 Elizabeth Bradley. “Introduction to The Legend of Sleepy Hollow and Other Stories.” In Washinton Irving, The Legend of Sleepy Hollow and Other Stories. (New York: Penguin Books, 2014), ix. 17 Washington Irving “The Legend of Sleepy Hollow.” The Legend of Sleepy Hollow and Other Stories New York: Penguin Books, 2014, 339. 

                                                                                                                       50 

especially as it related to the exact nature of the sexual encounter involved.  Indeed, the story, as 

Lovecraft later observed, diverged greatly from Irving’s usual “lighter treatment of eerie themes.”18   

Set in Paris during the French Revolution, “The Adventure of the German Student” recounted the 

story of young German man, Gottfried Wolfgang, who spent the night with a random woman.  

Later, the next day, he returned to his apartment and found the woman—still in his bed—dead.   

Local authorities soon arrived and informed Wolfgang that the woman in his bed had actually been 

executed on the guillotine a day earlier.  The story ended with the authorities removing the body 

from the bed, and the head falling to the floor. 

Another early American contributor to horror was Nathaniel Hawthorne (1804-1864).  

Born in Salem, Massachusetts into a prominent family with long ties to the community—dating, 

in fact, back to the late seventeenth century and the Salem Witchcraft Trials-- Hawthorne sought 

to disassociate himself from his Puritan ancestors, and even added a “w” to the spelling of his last 

name. 19  Indeed, Hawthorne’s great-great grandfather, John Hathorne, was an early judge in the 

1692 trials.20  Ultimately, Lovecraft wrote in the 1920s, Hawthorne was “a gentle soul cramped by 

the Puritanism of early New England; shadowed and wistful, and grieved at an unmoral universe 

which everywhere transcends the conventional patterns thought by our forefathers to represent 

divine and immutable law.”21  For him, Lovecraft added, evil was “a lurking and conquering 

adversary; and the visible world [became] in his fancy a theatre of infinite tragedy and woe.”22 

Hawthorne was a prolific writer who enjoyed an illustrious career with connections to several 

 18 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror of Literature, 18. 19 Edwin Haviland Miller. Salem is My Dwelling Place: A Life of Nathaniel Hawthorne. (Iowa City: University of Iowa Press, 1991), 20-21. 20 Hathorne, however, was not a judge on the Court of Oyer and Terminer, which was the body in Salem responsible for the conviction and execution of nineteen people ruled to be witches.  21 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror of Literature, 47. 22 Ibid. 

                                                                                                                       51 

contemporary literary figures, including Ralph Waldo Emerson, Herman Melville, and Henry 

Wadsworth Longfellow.  In the early 1820s, he attended Bowdoin College in Maine, where he met 

and became close friends with Franklin Pierce of New Hampshire.  Indeed, Hawthorne even wrote 

a campaign biography for the Democrat’s successful 1852 campaign for president, The Life of 

Franklin Pierce.23   Hawthorne published his first novel (Fanshawe) in 1828 at the age of twenty-

four.  Although it was generally well-received by critics, it was not a commercial success, and 

Hawthorne then turned to short stories.  

In 1837, Hawthorne—while working in the Customs House in Boston-- published his first 

collection of short stories, Twice-Told Tales.  In a review for Graham’s Magazine in 1842, Edgar 

Allan Poe insisted that the collection “belong[s] to the highest region of Art…  As Americans, we 

feel proud of the book.”24  While not a collection of horror stories, many of the tales were certainly 

gothic in nature—unsettling and macabre. For example, in “The Minister’s Black Veil”—a story 

that Poe called a “masterly composition”—a minister, the Reverend Hooper, began wearing a black 

veil over his face following the death of a young maiden in town.  Before her funeral, Hooper— 

adorned in his veil-- preached a sermon on secret sin, leading some townspeople to speculate on 

his relationship with the maiden. His strange behavior resulted in his fiancé breaking their 

engagement.  Still, Hooper wore the veil and refused to remove it throughout the rest of his life.  

Years later, on his deathbed—and as another minister, Reverend Clark, attempted to remove the 

veil—Hooper clinched tightly to it, crying “Never!... On earth, never!”  The startled Reverend 

Clark, then asked Hooper, “Dark old man… with what horrible crime upon your soul are you now 

 23 Hawthorne and Pierce remained close friends.  In 1853, President Pierce appointed him as U.S. consul in Liverpool.  In 1863, despite intense public disfavor towards the former president, Hawthorne dedicated his last book-- a series of sketches of life in England entitled Our Old Home—to Pierce.  The two friends were traveling together in New Hampshire when Hawthorne died suddenly in May 1864. 24 Edgar Allan Poe, “Twice Told Tales: A Review,” Graham’s Magazine (May 1842). https://www.ibiblio.org/eldritch/nh/nhpoe1.html 

                                                                                                                       52 

passing to the judgement?”  Hooper responded by reflecting on what “made this piece of crape so 

awful,” and why his attending minsters “tremble at me alone?... Tremble also at each other… I 

look around me, and, lo! on every visage a black veil!”  Hooper then died, and “Still veiled, they 

laid him in his coffin, and a veiled corpse they bore him to the grave.”  Hawthorne then concluded 

the story with an astonishingly macabre line: “The grass of many years has sprung up and withered 

on that grave, and the burial-stone is moss-grown, and good Mr. Hooper’s face is dust; but awful 

is still the thought that is mouldered [sic] beneath the black veil.”25 

In another story, “The Haunted Mind,” Hawthorne told the tale of an unnamed character 

lying alone in his bed.  It was, Poe insisted in his 1842 review of the collection, a “beautiful” piece 

with no “attempt at effect.  All is quiet, thoughtful, subdued.”26  Indeed, the character seemed to 

drift between dreaming and wakefulness.  In this drift, he contemplated time space, and grappled 

with anxiety, including thoughts of death.   Ultimately, Hawthrone demonstrated his ability to 

depict “horror” in the space between consciousness and unconsciousness, predating Freud in his 

awareness of the symbology of the unconscious mind.  

Then, in 1846, Hawthorne followed Twice-Told Tales with a new collection entitled Mosses 

from an Old Manse.  Hawthorne married the artist Sophia Peabody of Salem in 1842, and shortly 

thereafter moved to Concord, where the couple lived at the Old Manse—the former home of the 

Reverend William Emerson-- just a few yards from Concord’s North Bridge, the site of the first 

battle of the American Revolution.27  It was there in Concord, while residing at the Old Manse, 

 25 Nathaniel Hawthorne, Twice-Told Tales. (Philadelphia: David McKay, Publisher, 1919), 47-48. 26 Edgar Allan Poe, “Twice Told Tales: A Review,” Graham’s Magazine (May 1842). https://www.ibiblio.org/eldritch/nh/nhpoe1.html 27 The Reverend Emerson was the father of Ralph Waldo Emerson, and witnessed from his home the fight at North Bridge in 1775. Concord was a rich literary and transcendentalist community.  Indeed, Hawthorne was a neighbor of Ralph Waldo Emerson, Henry David Thoreau, and the family of Maria Louise Alcott.  In fact, Hawthorne purchased the Alcott’s home, Wayside, in 1852. 

                                                                                                                       53 

that Hawthorne wrote the stories for Mosses from an Old Manse.  As with Twice-Told Tales, many 

of the stories in Mosses were dark, bleak, and “weird.”  One of the stories, for example, “Young 

Goodman Brown,” was set during the Salem Witchcraft Trials, and involved the protagonist, 

Goodman Brown, travelling through a dark forest at night.  There he encountered an elderly man 

holding a serpent-shaped staff and leading a group of townspeople to an altar in the woods. In 

another story, “The Birthmark,” a scientist, Alymer, became obsessed with removing a small 

birthmark from his beautiful wife, Georgiana’s face.  After several experiments, the birthmark 

indeed faded, but then Georgiana died.  In his positive review of the collection in 1850, Herman 

Melville insisted that a “black conceit pervades him through and through. You may be witched by 

this sunlight- transported by the bright gliding’s in the skies he builds over you; but there is the 

blackness of darkness beyond; and even his bright gliding’s but fringe and play upon the edges of 

thunder-clouds.”28   

Not long after the publication of Mosses, Hawthorne relocated to Salem, where he worked 

as Inspector of the Revenue for the Port of Salem, as well as Surveyor for the District of Salem 

and Beverly.  It was while in Salem in those roles that Hawthorne wrote The Scarlet Letter 

(1850)—perhaps his most famous work. Overall, the novel—reflecting the unique American 

gothic qualities of vast landscape and Puritan-controlled community—was, author Henry James 

later observed, “densely dark” and contained “little enough of gaiety or of hopefulness.”29  

Ultimately, it focused on the effects of psychological horror, anticipating what twentieth century 

author Joseph Conrad later observed in his Under Western Eyes (1911)—i.e. that “the belief in a 

supernatural source of evil is not necessary; men alone are quite capable of every wickedness.” 30  

 28 Fredrick Crew. The Sins of the Fathers: Hawthorne’s Psychological Themes. (Berkeley: University of California Press, 1989), 8.  29 Henry James, Hawthorne. (New York: Harper & Brothers, 1901), 106. 30 Thomas Ligotti. The Conspiracy Against The Human Race. (New York: Penguin Books, 2018), 199.  

                                                                                                                       54 

Writing to a friend in early 1850, Hawthorne confessed that the conclusion of the book, broke his 

wife’s heart, “and sent her to bed with a grievous headache—which I look upon as a triumphant 

success.”31 

Set in Massachusetts Bay Colony in the mid seventeenth century, The Scarlet Letter dealt 

with the themes of guilt, sin, and legalism.  Specifically, it told the story of the young Hester 

Prynne, who—abandoned by her much older and villainous husband-- had a child outside of 

marriage with the rising young Puritan minister in the community, Arthur Dimmesdale.  Hester’s 

relationship with Dimmesdale was a secret one, and Hester was punished by the Puritan 

community for her sexual transgression that led to the birth of her daughter, Pearl.   Hester was 

forced to stand on a scaffold for three hours in public humiliation and wear a scarlet “A” (for 

adultery) around her neck for the rest of her life.  Meanwhile, Dimmesdale was consumed with 

guilt for not acknowledging the truth about Peral’s paternity, and for his own inability or 

unwillingness to confess his affair with (and love for) Hester—all the while Hester silently suffered 

and was publicly scorned.  Later, Hester’s husband, Roger Chillingworth, arrived in the community 

after being presumed dead by Hester.  Unknown in the community, and posing as Dimmesdale’s 

doctor, the fiendish Chillingworth—having discovered Hester’s affair with Dimmesdale—slowly 

poisoned the already ill Puritan minister.  At the novel’s end, Dimmesdale, near death, climbed the 

public scaffold and confessed his relationship with Hester, and then died in her arms. 

The following year, Hawthorne--now living in Lennox, Massachusetts-- published the 

foremost among his “weird material,” The House of Seven Gables (1851).32  Like its predecessor, 

 31 Nathaniel Hawthorne to Horatio Bridge, February 1850, quoted in Henry James, Hawthorne. (New York: Harper & Brothers, 1901), 106. 32 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror of Literature, 48.  Hawthorne was inspired by a real house in Salem (built in 1668) that belonged to relatives and had seven gables.  Located near Salem Harbor, it is today a museum. 

                                                                                                                       55 

The House of Seven Gables considered the historical legacy of Puritanism—and in this case, the 

Salem Witch Trials.  Set in contemporary times (i.e. the 1850s) in Salem, the “House of Seven 

Gables” was built on prime land that had been stolen from the original owner by a corrupt 

magistrate in the 1690s.   Indeed, the original owner of the property, Matthew Maule, was accused 

of sorcery and executed in the witchcraft trials. His fate had rested with the corrupt Colonel 

Pyncheon, who had long coveted the property, and now seized it, and built his grand home, the 

“House of Seven Gables.”  Shortly after the house was completed, though, the Colonel was found 

dead in his study-and a deed that contains vast amounts of land in Maine is discovered to be 

missing..  The estate and the family were, then, deemed cursed.  As time passed, the status of the 

family dimmed—the deteriorating house all that remained of their prior glories.  

In “present-day,” an elderly woman named Hepzibah—one of the last members of the 

Colonel’s direct line-- still resided in the house, renting rooms and operating a small “cent shop” 

in one corner.   At the start of this contemporary section of the story, Hepzibah had one boarder, 

Holgrave, a young daguerreotypist (and a secret descendant of Matthew Maule).  She was also 

soon joined by her distant cousin Phoebe, a young beautiful woman of “feminine grace,” who 

quickly charmed those she met—and especially (and predictably) Holgrave-- with her cheerfulness 

and kindness.33  Meanwhile, the return of Hepzibah’s brother Clifford, who had been framed for 

murder thirty years earlier by their cousin Judge Jaffery Pyncheon, further transformed the house 

for the good.  Soon, however, Judge Pyncheon visited the home, instilling fear, anger, and anxiety 

in the household. “Why do you persecute us any longer?” Hepzibah asked. “Leave poor Cliford 

and me in peace. Neither of us desires anything better!”34 The Judge replied that he believed 

 33 Nathaniel Hawthrone. The House of Seven Gables. (New York: Simon & Schuster, 2004.), 94. 34 Ibid., 265. 

                                                                                                                       56 

Clifford to be aware of the fraudulent documents that deeded the house to the family.  “Clifford as 

he chooses and choose, he must-- can inform me where to find the schedule, the documents, the 

evidence, in whatever shape they exist in the vast amount of Uncle Jaffery’s missing property.”  If 

he refused, the Judge insisted, Clifford would be returned to prison, “his confinement, probably 

for the remainder of his life.”35 In the end, however, the Judge died, Clifford acquired his estate, 

and Holgrave and Phoebe proclaimed their love for one another.  All, including Hepzibah, were 

finally able to leave the old House of Seven Gables for new horizons and a bright future.  

The novel was originally subtitled “A Romance,” which reflected its happy ending. 

However, it did contain “horror” in a way that Hawthorne’s previous works did not—i.e. with 

curses, witchcraft, and unexplained deaths. The work was well received when published with 

authors like Longfellow calling the work a “weird wild book, like all he writes.”36 Later,  Lovecraft 

called The House of the Seven Gables “New England’s greatest contribution to weird literature-

and we can feel in an instant the authenticity of the atmosphere presented to us.”37  Most certainly, 

Lovecraft added, the edifice itself—"with its spectral peaks, its clustered chimneys, its 

overhanging second story, its grotesque corner-brackets, and its diamond-paned lattice 

windows”—was “an object well calculated to evoke sombre [sic] reflections; typifying as it does 

the dark Puritan age of concealed horror and witch-whispers which preceded the beauty, 

rationality, and spaciousness of the eighteenth century.”38 Ultimately, though, it was not 

Hawthorne—“always gentle, elusive, and restrained” in his idea of the weird—that towered over 

nineteenth century horror.39 Instead it was Edgar Allan Poe, with all of his violence, daring, 

 35 Ibid., 268-269. 36 Brenda Wineapple. Hawthorne: A Life. (New York: Random House, 2004), 238. 37 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror of Literature, 31. 38 Ibid., 48.   39 Ibid., 47.   

                                                                                                                       57 

“intense dramatic sense,” “cosmic malignity,” and “undivided and impersonal artistry,” that 

ushered in a new age.40   

The key figure, then, in the development of “horror” in the United States was Edgar Allan 

Poe (1809-1849)—a figure that H.P. Lovecraft later called the “deity and fountain-head [sic] of all 

modern diabolic fiction.”41  Born in Boston, Poe spent his early years in Virigina, where his parents 

were actors.  His father, a native of Baltimore, abandoned the family when Poe was an infant.  

Then, in late 1811, Poe’s mother, Eliza, died of tuberculosis in Richmond.42   Raised by a wealthy 

family in Richmond, Poe spent his youth in England and Scotland before returning to Richmond 

in 1820.  In 1826 and 1827, Poe attended the University of Virginia in Charlottesville.  In 1827, he 

enlisted in the military, and in 1830, attended West Point. The following year he was court-

martialed and dismissed from the academy.43 In 1831, Poe relocated to Baltimore and began to 

write full-time.44. He soon began to publish short stories, and in 1833 was awarded a prize from 

the Baltimore Saturday Visitor for his story, “MS. Found in a Bottle.”45  

“MS. Found in a Bottle” detailed the experiences of an unnamed narrator alone and adrift 

on a cargo ship near the South Pole. Eventually, the narrator encountered (and boarded) another 

ship, the Galleon. The ship was ancient, as were its contents, including maps and instruments.  

Furthermore, the narrator could not be seen by any of the ship’s inhabitants.  The narrator, then, 

 40 Ibid. 41 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction (New York: Barnes & Noble, 2008), 1067. 42 Eliza Poe was an actress at the Richmond Theatre, which was destroyed in a devastating fire just a few weeks after her death, killing seventy-two people, including the governor, George William Smith, and a former U.S. Senator.  43 It was during these years that Poe published his first book of poems Tamerlane and Other Poems (1827). The collection received little notice and only 50 copies were printed. Poe continued to publish poetry while in the military. 44 Jeffrey Meyers. Edgar Allan Poe: His Life and Legacy. (New York: Cooper Square Press, 1992), 138.  45 Dawn B. Sova. Edgar Allan Poe A to Z: The Essential Reference to His Life and Work. (New York: Checkmark Books, 2001), 162.  

                                                                                                                       58 

retired to the captain’s cabin to write his journal—i.e. his manuscript (“MS”) of his ordeal.  He 

then placed it in a bottle and threw it into the sea.  The ship proceeded southward and sank in a 

whirlpool in a clearing in the ice--  the only proof of its journey being a letter released at sea by 

the unseen narrator.  While possibly a satire of the sea stories that were popular at the time, Poe’s 

story had many of the same attributes as later, weird literature-- specifically the use of atmosphere 

over plot.  Indeed, the reader remained adrift with the narrator and was unaware of the reasons 

behind the series of events that had befallen him.   

In 1835, Poe became an assistant editor at the Southern Literary Messenger but was 

discharged a few weeks later for being drunk at the job. Shortly after that, in 1835, Poe—aged 

twenty-six-- married his thirteen-year-old cousin, Virginia Clemm (1822-1847).  Afterwards he 

was reinstated at the Messenger where he was employed until 1837. In 1838 he relocated to 

Philadelphia and begin to work on the short novel, The Narrative of Arthur Gordon Pym of 

Nantucket—at tale of Pym and his adventures upon various ships-- which became one of his first 

works to be widely reviewed. While the work was not a part of the weird literature cannon it shared 

the linear plotlines of other classics of literature such as Voltaire’s Candide in its description of 

misadventures. The work itself was a critical and a commercial failure though its influence was 

quite significant. Indeed, one author later inspired by it was the French author Jules Verne, author 

of Twenty Thousand Leagues Under the Sea (1870) and Around the World in Eighty Days (1872). 

Verne even published a sequel to the novel in 1897, entitled An Antarctic Mystery, which followed 

a crew looking for answers to the question of what occurred to Pym.46  

 46 John Tresch. “Extra! Extra! Poe Invents Science Fiction” The Cambridge Companion to Edgar Allan Poe. In Kevin J. Hayes, ed. (New York: Cambridge University Press, 2002), 117. 

                                                                                                                       59 

Despite the commercial failure of The Narrative of Arthur Gordon Pym of Nantucket, Poe 

continued to write and soon became an assistant editor for Burton's Gentlemen Magazine in 

Philadelphia. Then, in 1839, he published one of his most influential short story collections, Tales 

of the Grotesque and Arabesque.  While the work was comprised of previously published stories— 

including “Morella” (1835), “William Wilson” (1839), and “The Fall of the House of Usher” 

(1839)--  it was the first time that all of them were included in one volume. “Morella” was one of 

the earliest stories in the collection, and recounted the narrative of a man, whose wife, Morella 

passed away giving birth to a healthy daughter.  As Morella took her last breath, she declared “I 

am dying, yet shall I live.”47 As the daughter—which the narrator never named-- grew older, she 

bore a striking resemblance to her mother. Unnerved, the father decided to have the daughter 

baptized.  When the minister asked for her name, the daughter responded “Morella,” and then 

declared “I am here.”48  The child, then, died. The story ended with the daughter’s body being 

taken to the family vault, that when opened, had no trace of her mother’s body. It was with 

“Morella” that Poe began his true journey into weird literature with a plot that encompassed 

supernatural atmospherics with no explanation for the occurrences within.49  

“William Wilson,” meanwhile, was a “weird” story involving a doppelganger-- i.e. “a 

ghostly double of a living person, [especially] one that haunts its own fleshly counterpart.”50  

Wilson was a nobleman who first met his double as a child. His double looked like him, dressed 

liked him, and possessed the same name.  Throughout his life, Wilson’s double was there to annoy 

 47 Edgar Allan Poe. “Morella” Complete Tales and Poems of Edgar Allan Poe. (New York: Vintage Books, 1975), 669.   48 Ibid.,670.  49 The story held a major influence on horror literature and was adapted into a film by the famous independent producer and director Roger Corman in the 1960s. 50 William Morris, The American Heritage Dictionary (Boston: Houghton Mifflin, 1982), 418. The Russian writer Fyodor Dostoevsky also used the doppelganger in his 1846 novel, The Double. 

                                                                                                                       60 

him, and to sabotage his (usually less than honorable) plans.  Eventually, Wilson forced his double 

into a sword fight and killed him.  Then, a large mirror appeared, and Wilson—to his terror—saw 

“mine own image, but with features all pale and dabbled in blood.”  His reflection was his 

doppelganger, who now declared: “You have conquered, and I yield. Yet henceforward art thou 

also dead to the world, to Heaven, and to Hope! In me did thou exist-- and, in my death see by this 

image, which is thine own, how utterly thou has murdered thyself.”51 

Then, in “The Fall of the House of Usher”—a story Lovecraft later called “the supreme 

weird tale of all the ages”-- Poe chronicled the visit of an unnamed narrator to the home of his 

childhood friend, Roderick Usher, whom he had not seen in years.52  The narrator—Roderick’s 

“best and only friend”— admitted that “I really knew little of my friend.  His reserve had been 

always excessive and habitual. I was aware, however, that his very ancient family had been 

noted… for a peculiar sensibility of temperament…”  Roderick’s home—described by the narrator 

as a “mansion of gloom”—was surrounded by “an atmosphere which had no affinity with the air 

of heaven, but which had reeked up from the decayed trees, and the grey wall, and the silent tarn— 

a pestilent and mystic vapour, dull sluggish, faintly discernible, and leaden-hued.”53  Upon meeting 

his friend, the narrator was shocked at his physical decline.  Indeed, “The now ghastly pallor of 

the skin, and the now miraculous lustre [sic] of the eye, above all things startled and even awed 

me.”54  Roderick told the narrator that “the nature of his malady” was “a constitutional and a family 

 51 Edgar Allan Poe. “William Wilson” Complete Tales and Poems of Edgar Allan Poe (New York: Vintage Books, 1975), 641. Was the double a literal double, or William’s consciousness, or a supernatural figure? Poe left the question unanswered.  Instead, he focused on plot and atmosphere.  Washington Irving was a fan of the story, insisting that it had a “highly picturesque style and the singular ad mysterious interest is well sustained throughout.” Brian Jay Jones. Washington Irving: An American Original (New York: Arcade Publishing, 2008), 334. 52 Lovecraft to Wilfred Branch Talman, August 24, 1926, in Derleth and Wanderlei, H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1925-1929, 69. 53 Edgar Allan Poe, Edgar Allan Poe’s Tales of Mystery and Imagination (London: E.P. Dutton & Co., 1928), 129-130. 54 Ibid., 132. 

                                                                                                                       61 

evil… [that] displayed itself in a host of unnatural sensations”—including “a morbid acuteness of 

the senses; the most insipid food was alone endurable; he could wear only garments of certain 

texture; the odours [sic] of all flowers were oppressive; his eyes were tortured by even a faint light; 

and there were but peculiar sounds, and these from stringed instruments, which did not inspire him 

with horror.”55    

Roderick lived in the house with his sickly, twin sister, “Lady Madeline,” with whom the 

exact nature of his relationship was unclear.  Shortly after the narrator’s arrival, Madeline died 

and—due to Roderick’s fear that her body would be exhumed by unscrupulous physicians-- was 

temporarily entombed in a family vault inside the house.  A few weeks later, as Roderick slipped 

further into madness—the narrator heard “a distinct, hollow, metallic, and clangorous, yet muffled 

reverberation.”  The narrator was “[c]ompletely unnerved,” but Roderick was undisturbed. “[Y]es, 

I hear it, and have heard it” Roderick confessed. “Long—long—long—many minutes, many hours, 

many days, have I heard it—yet I dared not—oh, pity me, miserable wretch that I am!”  In horror, 

the narrator immediately understood that Madeline had been placed in the vault alive.  Suddenly, 

Madeline—bloody and “with the evidence of some bitter struggle upon every portion of her 

emaciated frame”--  burst through the door.  She gave “a low moaning cry” and then “fell heavily 

inward upon the person of her brother, and in her violent and now final death agonies, bore him to 

the floor a corpse, and a victim to the terrors he had anticipated.”  Aghast, the narrator fled from 

the house and out into a storm. Looking back, he saw “a wild light” and the house collapsing.  56    

In 1840, one year after the publication of Tales of the Grotesque and Arabesque, Poe began 

working to establish his own journal—what he originally called The Penn. In a letter to James 

 55 Ibid., 133. 56 Ibid., 143-144. 

                                                                                                                       62 

Russell Lowell in early 1844, Poe reflected on the “dreadful… present condition of our Literature,” 

and expressed hope that his proposed journal could be “externally, a specimen of high, but not too 

refined Taste... Its chief aims should be Independence, Truth, Originality.”57  By the late 1840s, his 

“endeavor to support the general interests of the republic of letters, without reference to particular 

regions,” was still only a dream, and one that at the time of his death in 1849, remained 

unrealized.58   Meanwhile, he continued to work as an editor of journals, and to write short stories.  

In 1841, Poe became an editor of the successful Graham’s Magazine in Philadelphia. A few years 

later, he moved to New York City, where he became editor (and later owner) of The Broadway 

Journal.  It was during his time at The Broadway Journal that Poe wrote some of his most famous 

works, including the poems “The Raven” (1845) and “Annabel Lee” (1849).  

Both poems resonated with a culture acutely aware of the possibility of early death.  

Published in the New York Evening Mirror in January 1845, “The Raven” was a narrative poem 

that told of a talking Raven, who— “Once upon a midnight dreary”—visited the “weak and weary” 

narrator, who was grieving his “lost Lenore.”  The “ebony bird” tapped at his window, and then 

“Perched upon a bust of Pallas above my chamber door.”  His only word was “Nevermore.”  Set 

in December and late at night, the poem exhibited a supernatural atmosphere.  Indeed, the narrator 

recognized the Raven as “Prophet” with a message from the afterlife—i.e. “from the Night’s 

Plutonian shore.” The narrator—full of “sorrow laden” -- showered the bird with questions such 

as “Is there—is there balm in Gilead?” and would he ever again “clasp the rare and radiant maiden 

whom the angels name Lenore[?]” To these questions, the Raven responded simply, 

 57 Edgar Allan Poe to James Russell Lowell, March 30, 1844.  Edgar Allan Poe Society of Baltimore. https://www.eapoe.org/works/letters/p4403300.htm 58 Dawn B. Sova. Edgar Allan Poe: A o Z. (New York: Checkmark Books, 2001), 183.  By 1846, Poe had renamed his proposed journal to The Stylus.   

                                                                                                                       63 

“Nevermore.”59 Similarly, Poe’s last poem, “Annabel Lee” (1849), explored death and grief.  

Unlike “The Raven,” however, there was no supernatural element. The loss “Of the beautiful 

Annabel Lee” was horror in and of itself.  Still, the narrator’s coping mechanism at the close of 

the poem was eerie and macabre: 

  For the moon never beams without bringing me dreams 

  Of the beautiful Annabel Lee; 

  And the stars never rise but I feel the bright eyes 

  Of the beautiful Annabel Lee; 

  And so, all the night-tide, I lie down by the side 

  Of my darling—my darling—my life and my bride, 

  In the sepulchre there by the sea, 

  In her tomb by the sounding sea.60 

The 1840s also witnessed the publication of more Poe short stories—including “The 

Masque of the Red Death” (1842), “The Pit and the Pendulum” (1842), “The Tell-Tale Heart” 

(1843), and “The Black Cat” (1843).  Some of the stories were “tales of logic and ratiocination, 

forerunners of the modern detective story,” while others dealt with “abnormal psychology and 

monomania as to express terror but not weirdness.”  Others still, possessed an extravagance that 

relegated them “to the borderline of the grotesque.”61  In all of them, James Russell Lowell wrote 

in 1845, Poe had “chosen to exhibit his power chiefly in that dim region which stretches from the 

very utmost limits of the probable into the weird confines of superstition and unreality.”62  

 59 John Henry Ingram, ed., The Complete Poetical Works of Edgar Allan Poe (Chicago: Donohue & Henneberry, 1890), 161-167. 60 Ibid., 186-187. 61 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction (New York: Barnes & Noble, 2008), 1067. 62 James Russell Lowell, “Edgar A. Poe,” Graham’s Magazine (February 1845), reprinted in Edgar Allan Poe, Prose Tales (New York: Thomas Y. Crowell & Company, 1901), xv. 

                                                                                                                       64 

Regardless, one literary admirer observed in the early twentieth century, he “established a new 

standard of realism in the annals of literary horror.”63 

While his creative output during this period, then, was great, Poe’s personal life 

experienced rapid decline.  In 1842, his wife, Virginia, was diagnosed with tuberculosis, to which 

she succumbed in 1847.  Poe himself, who may have “possessed much of the depression 

sensitiveness, mad aspiration, loneliness, and extravagant freakishness” of his own characters, 

sank into alcoholism, financial distress, and his own physical decline.64 In 1848, Poe was briefly 

engaged to Sarah Helen Whitman, a transcendentalist and amateur poet from Providence, Rhode 

Island.  That December, Whitman called off the wedding due to Poe’s problem with alcohol.  Poe, 

who still lived in New York, later traveled to Richmond, Virginia, where he became reunited with 

a childhood sweetheart, Elmira Royster Shelton.65 Then, on October 3, 1849, he was found in 

Baltimore “rather the worse for wear… [and] in great distress.”66  Incoherent, the forty-year-old 

Poe was taken to Washington Medical Center, where he died on October 7, 1849, of what one 

newspaper called “congestion of the brain.”67  

In his 1927 essay on “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” Lovecraft  -- who deeply admired 

Poe, and even dedicated a whole chapter to him in the essay—argued that Poe was a “literary dawn 

directly affecting not only the history of the weird tale, but that short fiction as a whole...”68  Indeed, 

Lovecraft insisted, he “invented the short story in its present form.”  Specifically, though, Lovecraft 

 63 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction (New York: Barnes & Noble, 2008), 1066. 64 Ibid.,1069. 65 “The Mysterious Death of Edgar Allan Poe,” The Edgar Allan Poe Society of Baltimore. https://www.eapoe.org/geninfo/poedeath.htm. 66 Joseph W. Walker to Dr. J.E. Snodgrass, October 3, 1849, quoted in Ibid. 67 Baltimore Clipper quoted in Ibid.  While the exact reasons for Poe’s death remain unknown, “congestion of the brain” was a nineteenth century euphemism for death as a result of alcoholism. 68 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction (New York: Barnes & Noble, 2008), 1065. 

                                                                                                                       65 

believed that Poe’s greatness was rooted in two things.  One, there was “the pervasive potency of 

his mind as an opener of artistic vistas.”  Prior to Poe, Lovecraft observed, “the bulk of weird 

writers had worked largely in the dark; without an understanding of the psychological basis of the 

horror appeal, and hampered by… conformity to certain empty literary conventions such as the 

happy ending [and] virtue rewarded…”  In contrast, Poe “perceived the essential impersonality of 

the real artist; and knew that the function of creative fiction is merely to express and interpret 

events and sensations as they are, regardless of how they tend or what they prove—good or evil, 

attractive or repulsive, stimulating or depressing—[and] with the author always acting as a vivid 

and detached chronicler rather than a teacher, sypathiser [sic], or vendor of opinion.”69  And two, 

Poe understood “that all phases of life and thought are equally eligible as a subject-matter for the 

artist, and being inclined by temperament to strangeness and gloom, decided to be the interpreter 

of [things that]…  attended pain rather than pleasure, decay rather than growth, terror rather than 

tranquility, and which are fundamentally either adverse or indifferent to the tastes and traditional 

outward sentiments of mankind, and to the health, sanity, and normal expansive welfare of the 

species.”70 

Twelve years after Poe’s death, the United States was plunged into a different kind of 

horror—civil war. Over the course of the American Civil War (1861-1865), over 630,000 

Americans were killed, and thousands more were maimed for life.  In her superb This Republic of 

Suffering: Death and the Civil War (2008), historian Drew Gilpin Faust noted that the war 

embarked the United States “on a new relationship with death,” marked by the “prominence of 

bodies… [in dooryards and along streets] that depicted their destruction and deformation, 

 69Ibid. 70 Ibid., 1065-1066. 

                                                                                                                       66 

inevitably raising the question of how they related to the persons who had once inhabited them.”71  

Indeed, Faust observed, “Civil War Americans often wrote about what they called ‘the work of 

death,’ meaning the duties of the soldiers to fight, kill, and die, but at the same time invoking 

battle’s consequences: its slaughter, suffering, and devastation.” It was “work” to die, she 

concluded, and it was also “work” to deal with the dead—i.e. “to remove them in the literal sense 

of disposing of their bodies…”72  Meanwhile, the advent of Matthew Brady’s photography 

revealed in vivid and gruesome images this “harvest of death” to those further removed from the 

battlefield.73  Later, of course, the war was also revealed in literature—both in non-fiction 

(memoirs and histories) and fiction.  One of the most popular American fiction writers in the late 

nineteenth century—known for both his horror and Civil War stories—was Ambrose Bierce (1842-

1914).   

Born in Meigs County, Ohio during the prime of Poe’s writing career, Bierce spent much 

of his youth in Indiana. When the Civil War broke out in 1861, Bierce, aged nineteen, enlisted with 

the Union Army’s 9th Indiana Infantry, where he spent the next four years.  In 1881, he wrote his 

memoir What I Saw of Shiloh, depicting his experiences in the horrific battle from April 1862 that 

resulted in nearly 24,000 casualties in just two days. “Ah, Youth,” Bierce wrote, “There is no such 

wizard as thou! Give me but one touch of thine artist’s hand upon the dull canvas of the present; 

gild for but one moment the dear and somber scenes of to-day. And I will willingly surrender the 

one that I should have thrown away at Shiloh.”74 In the postwar era, Bierce worked as a journalist 

in San Francisco, becoming renown for his true crime reporting, and ultimately, his short stories.  

 71 Drew Gilpin Faust, This Republic of Suffering: Death and the American Civil War (New York: Alfred A. Knopf, 2008), xi & xvii. 72 Ibid., xiv. 73 Ibid., xiii. 74 Ambrose Bierce. “What I Saw of Shiloh” American Literature.com. Accessed May 7, 2024. https://americanliterature.com/author/ambrose-bierce/short-story/what-i-saw-of-shiloh 

                                                                                                                       67 

In the early 1870s, he published three collections: The Fiends Delight (1872), Nuggets and Dust 

Panned Out In California (1872), and Cobwebs from an Empty Skull (1874). Bierce soon returned 

to San Francisco after being offered the prestigious position of co-editor of the Argonaut, which 

eventually lead to a five-year tenure during the 1880’s as the editor of The San Francisco 

Illustrated Wasp while also attempting and failing at a career in mining. After that position he was 

able to obtain a position under the most famous newspaper mogul of the time, William Randolph 

Hearst, and his paper The San Francisco Examiner where Bierce had the opportunity to write his 

own column. In the 1890s, Bierce published some of his most memorable stories across four 

collections: Tales of Soldiers and Civilians (1891), The Monk and the Hangman’s Daughter (1892), 

Black Beetles in Amber (1892), Can Such Things Be? (1893), and Fantastic Fables (1899). 

Overall, Bierce’s stories— including his most famous, “An Occurrence at Owl Creek 

Bridge” (1890)-- earned him a reputation as “a minor prophet of hopelessness.”75  Indeed, H.P. 

Lovecraft called his stories “grim and savage.”  The were virtually all “tales of horror,” Lovecraft 

wrote in 1927, “and whilst many of them treat only of the physical and psychological horrors 

within Nature, a substantial proportion admit the malignly supernatural and form a leading element 

in America’s fund of weird literature.”76  Meanwhile, another contemporary, literary critic and poet 

Samuel Loveman insisted that “In Bierce, the evocation of horror becomes for the first time, not 

so much the prescription or perversion of Poe and Maupassant, but an atmosphere definite and 

uncannily precise. Words so simple… [now] take on an unholy horror, a new and unguessed 

transformation.”77  According to early Bierce scholar Clifton Fadiman, Bierce ran counter to the 

 75 Clifton Fadiman, “Ambrose Bierce: Portrait of a Misanthrope,” in The Collected Writings of Ambrose Bierce (New York: The Citadel Press, 1946), xiv. 76 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction (New York: Barnes & Noble, 2008), 1074. 77 Samuel Loveman quoted in Ibid. 

                                                                                                                       68 

“dominating tendency of American literature and social thought”—i.e. optimism.  Instead, like 

Hawthorne and Poe, he was a part of “a narrower current, the shadowed stream of pessimism.”  

His nihilism, Fadiman wrote, was “brutal”-- the mind of Man darkened like the passing of day, 

and hatred, violence, and despair soon followed.78   

Originally published in the San Franciso Examiner in July 1890 (and later, in the 1891 

collection, Tales of Soldiers and Civilians), “An Occurrence at Owl Creek Bridge” centered around 

the experience of Peyton Farquhar, an Alabama slaveholder and Confederate sympathizer during 

the Civil War who was captured by Union forces while attempting to burn the railroad bridge at 

Owl Creek.  As he was being prepared for hanging, the hangman’s rope broke, and Peyton fell 

from the bridge into the creek (and safety). His route home through the woods, however, was 

strange and confusing, and he marveled “that he lived in so wild a region.”  Tired and hungry, he 

nevertheless pressed forward through the dark night—“[t]he thought of his wife and children urged 

him on.”  Finally, at dawn, he arrived at “the gate of his own home… [with] his wife, looking fresh 

and cool and sweet” there to greet him.  As he reached out to embrace his wife, Peyton felt “a 

stunning blow upon the back of the neck; a blinding white light blaze[d] all about him with a sound
