published The Origin of Species, which “created an entirely new climate for the study of man, 

history and the development of civilization.” Indeed, the very idea of “prehistory” was new—“the 

word itself only came  into common currency in Europe with Daniel Wilson’s Prehistoric Annals 

(1851) and John Lubbock’s Prehistoric Times, published in 1865.”  Thus, when Schliemann first 

broke spade at Hisarlik in 1870, “most western intellectuals viewed ‘civilization’ as meaning their 

own culture: a Christian, western, capitalist, bourgeois, imperialist democracy. Their texts were 

the classical writers and the Bible, and empires such as the British and German were seen as the 

logical culmination of ancient culture…”34 The rise of archeology in the 1870s, however, revealed 

“the riches of civilizations far more ancient—Egyptian, Assyrian, Babylonian and Sumerian— 

which, when their languages were deciphered, turned out to have had an incalculable influence on 

the development of the ‘younger’ civilizations of the Mediterranean.”35 

Meanwhile, the late nineteenth century witnessed European colonialism in “exotic” lands, 

which, in turn, gave rise to new heroes and new military conflicts.  For example, in the 1840s and 

1850s, the Scottish missionary and explorer, David Livingstone (1813-1873), travelled throughout 

 33 Michael Wood, In Search of the Trojan War (New York: Facts on File Publications, 1985), 51. 34 Ibid. 35Ibid. 

                                                                                                                       105 

Africa looking for a convenient water route across “the Dark Continent.”  In 1857, he released a 

memoir of his African adventures, Missionary Travels, which became an immediate bestseller.  In 

1866, he set out in search of the source of the Nile River, and soon lost contact with the outside 

world.  Three years later, the owner of The New York Herald, James Gordon Bennett, Jr., funded 

an expedition, led by the Welsch-American explorer Henry Morton Stanley (1841-1904) to find 

Livingstone.  In November 1871, Stanley found Livingston in Tanzania, and upon returning home 

published the popular How I Found Livingstone (1872)—soon followed by Coomassie and 

Magdala (1874), Through the Dark Continent (1878), In Darkest Africa (1890) and My Dark 

Companions and Their Strange Stories (1893).   

European colonialism often led to tension, exploitation, and ultimately, the outbreak of 

conflict.  Belgian abuse in the Congo under Leopold II. (1836-1909), for example, inspired Joseph 

Conrad’s 1899 novella, The Heart of Darkness.36  Throughout the period, the leading European 

hegemon, Great Britain, was involved in a number of military conflicts, including the Anglo-

Afghan War (1878-1880), the Zulu War (1879), and the Boer Wars (1880-1882, 1899-1902).37  

There were also conflicts in Egypt (1882) and Burma (1885).  Meanwhile, between 1884 and 1885, 

the French fought the Chinese in the Sino-French War.  Unsurprisingly, then, this contact with 

non-European cultures—especially with the era’s technological advances in communications and 

transportation—impacted Western literature, including the popularization of adventure novels.  

Indeed, H. Rider Haggard (1856-1925), one of the period’s most popular, English adventure 

 36 See Adam Hochschild’s masterful King Leopold’s Ghost: A Story of Greed, Terror and Heroism in Colonial Africa (1998), for more detail on European exploitation of the Congo during this period.  37 The fictional Dr. John Watson, Sherlock Holmes’s associate in Arthur Conan Doyle’s classic detective series, was a veteran of the Anglo-Afghan War, and had also served the British Empire in India. 

                                                                                                                       106 

novelists, began his career as an unpaid secretary to the lieutenant-governor of the colony of Natal 

in South Africa in the 1870s.   

Haggard began his literary pursuits back in London in the early 1880s, publishing The 

Witch’s Head in 1884.  The story, set in South Africa, was met with modest success.  “Mr. 

Haggard,” one early reviewer observed, “knows a good deal about Zululand, and rifle shooting, 

and the wilder pleasures of the country, and he has contrived to make a lively story out of these 

and other materials… The description of [the witch’s head]—for it was an actual human head 

without the usual appendages-- is ghastly enough….  [but it] may be read without too many 

shudders, and the reader’s attention is retained throughout.”38  The following year, Haggard 

published his most famous work, King Solomon’s Mines (1885).  The story—somewhat 

reminiscent of Stanley’s search for Livingstone in 1871—followed the adventures of an explorer, 

Allan Quatermain and his small band as they searched for a missing person in unchartered areas 

of interior Africa.  The novel was an instant sensation, and was soon followed by Allan Quatermain 

in 1887, and then another twelve Allan Quatermain novels between 1887 and 1927.  King 

Solomon’s Mines also opened the door for future “lost world” works—i.e. those stories that 

involved the discovery of previously unknown civilizations.  Indeed, Rudyard Kipling’s “The Man 

Who Would Be King” (1888), Edgar Rice Burrough’s The Land That Time Forgot (1918) and 

Arthur Conan Doyle’s The Lost World (1912)—about a South American expedition through the 

Amazon basin where prehistoric animals still flourished—all followed in Haggard’s footsteps.  

As with science fiction and detective stories, the imperial adventure stories of the late 

nineteenth century were far removed from horror. Nevertheless, many of the authors of science 

 38 “Novels of the Week,” The Athenaeum (January 10, 1885), 49. 

                                                                                                                       107 

fiction, detective stories, and even “imperial pulp gothic,” were appreciated by later horror writers 

such as Lovecraft.  The Victorians—Collins, Haggard, Dickens, and Stevenson—“went in strongly 

for weird fiction,” Lovecraft wrote in the early 1930s. “[They] turned out reams of it…  It [weird 

fiction] reflects a permanent mood of human nature & will probably never become wholly 

extinct.”39  Furthermore, many of these late nineteenth century adventure authors—because of the 

content of their stories—stimulated interest, and inspired a young generation of writers, including 

Lovecraft—to start writing their own strange tales.   

Coinciding with these innovations and changes of the nineteenth century was the Romantic 

movement in literature.  Beginning in Europe in the late eighteenth century as a reaction to the 

Age of Reason, Romanticism emphasized a reverence for nature, a nostalgia for and celebration 

of an idealized and heroic past, and a fascination with the mysterious and supernatural. 40  Thus, 

twentieth century author Virginia Woolf later observed, “A rational age is succeeded by one which 

seeks the supernatural in the soul of man, and the development of the psychical research offers a 

basis of disputed fact for this desire to feed upon.”41  Across the nineteenth century, then, “ghosts” 

and “spirits” were often prominent in “mainstream” fiction—Charles Dickens’s 1843 A Christmas 

Carol being one obvious example.  By the 1850s, Spiritualism—i.e. the belief that “death is only 

a transition, a shedding of the body, and the spirits of individuals not only survive beyond the grave 

but also communicate from the other side [via a medium]”—was popular in both the United States 

 39 Lovecraft to Elizabeth Toldridge, August 28, 1933, in H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1932-1934, 239.  Lovecraft strongly disliked Burroughs, once referring to him as the “veriest [sic] idiot and ignoramus.” Writing a friend in the late 1920s, he added that “you may depend upon it that I shall not use… Edgar Rice Burroughs as my model!  Lovecraft to Zealia Brown Reed, March 20, 1929, in H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1925-1929, 325; Lovecraft to Clark Ashton Smith, December 3, 1929, in H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1929-1931, 88. 40 Maurice Cranston, The Romantic Movement (Cambridge: Blackwell-Wiley, 1994), 1.  41 Virginia Woolf, “The Supernatural in Fiction,” in Woolf, Granite and Rainbow (London: The Hogarth Press, 1958) 63. 

                                                                                                                       108 

and Great Britain.42  As early as 1847, Andrew Jackson Davis, a spiritualist from Blooming Grove, 

New York, authored The Principles of Nature, Her Divine Revelations, and a Voice to Mankind, 

insisting that “It is a truth that spirits commune with one another while one is in the body and the 

other in the higher spheres…. all the world will hail with delight the ushering in of that era when 

the interiors of men will be opened, and the spiritual communication will be established.”43 

The Late Victorian Era, then, represented a high tide for these various intellectual currents, 

and “horror” in all its forms—and especially “ghost” stories—became increasingly integrated into 

the literary impulses of the age.  Highlighting the period’s fascination with spirits, then, was The 

Turn of the Screw (1898), by the American-born Henry James (1843-1916).  One of the leading 

literary figures of the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, James enjoyed a long and varied 

literary career.  He was, Edith Wharton later observed, “essentially a novelist of manners, and the 

manners he was qualified by nature and situation to observe were those of the little vanishing group 

of people among whom he had grown up [in New York], or their more picturesque prototypes in 

older societies.”44 James’s great works, then, were of Gilded Age society, and the contrasting—in 

works such as The Americans (1877), The Europeans (1878), Daisey Miller (1879), Washington 

Square (1881), and The Portrait of a Lady (1881)--  of American and European values, 

perspectives, and social protocols relating to, among other things, class and gender.  “For better or 

worse,” Wharton added, “…it was the only food his imagination could fully assimilate.  He was 

acutely conscious of this limitation and often bewailed to me his total inability to use the ‘material,’ 

 42 Barbara Weisburg, Talking to the Dead: Kate and Maggie Fox and the Rise of Spiritualism (New York: Harper One, 2004), 5.  Literary figures such as Arthur Conan Doyle, Arthur Machen, and Algernon Blackwood were all associated with Spiritualism.  Even Queen Victoria and husband Prince Albert participated in seances as early as the 1840s.   43 Andrew Jackson Davis quoted in Abbott Kahler, “The Fox Sisters and the Rap on Spiritualism,” Smithsonian Magazine (October 30, 2012). https://www.smithsonianmag.com/history/the-fox-sisters-and-the-rap-on-spiritualism-99663697/. 44 Edith Wharton, A Backward Glance (New York: D. Appleton-Century Company, 1934), 176. 

                                                                                                                       109 

financial and industrial, of modern American life.”45  Most certainly, then, James was not known 

for horror writings.  Nevertheless, he published over the course of his career eighteen “weird” 

stories, including “The Romance of Certain Old Clothes” (1868), “The Ghostly Rental” (1876), 

“The Way It Came” (1896), “The Jolly Corner” (1908), and, of course, his ghostly masterpiece, 

The Turn of the Screw (1898).46   

Originally serialized in Collier’s Weekly—and later published in a small collection of James 

novellas entitled The Two Magics (1898)—The Turn of the Screw told the story of a young 

governess recently hired to care for two orphaned children at their uncle’s country estate.  One day, 

the governess saw a strange man standing outside a window and went to greet him.  Once outside, 

however, she could not find him.  She later told the housekeeper of the incident and described the 

man in the window.  She was informed that the description resembled a former worker on the estate 

who had recently died (along with the previous governess).   Unnerved, the governess began to 

see the two entities repeatedly and became convinced that they were spirits intending to harm the 

children.  The dark story, which ended with the death of one of the children, was ambiguous as to 

the reality of the situation—i.e. whether there were really ghosts or whether it was a tale of a 

downtrodden, young woman slipping into madness.  Because of that ambiguity, and the unease 

and uncertainty it instilled in the reader, it-- Edith Wharton observed in her 1925 book, The Writing 

of Fiction—stood "alone among tales of the supernatural in maintaining the ghostliness of its 

ghosts… close on two hundred [pages]—the economy of horror… carried out to its last degree.”47   

 45 Ibid. 46At the end of his life, he was working on a novel entitled The Sense of the Past—ultimately left uncompleted—that revealed “he was again experimenting with the supernatural as a subject for a long novel.”  Edith Wharton, The Writing of Fiction (New York: Charles Scribner’s Sons, 1925), 105.   47 Ibid., 40. 

                                                                                                                       110 

The story was an immediate commercial and critical success.  The Athenaeum, a British 

magazine published in London between 1828 and 1921, praised The Turn of the Screw as one of 

the “most engrossing and terrifying ghost stories ever read. It is a real creation, and the idea of it 

is quite novel.”48  Meanwhile, Virginia Woolf, in a 1918 essay entitled “The Supernatural in 

Fiction,” insisted that the short novel was a “masterpiece” that roused “‘the dear old sacred terror…  

[t]he horror of the story comes from the force with which it makes us realize the power that our 

minds possess for such excursions into the darkness; when certain lights sink or certain barriers 

are lowered, the ghosts of mind, untraced desires, indistinct imitations, are seen to be a large 

company.”49 Writing in the early 1930s, literary critic Edmund Wilson saw the novella as more of 

a psychological study of the governess.  “The Turn of the Screw is primarily intended as a 

characterization of the governess,” Wilson insisted.  “[H]er somber and guilty visions and the way 

she behaves about them seem to present, from the moment we examine them from the obverse side 

of her nature, an accurate and distressing picture of the poor country parson’s daughter, with her 

English middle class consciousness, her inability to admit to herself her natural sexual impulses 

and the relentless English ‘authority’ which enables her to put over on inferiors even purposes 

which are totally deluded and not at all in the other people’s best interests.”50  

Meanwhile, even the great Irish playwright and poet Oscar Wilde (1854-1900) added to the 

field—and sealed his reputation “amongst weird writers”--  with his classic novel, The Picture of 

Dorian Grey (1891).51 A morality tale of guilt and remorse, the story involved young Dorian Grey, 

 48 David L. Sweet, “Comment and Questions,” in Henry James. The Turn of The Screw, The Aspen Papers and Two Stories (New York: Barnes & Noble Press, 2003), 306. 49 Virginia Woolf, “The Supernatural in Fiction” in Woolf, Granite and Rainbow. (New York: Harcourt, Bruce and Co, 1958), 63. 50 Edmund Wilson, “The Ambiguity of Henry James,” in F.W. Dupee, ed., The Question of Henry James: A Collection of Critical Essays (New York: Henry Holt and Company, 1945), 166.  51 H.P. Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror in Literature (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2000), 55. 

                                                                                                                       111 

whose “marvelous portrait for years assume[d] the duty of aging and coarsening instead of the 

original [i.e. Dorian], who meanwhile plunge[d] into every excess of vice and crime without the 

outward loss of youth, beauty, and freshness.”52  Throughout the story Dorian was torn between 

two friends, Lord Henry Wotten, who encouraged Dorian to live a life of pleasure and vice, and 

artist Basil Hallward, who encouraged him to live one of virtue.  Henry’s advice won out, and for 

the next twenty years Dorian lived an excessive and hedonistic lifestyle—all the while maintaining 

the look of an eighteen-year-old.  At the story’s conclusion, Basil confronted him, and petitioned 

Dorian to make amends for his sins.  “It is never too late, Dorian,” Basil insisted.  “Let us knell 

down and try if we cannot remember a prayer. Isn’t there a verse somewhere. ‘Though your sins 

be as scarlet, yet I will make them as white as snow.’?”53  In a fit of anger Dorian murdered Basil.  

Riddled with guilt, Dorian then stabbed his portrait in the heart with a knife.  Shortly thereafter 

Dorian’s body was discovered by his servants.  “When they entered, they found hanging upon the 

wall a splendid portrait of their master as they had last seen him, in all the wonder of exquisite 

youth and beauty. Lying on the floor was a dead man, in evening dress, with a knife in his heart. 

He was withered, wrinkled, and loathsome of visage. It was not to they had examined the rings 

that they recognized who it was.”54 

Unquestionably, though, horror itself was dominated by the publication in 1897 of Dracula, 

by the Irish writer Bram Stoker (1847-1912).   Stoker was very prolific and versatile, and first 

began publishing stories in the mid-1870s.  One of his first was The Chain of Destiny (1875), a 

gothic romance novella first serialized in the Dublin paper, Shamrock. Then, in 1881, his first 

collection of “weird” and fantasy short stories, Under the Sunset, was published.  He even ventured 

 52 Ibid. 53 Oscar Wilde, The Picture of Dorian Grey. (New York: Barnes and Noble Publishers, 2004), 162. 54 Ibid., 229. 

                                                                                                                       112 

into imperial fiction with The Snake’s Pass (1890), and American westerns with The Sholder of 

Shasta (1895).  It was, of course, Dracula that established Stoker as a popular writer.     

The tale—which was told through journal entries, letters, and newspaper accounts-- began 

with Johnathan Harker, a young British solicitor in route to Romania to meet Count Dracula-- a 

wealthy nobleman whom Harker believed was interested in buying real estate in England. Harker 

was immediately unnerved by both Dracula and his castle and soon found himself a prisoner.   In 

one of his attempts at escape, Harker wandered into a room where he was confronted by three 

female vampires.  In a combination of horror and eroticism, the three vampires began to bite and 

feed off Harker, who was rescued only by the appearance of Dracula.  Compensating the three 

female vampires for their loss, Dracula gave them a small bag—its contents being a bound child-

- upon which to feed.  The Count then left on his journey to Britian while Harker was left at the 

castle planning his escape.  

Meanwhile, in England, Mina Murray, Harker’s finance, anxiously awaited Harker’s return 

from abroad.  The Count, though, had arrived by ship at Whitby (in North Yorkshire), and soon 

began to prey upon Mina’s friend, Lucy Westenra—a vivacious girl with three suitors: Dr. John 

Seward, the American Quincy Morris, and Lord Arthur Holmwood.  After Lucy became weak and 

ill, a concerned Seward discovered that she showed signs of anemia.  Troubled, Seward called 

upon his mentor, Dr. Van Hesling. Together they gave Lucy blood transfusions to restore her to 

health, and to keep her alive. Still, every day she continued to be drained of blood.55  As Lucy 

succumbed to her injuries, Van Helsing realized that it was a vampire who was responsible for her 

“death.”  Indeed, Van Helsing and company—i.e. Seward, Morris, and Holmwood—later 

 55 As Lucy’s health deteriorated, Mina traveled to Romana after having discovered that Johnathan was alive and recovering from a serious illness. The two were shortly thereafter married. 

                                                                                                                       113 

confronted and destroyed an “undead” Lucy.   Seeking retribution for Lucy’s death, Van Helsing 

and company sought to find and kill Dracula.  They ultimately tracked him back to Romania, and 

after a climactic struggle, defeated and destroyed the vampire. 

Stoker’s Dracula, of course, was not the first in vampire literature.  While there were some 

vampire references in some eighteenth century German poems—including Heinrich August 

Ossenfelder’s “The Vampire” (1747) and Goethe’s “The Bride of Corinth” (1797)--  the first 

mention of vampires in English did not appear until 1801, and Robert Southy’s epic poem, Thalaba 

the Destroyer.  Then, in 1819, John Polidori (1795-1821) published the short story “The Vampyre” 

(1819), one of the earliest vampire tales, and certainly the starting point of the vampire in popular, 

narrative literature.56  Polidori, the son of an Italian scholar, was a medical doctor, receiving his 

medical degree at the University of Edinburgh in 1814.  Shortly thereafter he became the personal 

physician of the poet Lord Byron and was one of the guests of Percy and Mary Shelley at the 1817 

dinner party that inspired Shelly’s Frankenstein. Indeed, Polidori’s own work to come out of that 

encounter was “The Vampyre,” first published by London’s New Monthly Magazine in 1819.57  

With this story, Polidori established the vampire character as a “suave villain of the true gothic or 

Byronic type [i.e. aristocratic, powerful, and sexual],” and presented readers with “excellent 

passages of stark fright.”58  

Overall, “The Vampyre” followed the travels and travails of Aubrey, a wealthy, young 

gentleman from London.  At a dinner party, Aubrey met a sophisticated British nobleman, Lord 

Ruthven, and the two set out to travel across Europe.  Soon, though, Aubrey grew suspicious of 

 56 Bram Stoker. “Introduction” Dracula. (New York: Barnes & Noble, 2013), ix.  57 John Polidori. The Vampyre And Other Tales of the Macabre. “Introduction” Ed. By Robert Morrison and Chris Baldick. (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2008), vi-vii. Two years later, Polidori, only aged twenty-six, committed suicide.     58 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror of Literature, 17.  

                                                                                                                       114 

Ruthven’s morals and character—especially after Ruthven attempted to seduce the daughter of a 

mutual acquaintance in Rome—and departed from his company.  Later, in Greece, Aubrey fell in 

love with a local girl named Ianthe, who told him about the area’s vampire legends (many of which 

reminded Aubrey of Ruthven).  Then, Ianthe was violently murdered—her throat torn out—and 

Aubrey was seriously wounded.  A peculiar dagger was found at the site of Ianthe’s murder, but 

locals insisted she was the victim of a vampire attack.  Ultimately, Ruthven arrived in the village 

and nursed Aubrey back to health.  Somewhat recovered, Aubrey agreed to travel again with 

Ruthven. Shortly thereafter, the two travelers were attacked by bandits, and Ruthven was killed.  

As he was dying, Ruthven extracted an oath from Aubrey pledging not to tell anyone of his death 

for a year and one day.   When Aubrey returned to London, he was shocked to find Ruthven alive 

and engaged to his sister.   Bound by his oath, Aubrey watched helplessly as Ruthven—now living 

as the Earl of Marsden--  integrated himself into his own family.  Before the wedding—set for the 

day that Aubrey’s oath expired—Aubrey suffered a stroke. He was ultimately able to recount the 

truth about Ruthven before dying but it was too late.  Ruthven was gone, and Aubrey’s sister was 

found dead—her body drained of blood.  Near her body was a sheath that matched the dagger 

found at Ianthe’s crime scene.    

Vampires made another significant stride in English literature with the publication, in the 

mid-1840s, of James Malcolm Rymer’s Varney the Vampire; or The Feast of Blood (1847).59  

Originally serialized in the penny dreadfuls in 1845 and 1846, it was published in book form— 

 59 The authorship has long been the topic of furious controversy.  The publisher of the Varney penny dreadfuls did not allow authors to include their names on any of the penny dreadfuls. Some, such as Lovecraft in the 1920s, believed that Thomas Prest was the author, or possibly co-author with Rymer.  Indeed, the two writers collaborated to create the Sweeney Todd—the murderous barber—in The String of Pearls penny dreadful series (1846-1847).  Most scholars today believe that Rymer was the sole author of Varney the Vampire.  

                                                                                                                       115 

numbering almost nine hundred pages—in 1847.60  While Rymer’s vampire could eat human food 

and be outside during the daytime, it was with Varney that some of the lasting vampire tropes were 

established.  For example, Varney possessed hypnotic powers and attacked one of his victims by 

entering through a window.  He also had fangs, and left puncture marks on the necks of his victims.  

Furthermore, Varney was a complex psychological character.  Acutely aware of his hideous and 

lonely condition, Varney-- “the first existential vampire”—loathed himself and the misery he 

created.  Indeed, one modern scholar observed, “No other literary vampire before 1845 had the 

psychological insight into his own condition like Sir Varney.”61 

Another important early vampire story was “Carmilla” (1872), by Irish writer Sheridan Le 

Fanu (1814-1878).  Originally serialized in the magazine Dark Blue, the novella was published in 

Le Fanu’s collection of stories entitled In a Darkly Glass (1872).  Most of Le Fanu’s works were 

gothic horror in nature.  Indeed, his novels, including The House by the Churchyard (1863), Wyler’s 

Hand (1864), Uncle Silas (1864), and Guy Deverell (1865), all fell into that category, and it was 

as a horror writer that Le Fanu was most recognized.  “Carmilla” was unquestionably one of his 

most popular and influential stories. Typical of gothic tales, it was set in a castle in a remote 

forest—in this case, Styria, Austria.  In general, it focused on Laura, a young maiden who lived 

with her father in a secluded and lonely castle.  One day a mysterious woman, accompanied by her 

daughter, Carmilla, arrived at the castle after having a carriage accident.  The mother explained 

that she must continue on her journey—which was, she insisted, of great importance-- and 

requested that Carmilla be allowed to stay at the castle for the next three months.  The two girls 

became fast friends, but Laura soon became uncomfortable with Carmilla’s behavior—including 

 60 Stefan Dziemianowich. “Introduction” Penny Dreadfuls: Sensational Tales of Terror. (New York: Barnes & Nobles, 2014), ix.  61 Curt Herr, ed., Varney the Vampire. (Crestline, California: Zittaw Press, 2008), 18.  At the end of the work, Varney ended his own “life” by throwing himself into an active volcano.   

                                                                                                                       116 

her sexual advances toward Laura, her moodiness, and her habit of sleeping for most of the day.  

Meanwhile, young women in surrounding areas began to die of an unknown illness, and Laura 

began having strange dreams—including being attacked by a large beast while asleep in bed.  

Soon, Laura’s own health began to fail. Shortly thereafter Laura’s father encountered an old friend, 

General Spielsdorf, who relayed that he too he had been approached by a mother and daughter, 

and that his own niece, Bertha, had been killed by the daughter, a vampire named Countess 

Mircalla Karnstein.  The two men realized the Carmilla was actually Mircalla, and assisted by 

Baron Vordenburg, a descendant of a vampire hunter who had first defeated Mircalla two centuries 

earlier, find the tomb of Mircalla, and destroyed her body—piercing a steak a through her heart, 

removing her head, and burning her body.62  

Dracula’s influence was felt almost immediately.63  Indeed, newspapers such as The Daily 

Mail informed their readers that “It is said of Mrs. Radcliffe that, when writing her now almost 

forgotten romances, she shut herself up in absolute seclusion, and fed upon raw beef, in order to 

give her work the desired atmosphere of gloom, tragedy and tear. If one had no assurance to the 

contrary, one might well suppose that a similar method and regimen had been adopted by Mr. Bram 

Stoker while writing his new novel Dracula.”64 The unnamed reviewer was very aware of the 

lineage of the gothic novel and his review. Other reviewers did not look as far back and compared 

the work to the novels of Wilkie Collins.65 Other writers called Stoker “The Edgar Allan Poe of 

 62 “Carmilla” was used as the source material for various films including Vampyr from 1932.  63 Dracula found its way into adaptations soon after its publication. The great grandnephew of Bram Stoker and published writer Dacre Stoker insisted that much of Dracula’s enduring status may come from the fact that because of failure by Stoker to comply by United States copyright law meant that a license fee was not needed to use the character in films or other adaptations.63 This eventually led to Dracula being one of most adapted novels of all. Movies as famous as F.W. Murnau’s Nosferatu (1929) and Todd Browning’s Dracula (1931) were based on the work with actors such as Bella Lugosi, Max Schreck and Chirstopher Lee becoming iconic for playing the role. Lovecraft recognized Stoker’s genius.  64 “Review” The Daily Mail. 1897, 3, accessed July 1, 2024 65 “Recent Novels” Reviews of Politics, Literature, Theology, and Art. 79. London, 150-151. July 31st, 1897. accessed July 1, 2024. 

                                                                                                                       117 

the nineties.” 66 Early on reviewers took notice of Stokers choice of setting with The Daily 

Telegraph going as far as comparing the settings of Stoker’s work with the by now almost ancient 

works of The Castle of Otranto.67  While he never again achieved the acclaim that he did with 

Dracula, Stoker continued to write weird fiction, including The Lair of the White Worm (1911), 

and a collection of short stories entitled Dracula’s Guest and Other Weird Stories (1914).  

Finally, there was Edith Wharton (1862-1937), whom Lovecraft regarded as “an eminate  

[sic] olde schooler getting close to the brink.”  Wharton also commented on the work of her close 

friend Henry James in the preface to the short story collection Ghosts (1937). Wharton stated that 

when discussing the “imaginative handling” of the supernatural tale, James was an expert. 

However, he was too “individualistic” to be put into the current categories of the ghost story.68 

Wharton was born to privileged wealth in New York City and spent time in Europe and Newport, 

Rhode Island. She began to write quite early in her youth and by the age of eighteen had already 

published poetry and a novella. Wharton wrote three of the most well-respected novels of the early 

twentieth century: The House of Mirth (1905), Ethan Frome (1911) and the Pulitzer Prize winning 

The Age of Innocence (1921), which made her the first woman to ever win the prestigious prize. 

She died in the year 1937. One of Whartons last literary acts was compiling her collection of short 

stories Ghosts (1937). In Whartons preface to the work she discussed the ways in which a ghost or 

horror story can be read. To be a “ghost feeler” one must be “sensible of invisible currents of being 

in certain places and at certain areas.”69 She described the ghost-feeler by using the question “Do 

you believe in ghosts” with the interesting answer “No I don’t believe in ghosts, but I’m afraid of 

 66 “Untitled” Publishers Circular and Bookseller’s Record of British and Foreign Language. London. August 7th, 1897, 131.  67 “Books Of The Day.” The Daily Telegraph. London. June 3rd, 1897. 68 Edith Wharton. Ghosts. (New York: New York Review Books, 2021), x. 69 Ibid., vi. 

                                                                                                                       118 

them.”70 To be a good ghost story, there need be no sources from which the story comes since “the 

good ones bring with them the internal proof of their ghostliness.”71 A good ghost story or horror 

story for that matter need two ingredients to survive according to Wharton “silence and 

continuity.”72 This was something that has been seen in many of the horror writers mentioned 

before Wharton and that would be observed in Lovecraft’s work as well. The horror of the story 

would only be seen by observing that some occurrences cannot be explained and that must be left 

to the imagination of the reader and that the true ingredient to create fear was atmosphere.  

The stories included in Ghosts were written between the years 1902 and 1913. The moods 

of the story vary because of the various eras. The first story in the collection “All Souls” were one 

of her last stories and gives a description of an injured woman who has woken up alone in a house 

isolated by a snowstorm and in need of help. While not a ghost story it was a work of horror that 

described the fear of mortality. The story “Pomegranate Seed” gives a description of  a couple who 

after having returned from their honeymoon begin to receive an array of letters that are in 

handwriting that she seems to recognize, and it is quite faint. The work when published was quite 

a sensation. One of the most popular works in the collection is “Afterward.” The story describes a 

couple who have  moved into a supposedly haunted house. While at first, they mock the house the 

mood is changed to terror after the husband disappears.  

As the nineteenth century came to its close horror had advanced and had become popular 

in ways that were unimaginable. The genre of Science Fiction had become popular through the 

works of Jules Verne and H.G. Wells with its immediate influence being found in the works of 

Robert Louis Stevenson. Writers of incredible importance begin to dabble in the horror genre with 

 70 Ibid., vi. 71 Ibid., x. 72 Ibid., ix. 

                                                                                                                       119 

iconic works written by such literary mavericks as Oscar Wilde, Henry James and Edith Wharton 

rewriting the rules of the horror and gothic genres. Horror, while becoming popular, also found 

critical significance in these writings. By the end of the century, the publication of Dracula soon 

made horror an extremely popular genre and make the vampire an image known worldwide. As 

the twentieth century arrived, horror continued to become ever more popular and ever more a part 

of the pulp realm despite the writings of what Lovecraft called the four “modern masters.”, perhaps 

the only four writers of Lovecraft’s day that equaled him in his ability to transform the genre as 

well as give it any sense of critical standing among serious literary readers.  

By the dawn of the twentieth century, then, the basis for the horror genre had been 

established.  Writing in his 1981 treatise on horror, Danse Macabre, Stephen King insisted that 

the “modern horror story” rested upon the foundation of three classic works: Shelley’s 

Frankenstein, Stevenson’s Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde, and Stoker’s Dracula.  “[A]t the center of 

each,” King wrote, “stands (or slouches) a monster that has come to join and enlarge what Burt 

Hatlan calls ‘the myth-pool’—that body of fictive literature in which all of us… have continually 

bathed.  Like an almost perfect Tarot hand representing our lusher concepts of evil, they can be 

neatly laid out: the Vampire, the Werewolf, and the Thing Without a Name.”73   In the 1920s, 

Lovecraft added a new dimension, and new card to the horror genre. What Lovecraft did that 

differed from this triumvirate was the introduction of horror that was not based on these gothic 

origins (though Lovecraft touched upon them early in his career) but on the horrors of the 

smallness of man and the absence of meaning. Using the philosophical ideals of cosmism and 

materialism, Lovecraft crafted horrific encounters that sometimes were not of these earth as well 

 73 The Ghost—“the best-known mythic figure of the supernatural”—could not, King added, be identified with or summarized by any single novel. Stephen King, Danse Macabre.  (New York: Everest House, 1981), 61. 

                                                                                                                       120 

as at their extremes having no consciousness (“The Color out of Space”) while having an 

## unreliable narrator as inspired by Poe with a psychological complexity.

121 

## Chapter 5: The Rise of H.P. Lovecraft

The life and career of H.P. Lovecraft coincided with the emergence of modern America.  

Indeed, the years of his short life (1890-1937) were marked by tremendous change, including 

industrial acceleration, rapid urbanization, and increased immigration. By the end of the 1920s, a 

modern disillusionment—grounded in world war and economic calamity—dominated the 

intellectual environment. Unsurprisingly, these transformations ushered in new ways of 

interpreting both the past and present.  Indeed, the late nineteenth century witnessed the rise of 

new academic disciplines—including anthropology, sociology, and psychology— that were 

grounded in the abstract and the subjective. Philosophic and scientific inquiry also became more 

materialistic and emphasized a universe without intelligent design or moral need.  It was in this 

modernizing environment that Lovecraft grew to maturity. Ultimately, it was an environment that 

he—steeped in a nostalgia for the “simple past”—often resisted.  Indeed, Lovecraft was a complex 

figure riddled with many contradictions. Inspired by philosophers such as Friedrich Nietzsche 

(1844-1900) and biologists such as Charles Darwin (1809-1882), Lovecraft was a devoted 

materialist, who—his own fiction aside-- had no belief in the supernatural and denied the existence 

of anything that could not be expressed under the guidelines used by chemistry or physics.  Indeed, 

Lovecraft embraced “cosmicism,” and its assertion that Man was insignificant and temporary.  

Meanwhile, he was a social conservative—more comfortable in a romanticized past than in his 

own and troubled twentieth century—and an ardent racist and xenophobe, who nevertheless 

married, in 1924, a Jewish immigrant from Russia.  In politics, however, he possessed socialist 

sympathies, and by the end of his of life, was an enthusiastic supporter of Democrat Franklin 

Roosevelt and the New Deal.   

                                                                                                                       122 

Born in August 1890, in Providence, Rhode Island—a city he later described as “My 

Providence! What airy hosts turn still thy gilded vanes; What winds of elf that with grey ghosts’ 

people thine ancient lanes!”—Lovecraft possessed a nostalgic idealism for the city’s prestigious 

past.1  Located at the northern point of Narragansett Bay, Providence was, by the late 19th century,  

a thriving city with paved streets, electric streetcars, and a population of just over 136,000.  The 

local economy was rooted in textiles and the manufacturing of steam engines, silverware, and 

jewelry. The downtown, comprised of Washington, Westminster, Weybosset, and Benefit streets, 

boasted one of the highest concentrations of colonial buildings in the country.  Cultural attractions, 

meanwhile, included the First Baptist Church in America (organized by Roger Williams in the 

1630s), Brown University (founded in 1764 and housing the Ladd astronomical observatory), and 

the Providence Athenaeum (established in 1836 and frequented by Edgar Allan Poe, during his 

short courtship with Sarah Helen Whitman). Ultimately, Providence was, FWP state guide noted 

in 1937, “a little unusual because it contains the widespread wealth of a diversified-industry city 

plus the concentrated wealth of a single-industry town.”2   “When I was 3 years old,” Lovecraft 

later recalled, “I felt a strange magic & fascination (not unmixed with a vague unease & perhaps 

a touch of fear) in the ancient houses of Providence’s venerable hill…, with their fan lighted 

doorways, railed flights of steps, & a cow pastured under the gardeners care.”3  

The son of Winfield Scott Lovecraft, a traveling salesman, and Sarah Phillips, the daughter 

of a wealthy landowner and businessman, Lovecraft experienced a relatively financially stable, 

middle-class upbringing during his early years.  According to one scholar, however, “He was 

 1 H.P. Lovecraft “Providence.” in Joshi, ed. The Ancient Track: The Complete Poetical Works of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 304.  2 Federal Writers Project. Rhode Island: A Guide to the Smallest State. 254, (Boston, Houghton Mifflin Company, 1937).  3 S.T. Joshi. I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 29. 

                                                                                                                       123 

unfortunate in his parents”—his mother “an overprotected, genteel, if attractive girl, ill-equipped 

to deal with the world,” and his father mentally troubled.4  Indeed, in the early 1890s, Winfield, 

following a “psychotic episode” in a Chicago hotel, was committed to an asylum where he died of 

late-stage syphilis in 1898.5  “In April 1893 my father was stricken with a complete paralysis 

resulting from a brain overtaxed with study & business cares,” Lovecraft later wrote.  “He lived 

for five years in a hospital but was never again able to move hand or foot, or to utter a sound.”6  

Lovecraft’s mother, Sarah, meanwhile—as fiction writer and Lovecraft friend August Derleth 

insisted—was possessive and protective, and “visited all unwittingly upon her son the resentment 

she must have felt against her husband, by convincing Howard that he was an ‘ugly-looking’ boy, 

though he was not at all unhandsome.”7   

Following his father’s hospitalization and death, Lovecraft lived in the home of his 

wealthy maternal grandparents Whipple and Robie Phillips. Sheltered by his mother, and plagued 

with frequent bouts with illness, Lovecraft, as a child, “lived a relatively lonely and reclusive 

life.”8  Unsurprisingly, Lovecraft was soon drawn to his grandfather’s extensive library, which 

included books on science and astronomy, as well as the great works of literature. It was 

Lovecraft’s exposure to his grandfather Whipple’s library, then, which first attracted him to 

 4 H.P. Lovecraft “H.P. Lovecraft and His Work.” The Dunwich Horror And Others: The Best Supernatural Stories of H.P Lovecraft. Selected and with an Introduction by August Derleth. (Sauk City: Arkham House Publishers, 1963), ix. 5 Winfield Lovecraft was supposedly on a sales trip in Chicago for the Gorham Company. S.T. Joshi. I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 25.  6 Kenneth W. Faig Jr. The Parents of Howard Philps Lovecraft. (West Warwick:  Necronomicon Press, 1990), 23.  7 H.P. Lovecraft “H.P. Lovecraft and His Work.” The Dunwich Horror And Others: The Best Supernatural Stories of H.P Lovecraft. Selected and with an Introduction by August Derleth. (Sauk City: Arkham House Publishers, 1963), ix. 8Ibid.,  ix. 

                                                                                                                       124 

literary pursuits, and ultimately proved a tremendous influence on his own writing style.  “My 

beloved grandfather,” Lovecraft later wrote, “…became the Centre of my universe.”9 

 Lovecraft’s love of literature, then, began early in his formative years. As a youth, he 

read everything from Cicero to the Brothers Grimm to works based on the temperance movement 

such as John B. Gough’s Sunshine and Shadow (1880).10 It was the sciences, however, that first 

piqued his interest at a future occupation. “The science of chemistry… first captivated me… in a 

peculiar way,” Lovecraft later recalled.  “With the insatiable curiosity of early childhood, I used 

to spend hours poring over the picture in back of Webster’s Unabridged Dictionary-absorbing a 

miscellaneous variety of ideas. After familiarizing myself with antiquities, medieval dress and 

armor, birds, animals, reptiles, fishes, flags of all nations, heraldry, etc. etc., I lit upon the section 

devoted to ‘philosophical and Scientific Instruments,’ and was veritably hypnotized with it. 

Chemical apparatus especially attracted me, and I was resolved (before knowing a thing about 

the science!) to have a laboratory.”11  He also enjoyed reading the detective stories of Arthur 

Conan Doyle. “As to Sherlock Holmes,” Lovecraft wrote a friend in 1918, “I used to be 

infatuated with him! I read every Sherlock Holmes story published.”12  

As early 1898, when he was only eight years old, Lovecraft began to write juvenilia, 

specifically working over the detective genre found in many of the dime store novels of the time.  

Still, much of this early work—including short stories such as “The Secret Cave” (1898) and 

“The Mystery of the Grave-Yard” (1898)—was morbid in nature.  In “The Secret Cave,” for 

example, two young children, John and Alice Lee, left at home alone discover a secret cavern 

 9  S.T. Joshi. I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 28. 10 Lovecraft to Rheinhart Kleiner, November 16, 1916, in Joshi & Schutlz, eds., Lord of a Visible World: An Autobiography In Letters. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2019), 31.  11 Lovecraft to Alfred Galpin, August 29, 1918, in Ibid.,  40.  12 Lovecraft to Alfred Galpin, May 27, 1918, in Ibid.,  41.  

                                                                                                                       125 

behind their basement wall.  In exploring the cave, the two children find a “small box,” and 

accidentally start a flood, resulting in the death of Alice.  After his sister’s funeral, John—in the 

las sentence of the story-- remembered the box, and upon opening it, found “a solid gold chunk 

worth about $10,000 [--] enough to pay for any thing [sic] but the death of his sister.”13  During 

this same time, Lovecraft began—as a part of the amateur journalism movement that was 

popular in the late nineteenth century—to write amateur journals on chemistry and astronomy.     

Lovecraft’s early childhood seemed idyllic with a loving grandfather and a library filled 

with both fantasies and facts.  However, tragedy, a dark passenger that roamed through much of 

Lovecraft’s life, soon struck again. In 1904, when Lovecraft was fourteen, his Grandfather 

Whipple—beset by financial problems with his Owyhee Land and Irrigation company—died 

suddenly. “His death,” Lovecraft later wrote, “bought financial disaster beside its more serious 

grief. With his passing the rest of the board lost their initiative and courage. The corporation was 

unwisely dissolved at a time when my grandfather would have preserved-with the result that 

others reaped the wealth which should have gone to its stockholders. My mother & I was forced 

to vacate the beautiful estates at 454 Angell Street, 7 to enter the less spacious abode at 598, 

three squares eastward.”14  

For a child raised in relative luxury, the loss of significant income—not to mention father 

figure-- was traumatizing. “[F]or the first time I knew what a congested, servant less home… 

was,” Lovecraft recalled.  “I felt that I had lost my entire adjustment to the cosmos… [and] It 

seemed like a damn futile business to keep on living… Oh, hell! Why not slough off 

 13 The story was first published by Arkham House in a 1959 Lovecraft collection entitled The Shuttered Room and Other Pieces.  https://www.hplovecraft.com/writings/texts/fiction/sc.aspx 14 S.T. Joshi. I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 96. 

                                                                                                                       126 

consciousness all together.”15  Gone, then, were his private tutors and “free & easy” lifestyle.16  

In their place was a public high school and serious financial constraints.  It is obvious that he was 

traumatized by this loss, and throughout his life—and even in his own writings—he remained a 

searcher, seeking and venerating a lost and idyllic past.  Unsurprisingly too was his drift toward 

dark themes and horror writing.17   

Lovecraft seemed to enjoy public high school at first despite his adverse position. He 

later reminisced that “I always liked school, although I didn’t fit in with the discipline overly 

well during the fragmentary grammar-school snatches. In high school, however, I never realized 

there was such a thing as discipline. The teachers – a goodly percentage of them men – took the 

position that pupils were near enough to responsible adulthood to be treated as adults, and in 

return for this complement the vast majority – including myself – reciprocated by behaving like 

adults.”18  Then, in 1908, Lovecraft, aged eighteen, experienced a “breakdown” of some nature.  

“Many times in my youth,” he later wrote, “I was so exhausted by the sheer burden of 

consciousness and mental and physical activity that I had to drop out a school for a greater or 

lesser period and taker a complete rest free from all responsibilities.” He “could hardly bear to 

see or speak to anyone, and… liked to shut out the world by pulling down dark shades and using 

 15 Ibid., 96-97. 16 Ibid. 17 The Lovecrafts were not destitute, however.  As late as 1921, he still had $20,000 left from his grandfather’s inheritance. The problem—according to Lovecraft’s wife, Sonia Greene, was that “it was supposed to last the rest of the lives of his two aunts and himself.”  Sonia Davis, “Lovecraft As I Knew Him,” in in August Derleth, ed., Something About Cats and Other Pieces. (Freeport, New York: Books for Libraries Press, 1971), 237. 18 Lovecraft to Robert E. Howard, March 25-29, 1933. in Joshi & Schultz, eds., A Means to Freedom: The Letters of H.P. Lovecraft and Robert E. Howard. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2017), 582. 

                                                                                                                       127 

artificial light.”19   Unfortunately, Lovecraft’s transcripts have not survived, but apparently he did 

not receive his diploma, and ultimately “had to forgo college…”20  

As Lovecraft withdrew from social life, two parts of his personality began to emerge.  

One was his growing racism.  Lovecraft was no doubt impacted by his own environment.  

Providence, after all, was a relatively small and isolated--and mostly white and Protestant-- city.   

He had no interaction with the works of W.E.B. Du Bois, or later, those of the Harlem 

Renaissance.  Indeed, Lovecraft himself later observed to a friend, “I hardly wonder that my 

racial ideas seem bigoted to one born and reared in the vicinity of cosmopolitan New York, but 

you may better understand my repulsion to the jew [sic] when I tell you that until I was fourteen 

years old I do not believe I ever spoke to one so or saw one knowingly.”21 The dawn of the 

twentieth century was also marked by a “Lost Cause” romanticism in both fiction and non-

fiction.  Overall, this literature venerated America’s agricultural past, ignored the brutality and 

injustice of slavery, and often portrayed black males as ignorant, incompetent, and even sexually 

aggressive.  It was also a time marked by bigotry against Jews, Catholics, and Asians.    

Much of Lovecraft’s early racism was against African Americans, a belief that by aged 

fifteen he had already shown in his juvenile writing and that was shared by much of his family 

and friends.22 Some of Lovecraft’s early poems contained these racist sentiments.  For example, 

a poem like “New-England Fallen” (1912) contained lines such as “The Village rings with tribal 

foreign cries; Around the wine-shops loaf with bleary eyes. A vicious crew, that mock the name 

 19 Lovecraft to R.H. Barlow, April 10, 1934, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., O Fortunate Floridian: H.P. Lovecraft’s Letters to R.H. Barlow. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2016), 125. 20 Ibid.; S.T. Joshi. I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 102. 21Lovecraft to Rheinhart Kleiner, December 6, 1915, in Joshi & Schutlz, eds., Lord of a Visible World: An Autobiography In Letters. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2019), 96.  22 S.T. Joshi. I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 112-113. 

                                                                                                                       128 

of the “man” Yet dare to call themselves Americans.”23 Other poems such as the awful “On the 

Creation of Niggers” (1912) were self-explanatory in title. Of course, Lovecraft was not alone in 

his racist views.  In 1905, the novel The Clansman, by Thomas Dixon, was met with popular 

acclaim, and later became the basis for the 1915 Hollywood blockbuster film, The Birth of a 

Nation. The film, in turn, inspired the revival of the Ku Klux Klan, which now expanded its 

message of hate to include Jews, Catholics, immigrants, and urban “elites.”  Indeed, the Klan of 

the early twentieth century insisted that it was protecting traditional womanhood, Protestantism, 

and rural values from the scourge of “modernity.”  Lovecraft, then, often identified with the 

racial conservatives of the time.  Many of these conservative ideals ran counter to the second key 

component of his early intellectual development—i.e. his modernist views on science and 

religion.  

 Meanwhile, Lovecraft’s interests in science found their way into his first fully formed 

literary work, “The Beast in the Cave” (1905), when he was only fifteen.24 While the story was 

simple in plot in execution, the atmosphere that became Lovecraft’s signature was well on its 

way to fruition. The premise was that a man, separated from his guide while touring Mammoth 

Cave, inadvertently summoned a “beast” with his cries for help.  Alone and unable to see in the 

darkness, the man threw rocks in the direction of the noise of the “beast.”  The man then fled and 

was ultimately reunited with his guide.  After leading the guide back to the spot where he “felt” 

the beast, it was revealed that it was not a beast at all but a man who had been lost in the cave for 

 23 H.P. Lovecraft. “New England Fallen” The Ancient Track: The Complete Poetical Works of H.P. Lovecraft. in Joshi, ed.  (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 385-388. 24 “The Beast in the Cave” though written in 1905, would eventually be published in the amateur journal The Vagrant in 1918. Though to it finding publication eventually, it stands among Lovecraft’s first non-juvenile works.  

                                                                                                                       129 

years. For a teenager, the story was quite impressive, and it eventually found publication in an 

amateur magazine almost a decade later.    

 Lovecraft wrote one other early short story before his emotional breakdown in 1908, 

“The Alchemist” (1908).  Eventually published in United Amateur magazine in 1916, “The 

Alchemist” told the story of a French count named Antoine whose ancestor had been responsible 

for the death of a dark sorcerer hundreds of years earlier.  In retaliation, the sorcerer’s son, 

Charles le Sorcier, placed a curse on all the perpetrator’s descendants, causing them to die at the 

age of thirty-two.  As his own thirty-second birthday approached, Antonie—alone without any 

family--explored the castle in a desperate attempt for answers.  Soon he discovered a trap door 

and a tunnel.  There, in the passageway, he was attacked by a man, whom he killed.   The man 

was none other than Charles le Sorcier, who had used the elixir of life to complete his curse.  

 “The Alchemist” proved to mark the end of Lovecraft’s childhood and the beginning of 

his nearly eight-year hiatus from fiction writing and his nearly five-year hiatus from social life. 

Sadly, not much is known about Lovecraft’s activities during this time.  Lovecraft himself, 

though, later reflected that “I shunned all human society, deeming myself too much of a failure in 

life to be seen socially by those who had known me in my youth, and had foolishly expected 

such great things of me, from then to now [1916], I have been practically unknown to save a very 

few old acquaintances. I am a complete disappointment, having accomplished absolutely nothing 

during my 26 futile years of existence.”25 Lovecraft never left much of an explanation to his 

feelings beyond the disappointment in his lack of an academic career.  His mother, however, may 

have played a role. Indeed, one family friend recalled, “My call [to the Lovecraft home] was 

 25 Lovecraft to Rheinhart Kleiner, December 4, 1918, in Joshi & Schutlz, eds., Lord of a Visible World: An Autobiography In Letters. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2019), 65. 

                                                                                                                       130 

pleasant enough, but the house had a strange and shut up air and the atmosphere seemed weird, 

and Mrs. Lovecraft talked continuously of her unfortunate son who was so hideous that he hid 

from everyone and did not like to walk upon the streets where people can gaze at him. When I 

protested that she was exaggerating and that he should not feel that way, she looked at me with a 

rather pitiful look as though I did not understand about it. I remember that I was glad to get out 

in the fresh air and sunshine and that I did not repeat my visit.”26 Lovecraft’s mother seemed on 

the verge, then, of her own mental break, which left young Lovecraft without a support system. 

This atmosphere more than likely remained this way during his five-year self-imposed exile. It 

was, however, the act of reading literature that led him out of exile and gave him meaning and 

purpose as an adult.  Across the 1910s and 1920s he encountered the works of classic, gothic 

works, as well as newer, “weird tales.” 

 Between 1911 and 1924, there were three events that significantly shaped Lovecraft’s 

writing career.  The first was his debate in the pulp magazine The Argosy over the literary 

abilities of popular writer Fred Jackson (1886-1953).  As early as 1911, Lovecraft began reading 

The Argosy and writing letters to the editor.  Then, in 1913, he began writing the editor 

complaining of the works of Jackson.  Now largely forgotten, Jackson was a relatively popular 

and diverse author in the early twentieth century—writing novels, short stories, plays, musicals, 

and even screenplays.  Most of his works were either romances or comedies—such as his 1910 

novel, The Hidden Princess: A Modern Romance—but he did venture some into science fiction, 

fantasy, and mystery (especially with his short stories).  Many of these short stories were 

published in pulps such as The Argosy and Detective Story Magazine.27   

 26 S.T. Joshi. I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 131. 27 Ibid., 140. 

                                                                                                                       131 

According to Lovecraft, Jackson’s writings were “trivial, effeminate, and, in places, 

coarse.”28 Indeed, Lovecraft was appalled by what he viewed as race mixing.   “Into the breasts 

of characters, and appearing to dominate them to the exclusion of reason, [Jackson] places the 

delicate passions and emotions proper to negroes or arthropod apes,” Lovecraft wrote on one 

occasion.  Furthermore, he insisted, Jackson’s “literary style is feeble and often excessively 

familiar. He abounds in “split” infinitives and occasionally falls into the use out of outlandish 

words, as, for instance, “live-in-able,” instead of “habitable.”29  All of this “coarseness,” he 

claimed, was being thrust upon readers by design. “To the eye of a disinterested observer,” he 

railed in September 1913, “it appears as though an effort were being made to obtrude Mr. 

Jackson upon the reading public by an exampled [sic] campaign of advertising.”30  Ultimately, 

Lovecraft’s letters caused a yearlong tirade of letter writing in The Argosy among several 

readers, including John Russell and T.P. Crean.  The young men often debated each other  in the 

“Letters” section with poetry.  For example, on one occasion Russell wrote,  “Does Mr. 

Lovecraft think it wise/ with such long words to criticize/ an author whom we greatly prize? 

That’s Fred Jackson.”31 Lovecraft responded with “In truth, my words are not beyond the 

reach/of him who understands the English Speech;/ But Crean, I fear, by reading Jackson long, 

Hath lost the pow’r to read his mother tongue.”32  

Lovecraft’s letters quickly caught the attention of other readers. One such reader was 

Edward F. Dass, an amateur journalist from Milwaukee, Wisconsin who also happened to be the 

former president and official editor of the United Amateur Press Association (UAPA). Dass was 

 28 Ibid., 145. 29 Ibid. 30 Ibid., 144. 31 Ibid., 145. 32 Ibid., 146. 

                                                                                                                       132 

impressed with the work of Lovecraft and Russell’s public correspondence and invited them both 

to join the UAPA organization in 1914. “I entered Amateur Journalism late in life-- at the age of 

nearly twenty-four-- so that I cannot justly attribute all my education to its influence,” Lovecraft 

later recalled.  “This lateness was, however, most emphatically not of my choosing…  I spent the 

first few months in an attempt to discover just what Amateur Journalism is and just what it is not. 

My notions had been rather nebulous, and I was not sure whether delight or disillusion awaited 

me.”  Lovecraft found both.  Yet, “delight,” he added, “was so much in the majority that I soon 

realized I was a permanent amateur. That was in 1914. In 1921 I can report unchanged 

sentiments.”33  Ultimately, it was amateur journalism that connected Lovecraft to other writers 

(including Rheinhart Kleiner and Robert E. Howard) as well as to provide a platform for his first 

published works in the weird literature tradition. 

Lovecraft, then, found his home in amateur journalism.  In the fall of 1914—coinciding 

with the outbreak of the First World War (1914-1919) -- he was appointed chairman of the 

Department of Public Criticism, and in early 1915, he launched his own amateur journal The 

Conservative (1915-1923).  Overall, The Conservative contained both literary criticisms as well 

as political and moral pieces that were mostly “conservative” in nature.  In this case, however, 

“conservative” was not meant to necessarily reflect a political ideology, but a literary persuasion.  

Indeed, in the second issue of the journal Lovecraft explained to readers “his journalistic policy,” 

and “forecast of … future endeavors.”34 According to Lovecraft, then, the main goal of the 

journal was to advance the “traditional,” rhyme and meter styles of the eighteenth century (as 

expressed by Alexaner Pope and John Milton).  “The increasing use among us of slovenly prose 

 33 H.P. Lovecraft. “What Amateuerdom and I Have Done For Each Other” Collected Essays: Volume 1: Amateur Journalism. in Joshi, ed. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004), 271-272.  34 H.P. Lovecraft. “Editorial (July 1915). Collected Essays: Volume 1: Amateur Journalism. in Joshi, ed. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004), 51. 

                                                                                                                       133 

and lame meter,” he wrote, “…demands an active opponent, even though a lone one; and the 

profound reverence of The Conservative for the polished writers of a more correct age fits him 

for a task to which his mediocre talent might not otherwise recommend him.”35   

Most certainly, Lovecraft was a traditionalist when it came to his love of literature—and 

especially his devotion to the arts of the eighteenth century (including literature, architecture, and 

history).  “Our age,” Lovecraft wrote in an essay in 1922, “is in one of curious transition and 

divergence, with an increasing separation of art from the past and from all common life as well. 

Modern science has in the end, proved an enemy to art and pleasure; for by revealing to us the 

whole sordid and prosaic basis of our thoughts, motives, and acts, it has stripped the glamour, 

wonder, and all those illusions of heroism, nobility, and sacrifice which used to sound so 

impressive when romantically treated.”36 According to Lovecraft’s modern biographer, S.T. Joshi, 

“Lovecraft utilized poetry not for aesthetic but for psychological ends: as a means of tricking 

himself into believing the eighteenth century still existed-- or, at the very least, that he was a 

product of the eighteenth century who has somehow been transported into alien and repulsive 

era.”37 Unfortunately, this limited much of Lovecraft’s work in poetry to eighteenth century 

pastiche until he began to write “weird” poetry as the decade ended. “Take the form away, and 

nothing remains,” Lovecraft acknowledged in 1914.  “I have no real poetic ability, and all that 

saves my verse from utter worthlessness is the care which I bestow on its metrical construction.”38  

Beyond its promotion of eighteenth-century literary style, The Conservative also reflected 

Lovecraft’s positions on several other American home front issues, including race, prohibition, 

 35 Ibid. 36 H.P. Lovecraft. “Lord Dunsany and His Work” Collected Essays: Volume 2: Literary Criticism. in Joshi. ed. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004), 60. 37 S.T. Joshi. I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 192. 38 Ibid., 191. 

                                                                                                                       134 

military preparedness, and government.  Indeed, “Outside the domain of the pure literature,” 

Lovecraft explained, “The Conservative will ever be found an enthusiastic champion of total 

abstinence and prohibition; of moderate, healthy militarism as contrasted with dangerous and 

unpatriotic peace-preaching; of pan-saxonism [sic], or the domination by the English and kindred 

races over the lesser divisions of mankind; and of constitutional and representative government, 

as opposed to the pernicious and contemptible false scheme of anarchy and socialism.”39 At heart, 

Lovecraft was very traditional and sheltered—living in a small room lined with shelves of old 

books and “small pictures of Robert E. Lee, Jefferson Davis, and one or two others.”40  He rarely 

left Providence—perhaps partially out of financial constraints--  and was “carefully self-schooled 

in the correct eighteenth century code of conduct…”41  His wife, Sonia Greene later recalled that 

not long after she met Lovecraft (in 1921) the two went to an Italian restaurant in Cleveland.  “It 

was the first time Howard had ever been in an Italian restaurant (he was then in his early thirties), 

the first time he had ever eaten minestrone or spaghetti with meat and tomato sauce and Parmesan 

cheese,” Greene wrote.  “He balked at wine.  He said he never had tasted alcohol and didn’t wish 

to begin now.”42   Ironically, then, for a man who possessed such an expansive view of the universe 

and of worlds imagined, he himself was narrow-minded, provincial, and bigoted.   

It was during this period too that Lovecraft—“egged” on by amateur editor and critic W. 

Paul Cook-- returned to writing short stories. 43  In 1917, he wrote “two yarns” that demonstrated 

 39 H.P. Lovecraft. “Editorial (July 1915). Collected Essays: Volume 1: Amateur Journalism. in Joshi, ed. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004), 51. 40 Rheinhart Kleiner, “A Memoir of Lovecraft,” in August Derleth, ed., Something About Cats and Other Pieces.  (Freeport, New York: Books for Libraries Press, 1971), 219. 41 Ibid., 218. 42 Sonia Davis, “Lovecraft As I Knew Him,” in August Derleth, ed., Something About Cats and Other Pieces.  (Freeport, New York: Books for Libraries Press, 1971), 235. 43 S .T. Joshi. I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 239. 

                                                                                                                       135 

his developing style—and most certainly the influence of Poe.44  One was “The Tomb” (later 

published in The Vagrant magazine in 1922).  “My narrative pen was a very rusty-- believe me 

boys, very rusty indeed!” Lovecraft later explained.  “To drop back into the forms of fiction was 

exceedingly hard after nine quiescent years, and I feared that the result would be the limit of 

absurdity. But the spell of the gruesome was upon me, and I finally hammered out the hideous tale 

of Jervas Dudley. At last-a Poe again!”45  

“The Tomb” was a story told by the narrator Jervas Dudley, who confessed at the very 

beginning that “In relating the circumstances which has led to my confinement within this refuge 

for the demented, I am aware that my present position will create a natural doubt of the authenticity 

of my narrative.”46 From his earliest childhood Dudley had been “a dreamer and visionary” and 

“dwelt apart from the visible world.”47  One day in his youth, while walking through “a singular 

wooded hollow” near his home, he stumbled upon “a half-hidden house of death”-- a tomb for the 

Hyde family, who had all perished long ago in a fire caused by lighting and rumored to be the 

victim of a “divine wrath.”48  While Jervas did not have the ability to open the door, he became 

obsessed with the tomb (and its contents).   One night—while sleeping outside of the tomb – Jervas, 

now aged twenty-one, was wakened by the sound of ancient colonial voices and the appearance of 

a “light [that] had been hurriedly extinguished within the sunken sepulcher.”  Upon returning 

home, he “went with much directness to a rotting chest in the attic” and found a key “which next 

day unlocked with ease the barrier I had so long stormed in vain.”49   

 44 Ibid. 45 Lovecraft to the Kleicomolo, April 1820, in Joshi & Schutlz, eds., Lord of a Visible World: An Autobiography In Letters. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2019), 101. 46 Lovecraft, “The Tomb,” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction., 14. 47 Ibid. 48 Ibid., 15. 49 Ibid., 18. 

                                                                                                                       136 

Shortly thereafter—“in the soft glow of late afternoon”—Jervas entered the tomb. “A spell 

was upon me, and my heart leaped with an exultation I can but ill describe… I seemed to know 

the way; and though the candle sputtered with the stifling reek of the place, I felt singularly at 

home in the musty, charnel-house air.”  Inside the “dank portal” he saw marble slabs and coffins 

and the remains of coffins.  In one alcove he found a “fairly well-preserved and untenanted casket, 

adorned with a single name which to me both a smile and a shudder.  An odd impulse caused me 

to climb upon the broad slab, extinguish my candle, and lie down within the vacant box…  

Henceforth I haunted the tomb each night; seeing, hearing, and doing things I must never reveal.”50  

Subsequently, Jervas’s behavior became quite peculiar.  His speech and personality were 

significantly altered, and he obtained “an unspeakable horror” of fire and thunderstorms.  The 

prospect of being “[b]urnt alive to my ashes, my body dispersed by the four winds [ and that] I 

might never lie in tomb of Hydes!” filled him with fear.  Afterall, “Was not my coffin prepared for 

me?”51  Ultimately, Jervas was admitted to an insane asylum, where over time he began to doubt 

whether he had ever entered the tomb at all.  The story ended sometime later with a faithful old 

servant, Hiram, entering the tomb and finding it as Jervas had recounted. “On a slab in an alcove 

he found an empty coffin whose tarnished plate bears the single word ‘Jervas’”52  

The other “yarn” from 1917 was “Dagon,” later published in The Vagrant in late 1919, and 

again in 1923 in Weird Tales. Unlike “The Tomb,” “Dagon” looked toward the future. “I had been 

reading Poe again--for about the ten millionth time,” Lovecraft recounted. “The new or rather 

revived mood was hard to dismiss, and after limbering up my style a bit with practice work, I 

perpetrated ‘Dagon’ in August. To me it seemed better than ‘The Tomb’-- smoother, less halting 

 50 Ibid. 51 Ibid., 21. 52 Ibid., 22. 

                                                                                                                       137 

and angular. I felt that my practice had done me a bit of good.”53  Overall, the story was told by an 

unnamed narrator – a sailor-- who was “writing under an appreciable mental strain, since by tonight 

I shall be no more.”54  As with Poe’s stories, one modern historian observed, “it is the narrator’s 

emotional trauma that is at the heart of the tale.”55  Having avoided capture following the seizure 

of his vessel by a German sea-raider, he was adrift in the Pacific.  He soon found “landfall,”— 

actually a “slimy expanse of hellish black mire which extended about me… as far as I could see.”  

There he discovered an ancient monument on which was inscribed “a system of hieroglyphics 

unknown to me, and unlike anything I have seen in books; consisting for the most part of 

conventionalized aquatic symbols such as fishes, eels, octopi, crustaceans, mollusks, whales, and 

the like.”56  Then, a monster lurking under the slime appeared.  “With only a light churning to mark 

its rise to the surface, the thing slid into view above the dark waters. Vast, Polyphemus-like and 

loathsome, it darted like a stupendous monster of nightmares to the monolith, about which it flung 

its gigantic scaly arms, the while it bowed its hideous head and gave vent to certain measures 

sounds. I think I went mad then.”57  

Later, the narrator awoke in a San Fransico hospital with very little memory of how he 

arrived there.  He had been rescued by an American vessel, and in his delirium “had said much [to 

the captain], but found my words had been given scant attention.”  While he recuperated, the 

narrator was still haunted by the Philistine legend of Dagon, the Fish-God.  “It is at night, especially 

when the moon is gibbous and waning, that I see the thing.  I tried morphine; but the drug has 

given only transient surcease, and has drawn me into its clutches as a hopeless slave…  The end is 

 53 Lovecraft to the Kleicomolo, April 1820, in Joshi & Schutlz, eds., Lord of a Visible World: An Autobiography In Letters. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2019), 103. 54 Lovecraft, “Dagon,” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 23. 55 Ibid. 56 Ibid., 25-26. 57 Ibid., 26. 

                                                                                                                       138 

near I hear a noise at the door, as of some immense slippery body lumbering against it. It shall not 

find me. God, that hand! The window! The window!”58  According to Lovecraft scholar, S.T. Joshi, 

many of the stories features—including “the narrator’s psychological devastation at the monster’s 

mere existence”—foreshadowed “The Call of Cthulhu” and other, “later stories where the 

supernatural has been replaced by the scientifically plausible.”59 

These stories ushed in, then, he first and earliest phase of his literary career.  Lasting from 

the late 1910s to the late 1920s, it was one marked by “pure horror” and included much of the 

“fantastic, in the pattern of Lord Dunsany.”60 It was during this period, then, that Lovecraft 

composed “some of the weirdest & darkest fiction ever written by man!”61 Most of these “weird 

and terrible tales” were set in New England (and often in Providence itself), and included short 

stories like “The Cats of Ulthar” (1920),  “The Terrible Old Man” (1921), “The Picture in the 

House” (1921), “The Rats in the Walls” (1924), “The Shunned House” (1924), and “The Outsider” 

(1926).  Indeed, Lovecraft’s reputation as a weaver of horror tales was established with these early 

works.  For example, “The Cats of Ulthar” was rooted in a simple but “ghastly plot”--  all the cats 

in town took vengeance upon an old and hateful couple who enjoyed killing cats. 62  Meanwhile, 

in “The Shunned House”—based on an actual house in Providence at 135 Benefit Street— 

Lovecraft’s narrator traced the dark history of the abandoned structure, and all the tragedies 

 58 Ibid., 27. 59 Ibid., 23. Many of these early stories, including “Dagon,” “The Statement of Randolph Carter” (1920), “The Cats of Ulthar” (1921), and “The Hound” (1922) were submitted in hand-written form to publishers.  Unsurprisingly, some were reluctant to accept them. Indeed, writing a friend in the summer of 1923, Lovecraft noted that the editor of Weird Tales “liked [his stories],but could not consider their acceptance till I sent them in double-spaced typing.”  Lovecraft confessed that he was unsure if he would bother.  “I need the money badly enough,” he wrote, “but ugh! how I hate typing!... I abhor labour [sic].” Lovecraft to Frank Belknap Long, May 13, 1923, in Durleth and Wandrei, eds., H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1911-1924, 227. 60 August Derleth, “Introduction,” in Lovecraft, The Dunwich Horror and Other Stories (Sauk City, Wisconsin: Arkham House Publishers, 19630, xiii. 61 Lovecraft to Reinhardt Kleiner, November 16, 1916, in Durleth and Wandrei, eds., Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1911-1924, 41. 62 Lovecraft to Reinhardt Kleiner, May 21, 1920, in Ibid., 117. 

                                                                                                                       139 

surrounding its previous occupants.63  Then, in “The Outsider,” the narrator—uncertain about his 

identity or past—climbed through a passageway and into a new land, where after a few hours, he
