came upon “a venerable ivied castle… gorgeously ablaze with light and sending forth sound of the 

gayest revelry.”64  When the narrator walked into the abode to join in the festivities, those 

assembled shrieked and fled in fear.  Initially confused, the narrator saw what he believed to be a 

hideous monster.  Then, “in one cataclysmic second of cosmic nightmarishness and hellish 

accident,” he realized he was seeing his own reflection.65 

Without question, Lovecraft was heavily influenced by the writings of Edgar Allan Poe.  

Indeed, Lovecraft admired the early nineteenth century writer—calling himself “a close disciple 

of Poe & diligent delver into the regions of the ‘grotesque & arabesque…’”66   According to 

Lovecraft, “Poe saw beyond the vulgar anthropocentric sphere, and realized that men are only 

puppets; that events and circumstances are only the vital things.”67  To Lovecraft, then, Poe was 

“the apex of fantastic art—there was in him a vast and cosmic vision which no imitator has been 

able to parallel.”68 Poe’s influence on Lovecraft, then, was tremendous—especially in his early 

works, such as “The Music of Erich Zann” (1922) and “The Rats in the Walls” (1924)—both of 

which included old cryptic houses, unreliable narrators, and unexplainable occurrences.  Published 

 63 Lovecraft was familiar with house in Providence because his Aunt Lillian Clark lived there.  While the house in his story was the one on Benefit Street in Providence, he was inspired by a house he saw in Elizabeth, New Jersey during a trip in late 1924.  On the corner of Elizabeth Avenue and Bridge Street he wrote his aunt that November, there was “a terrible old house—a hellish place where night-black deeds must have been done in the early seventeen hundreds… It reminded me of the Babbit House in Benefit Street… Later its image came up again with renewed vividness, finally causing me to write a new horror story with its scene in Providence and with the Babbit House as its basis.”  Lovecraft to Lillian Clark, November 6, 1924, in Durleth and Wandrei, eds., Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1911-1924, 357.      64 Lovecraft, “The Outsider,” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 167. 65 Ibid.,168.  With “The Outsider,” Lovecraft again demonstrated the impact of Mary Shelley.   66 Lovecraft to Reinhardt Kleiner, November 16, 1916, in Durleth and Wandrei, eds., Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1911-1924, 41. 67 Lovecraft to Frank Belknap Long, June 4, 1921, in Ibid., 137. 68 Lovecraft to Frank Belknap Long, May 3, 1922, in Ibid., 173. 

                                                                                                                       140 

in the National Amateur in March 1922, “The Music of Erich Zann” was one of Lovecraft’s 

favorite stories—“chiefly because of the subtlety and restraint of its supernatural 

manifestations.”69 Indeed, in 1933, Lovecraft wrote a short autobiography for the magazine 

Unusual Tales under the title “Some Notes on a Nonentity” in which he insisted that “The Music 

of Erich Zann”—alongside “The Colour of Space”—were favorites.  “I doubt,” Lovecraft wrote, 

“if I could ever succeed well in the ordinary kind of Science Fiction.”70  The story, he believed, 

“isn’t as bad as most of the rest [of my stories]. I like it for what it hasn’t more than for what it 

has.”71  While rather cosmic in nature, “The Music of Erich Zann” reflected Poe’s influence, 

especially as it related to the fragility of sanity.  

“The Music of Erich Zann” told the tale of an unnamed narrator who—despite examining 

“maps of the city with the greatest care, yet has never again found the Rue d’Auseil,” the home 

apartment house in which “during the last months of my improvised life as a student of 

metaphysics at the university, I heard the music of Erich Zann.”72  In the apartment above the 

narrator heard strange music, and after an inquiry, learned the music was played by Erich Zann-- 

a man who chooses to live on the top floor with a single window in order to play his music through 

the night. The narrator was “haunted by the weirdness of his music. Knowing little of the art 

myself, I was yet certain that none of his harmonies had any relation to music I had heard before; 

and concluded that he was a composer of highly original genius.”73  Resolved to meet Zann, the 

narrator learned that the musician was elderly and mute and possessed a nervous temperament.  

 69 S.T. Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction (New York: Barnes & Noble, Inc., 2008), 174. 70 H.P. Lovecraft. “Some Notes on a Nonentity” Collected Essays: Volume 5: Philosophy: Autobiography & Miscellany. in Joshi, ed. New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004, 210. 71 S.T. Joshi. I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013, 416. 72 Lovecraft, “The Music of Erich Zahn,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 174. 73 Ibid., 175. 

                                                                                                                       141 

One night while outside Zann’s door the narrator heard “the shrieking viol swell into a 

chaotic babel of sound; a pandemonium which would have led me to doubt my own shaking sanity 

had their not come from behind that barred portal a piteous proof that the horror was real-the awful 

immaculate cry which only a mute can utter, and which rises only in moments of the most terrible 

fear and aguish.”74 The narrator, then, knocked on the door, and Zann answered, writing a note that  

“implored for the sake of me in the name of mercy, and the sake of my own curiosity, to wait where 

I was while he prepared a full account in German of all the marvels and the terrors which beset 

him.”75 Nearly an hour later, Zann’s face was “stark fear. He was trying to make a noise’ to ward 

something off or drown something out-- what I could not imagine awesome though I thought it 

might be.”76 His playing became more violent, and the Zann’s one window was broken by a 

shudder.  A sudden gust of wind blew Zann’s manuscript toward the window, and as the narrator 

attempted to retrieve it, he “saw no city spread below, and no friendly lights gleaming from 

remembered streets, but only the blackness of space illimitable; unimagined space alive with 

motion and music, and having no semblance to anything on earth.”77 The narrator then turned, and 

found Zann, “his ice-cold, stiffened unbreathing face, whose glassy eyes bulged usually into the 

void.”78   Ultimately, the narrator was able to escape the apartment and concludes that “Despite 

my most careful searches and investigations, I have never been able to find the Rue d’Auseil. But 

I am not wholly sorry; either for this or for the loss in undreamable abysses of the closely written 

sheets which alone could have explained the music of Erich Zann.”79  

 74 Ibid., 178. 75 Ibid. 76 Ibid., 179. 77 Ibid. 78 Ibid., 180. 79 Ibid.,180. 

                                                                                                                       142 

Meanwhile, Lovecraft’s “The Rats in The Walls” (1923)—according to one modern 

scholar--  was Lovecraft’s equivalent of Poe’s “The Fall of the House of Usher” and “the pinnacle 

of his work in gothic/Poe vein… [representing] an exponential leap in quality from his past work, 

and … [producing] nothing so good until “The Call of Cthulhu” in 1926.”80  Lovecraft himself had 

mixed feelings for the tale, calling it “the longest story ever to proceed from my pen. I shall never 

shew them to anybody because I will not type them and do not wish to hear the adverse criticism, 

I am sure little Alfie will give them.”81   Written in August and September 1923, it was rejected by 

the amateur magazine The Argosy for being “too horrible.”82  The following March, however, “The 

Rats in the Walls” was published by Weird Tales.  Shortly thereafter, Lovecraft wrote a friend, 

noting with excitement that the editor “flattered me outrageously, calling my ‘Rats in the Walls’ 

the best story Weird Tales has ever secured.”83  

“The Rats in the Walls” was the story of an unnamed narrator-- a Virginian who was a 

descendant of the unsavory, English Delapoore family, and who—along with his black cat-- recently 

took residence on the family’s ancient estate, Exham Priory, in England. The locals around the 

estate informed the narrator of the family’s history—dating back to 1261—and including such atrocities 

as murder, kidnapping, ritualistic sacrifice and witchcraft. The narrator’s imagination was assaulted 

by the lore—and especially the story of a family member who, after murdering his entire household, 

escaped to the English colonies in America.  According to the legend, Exham Priory had shortly thereafter 

been invaded by rats.  “The dramatic epic of the rats -the scampering army of obscene vermin which

The other work Lovecraft alludes to is the less known “The Unamenable” (1923)-the first work in which the fictional town of Arkham is mentioned. “Little Alfie” may be a reference to close friend and composer Alfred Galpin (1901-1983). 82 Lovecraft, “The Rats in the Walls,” in Joshi, H.P. Henneberger was the original publisher of Weird Tales. 

                                                                                                                       143 

had bought forth from the caste three months after the tragedy that doomed it to desertion-the lean, 

filthy, ravenous army which had swept all before it and devoured fowl, cats, dogs, hogs, sheep, 

and even two hapless human beings before its fury was spent.”84  

Haunted by the tale, the narrator soon noticed that his black cat began to act restlessly and 

continuously sniffed the walls of the house and came to insist that he himself “heard a low, distinct 

scurrying as of rats or mice.”85 The narrator then invited his friend Captain Edward Norrys to spend 

a night with him and solve the mystery.  While searching house, the two friends discovered a secret 

basement and a crypt full of “a ghastly array of semi-human bones. Those which retained their 

collocation as skeletons shewed attitudes of panic fear, and over all were the marks rodent 

gnawing.”86  While “all the bones were gnawed, mostly by rats, but somewhat by others of the half-

human drove.” With the narrator’s sanity on the brink after the realization of the history of 

cannibalism in his family, he attacked Norrys.  “Something bumped into me—something soft and 

plump. It must have been the rats; the viscous, gelatinous, ravenous army that feast on the dead 

and the living,” he testified.  “Why shouldn’t rats eat a de la Poer as a de la Poer eats forbidden 

things? . . . The war ate my boy, damn them all . . . and the Yanks ate Carfax with flames and burnt 

Grandsire Delapore and the secret . . . No, no, I tell you, I am not that daemon swineherd in the 

twilit grotto! It was not Edward Norrys’ fat face on that flabby, fungous thing! Who says I am a de 

la Poer? He lived, but my boy died! . . . Shall a Norrys hold the lands of a de la Poer? . . . It’s 

voodoo, I tell you . . . that spotted snake . . . Curse you, Thornton, I’ll teach you to faint at what 

my family do! . . . ’Sblood, thou stinkard, I’ll learn ye how to gust . . . wolde ye swynke me thilke 

wys? . . . Magna Mater! Magna Mater! . . . Atys . . . Dia ad aghaidh ’s ad aodann . . . agus bas 

 84 Lovecraft, “The Rats in the Walls,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 245. 85 Ibid., 246. 86 Ibid., 252. 

                                                                                                                       144 

dunach ort! Dhonas ’s dholas ort, agus leat-sa! . . . Ungl . . . ungl . . . rrrlh . . . chchch . . .”87 The 

narrator was found three hours later in a horrific state- “crouching in the blackness over the plump, 

half-eaten body of Capt. Norrys, with my own cat leaping and tearing at my throat.”88  Now 

institutionalized, the narrator insisted he was not to blame for the death of his friend.  “I did not do it,” he 

declared. “They must know it was the rats; the slithering, scurrying rats whose scampering will 

never let me sleep; the daemon rats that race behind the padding in this room and beckon me down 

to greater horrors than I have ever known; the rats they can never hear; the rats, the rats in the 

walls.”89  

A second event to fundamentally influence Lovecraft in the 1910s was his introduction to 

contemporary writer Lord Dunsany (1878-1957).  Dunsany was an early fantasy writer, publishing 

a collection of interconnected stories entitled The Gods of Pegana in 1905. In it—as the title 

suggested-- Dunsany invented a “new mythology,” a pantheon of gods in a place called Pegana.  

He was, The Boston Globe insisted in 1919, “a fabulist par excellence in a world of realists… His 

world is a dream world where weird and pitiless gods and singularly human men struggle against 

each other while the figure of Time watches them all ‘with an eager sword.’”90  Lovecraft himself 

later wrote that Dunsany was “Unexcelled in the sorcery of crystalline singing prose, and supreme 

in the creation of a gorgeous and languorous world of iridescently exotic vision.”  He was, 

Lovecraft added, “dedicated to a strange world of beauty, and pledged to eternal warfare against 

the coarseness and ugliness of diurnal reality… [and possessing] the most truly cosmic 

[worldview] of any held in literature of any period.”91  Lovecraft was not introduced to the writings 

 87 Ibid., 254-255. 88 Ibid. 89 Ibid. 90 “Lecture by Lord Dunsany,” The Boston Globe, October 19, 1919, 60. 91 Joshi, ed. The Supernatural in Horror, 67. 

                                                                                                                       145 

of Dunsany until 1919.  He began with the story “Poltarnees—Beholder of Ocean” from Dunsany’s 

1910 collection, A Dreamer’s Tales.  “The first paragraph,” Lovecraft recalled, “arrests me as with 

an electric shock, & I had not read two pages before I became a Dunsany devotee for life.”92 

In the fall of 1919, Dunsany visited the United States in conjunction with the opening of 

some the first productions of his plays in America.  In a broad ranging interview with the New York 

Sun that October, Dunsany reflected on genius and artistry, and the state of world affairs.  He 

acknowledged that the First World War, which had just officially ended that June with the Treaty 

of Versailles (1919), had affected him greatly.  “To-day I don’t know what my philosophy is,” 

Dunsany admitted.  “I must find out.  I am trying to adjust myself to the catastrophe that is just 

past. I don’t know whether I can adjust my life to philosophy and ideals.  I am like a man in a city 

that has been struck by an earthquake.”  Dunsany then pondered the question, “Why does a man 

write?” and his own quest for fame and genius.  “I would,” he insisted, “give my chance of fame 

to find and help a genius… We all want something.  In London we say, ‘Let’s get drunk and go the 

Derby.’ But some of us feel that that isn’t enough… Some people say, ‘Let’s make a million 

dollars,’ and some of us feel that isn’t enough.”  Still, he added, “Some of us feel that art alone 

matters.”93   

That same week, Dunsany traveled to Boston and delivered a lecture at the Copley-Plaza.94  

Lovecraft, who had only just read Dunsany’s A Dreamer’s Tales, attended the event.  He arrived 

early, and along with some fellow amateur journalists, “obtained front seats; so that during the 

 92 Lovecraft to Clark Ashton Smith, April 14, 1929, in David E. Schultz and S.T. Joshi, eds., Downward Spire, Lonely Hill: The Letters of H.P. Lovecraft and Clark Ashton Smith, 1922-1931 (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2020), 172. 93 M.K. Wisehart, “Ideas and Fame: A One-Act Conversation,” Books and the Book World of the Sun Section, New York Sun, October 19, 1919, 14-15. 94 Dunsany offered a brief overview of his American travels in his 1944 memoir, While the Sirens Slept, but never mentioned Lovecraft, whom he never met or had any correspondence. 

                                                                                                                       146 

address I sat directly opposite the speaker, not ten feet from him.”95   While “immensely impressed” 

with Dunsany, Lovecraft did not seek an autograph, telling a friend later that “I despise fawning 

upon the great…”  On his train ride back to Providence, though, Lovecraft began reading The Gods 

of Pegana—a book “kindly lent me” by fellow amateur journalist, Alice Hamlet of Boston.96  By 

1919, Lovecraft and Hamlet had become close enough friends that she was recommending books 

to him.  “I never considered Mr. Lovecraft handsome,” Hamlet later recalled, “and I am sure he 

was never interested in me as a girl!  We merely had similar tastes which made for a congenial 

acquaintance.”97   

According to Hamlet, Lovecraft’s enthusiasm for Dunsany was unsurprising.  “Mr. 

Lovecraft’s style of writing was highly imaginative as was Dunsany’s” she later observed, “and I 

thought Mr. Lovecraft would greatly enjoy hearing the Irish poet.”  Most certainly, Hamlet added, 

“There was this difference between the writers’ output—Lovecraft resembled Edgar Allan Poe, 

with his stark and wild imaginings… [while] Dunsany wrote in an almost Biblical style, with prose 

that was almost poetry.”  Regardless, Lovecraft’s “attendance at the Dunsany lecture,” she 

concluded, “was surely a milestone in his life…”98  Indeed, after his encounter with Dunsany 

Lovecraft began to incorporate more fantasy into his stories.  His 1920 short story, “The Doom 

That Came to Sarnath”—about an ancient people in the land of Mnar, who founded a city called 

 95 Lovecraft to Rheinhart Kleiner, November 9, 1919, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., H.P. Lovecraft Letters to Rheinhart Kleiner and Others, (New York: Hippocampus Books, 2020), 145.   96 Alice Hamlet quoted in R. Alain Everts, “Lovecraft and Lord Dunsany,” https://www.hplovecraft.com/study/articles/hpl-dunsany.aspx, Lovecraft to Rheinhart Kleiner, November 9, 1919, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., H.P. Lovecraft Letters to Rheinhart Kleiner and Others, (New York: Hippocampus Books, 2020), 145-147.   97 Alice Hamlet quoted in R. Alain Everts, “Lovecraft and Lord Dunsany,” https://www.hplovecraft.com/study/articles/hpl-dunsany.aspx.   

                                                                                                                       147 

Sarnath, and there fought against a strange race descended from the Moon (and worshipped a god 

named Bokrug)—clearly demonstrated Dunsany’s influence.  In fact, throughout the 1920s, 

Lovecraft—like Dunsany—invented his own mythology.  This was reflected not only in his 

“Dreamlands” stories (1918-1932), but also the “Cthulhu Mythos,” first advanced in his 1928 short 

story, “The Call of Cthulhu.”99  It was this fusion of fantasy and horror, one modern scholar noted,  

that resulted “in a major departure from the previous horror tradition—and, in many ways, from 

the entire Western literary tradition, which habitually if unconsciously stressed the centrality of 

human beings to the cosmos.”  For the rest of his life, Lovecraft’s stories increasingly emphasized 

“the insignificance of humanity in a universe that appears to be boundless both in space and 

time.”100 

        The third influence on Lovecraft during this period was his marriage to Sonia Haft Greene 

and his subsequent move from Providence to New York City.  Lovecraft meet Greene—a thirty-

eight-year-old pulp writer and publisher of the amateur journal, The Rainbow-- at an amateur press 

convention in Boston in 1921. They were introduced by a mutual friend, Rheinhart Kleiner, who  

described Greene as “a very attractive woman of Junoesque proportions.”101    Born in Ukraine in 

1883, Greene, who was Jewish, emigrated to the United States with her mother in 1892.  In 1899, 

at the age of sixteen, she was married to Samuel Greene, a New Yorker of Russian descent, who 

was ten years her elder.  The two had a daughter, Carol Weld, born in 1903.  The marriage ended 

 99 “The Call of Cthulhu” was written in 1926, but not published in Weird Tales until 1928.  Lovecraft had started working on the story as early as 1920, and was inspired—Lovecraft wrote friend Rheinhart Kleiner—by dreams he had in 1919.  While Lovecraft never mentioned Dunsany specifically in his letters to Kleiner, his dreams occurred during the time he was reading Dunsany’s two collections, A Dreamer’s Tale and The Gods of Pegana, and saw the great writer in person.  See Lovecraft to Kleiner, May 21, 1920, and Lovecraft to Kleiner, December 14, 1920, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., H.P. Lovecraft Letters to Rheinhart Kleiner and Others, New York: Hippocampus Books, 2020. 100 Joshi, “Introduction” in H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction. (New York: Barnes & Noble, 2008), xi. 101 Rheinhart Kleiner, “A Memoir of Lovecraft,” in August Derleth, ed., Something About Cats and Other Pieces.  (Freeport, New York: Books for Libraries Press, 1971), 221. 

                                                                                                                       148 

in 1916 with Greene’s death.  By 1921, Sonia and her daughter were living in the then-affluent 

Flatbush area of Brooklyn.  Sonia herself “held an executive position with a fashionable women’s 

wear establishment on Fifth Avenue,” earning around $10,000 annually.102  According to Kleiner,  

Greene “was morally an absolute puritan.  Her standards of conduct were not only severe, but 

[also]… naïve.”103  However, Lovecraft scholar,  R. Alain Everts, who interviewed Greene in 1953, 

later wrote that “she impressed me as a well-sexed woman, not easily satisfied.”104    

While often viewed as “very friendly” and “helpful,” Lovecraft was a recluse who rarely 

left Providence.  At home, on 598 Angell Street, Lovecraft was often—until her death in 1921— 

doted on by his mother, who, upon every hour, appeared in his room with a glass of milk105.  He 

was also in poor health.  Fellow amateur writer Alice Hamlet described him as “tall and large-

boned—with a long jaw—or perhaps I should say chin—from the lower lip downward.  He was 

rather dark complexioned and was extremely pale.  Evidently, he was not in very good health.  He 

had severe headaches and never was known to go far from home.”106  Greene herself later reflected 

that Lovecraft’s “love of the weird and mysterious… was born of sheer loneliness.”107 He also 

possessed, Kleiner admitted, “a certain tenseness of manner.” Still, Lovecraft could make a 

favorable impression on strangers—especially with his generosity.  “I remember especially his 

generosity with books,”  wrote Kleiner.  “[H]e would lend any volume to almost anybody.”108

(Freeport, New York: Books for Libraries Press, 1971), 252. 108 Rheinhart Kleiner, “A Memoir of Lovecraft,” in Ibid., 221. 

                                                                                                                       149 

After their meeting in Boston, Lovecraft and Greene began “a steady correspondence” over 

many months.109 Unfortunately, much of their correspondence was lost, but Greene did later write 

an essay about her life with Lovecraft—“Howard Phillips Lovecraft As His Wife Remembers 

Him”-- that was published in the Providence Journal in August 1948.110  According to Greene, 

Lovecraft was very insecure, especially about his looks.  “[T]he entire Lovecraft family had the 

prognathous jaw and the extremely short upper lip,” Greene wrote.  He once asked, “How can a 

woman love a face like mine?” Ultimately, Greene insisted, “I felt that if he could be made to feel 

more confident in his genius as a writer and to forget about his ‘awful looks,’ as he put it, he would 

become less diffident and more happy.  So whenever an opportunity presented itself I would not 

avoid giving him compliments.”111  In March 1924, Lovecraft traveled to New York, and he and 

Greene were married at St. Paul’s Church, “where Washington and Lord Howe and many other 

great men have worshipped.”112  Despite his atheism, then, Lovecraft insisted on both the church 

and a Christian minister.  In a letter to his aunt announcing the marriage, Lovecraft noted that “[a] 

detailed intellectual and aesthetic acquaintance since 1921…[as well as] a three-months visit in 

1922 wherein congeniality was tested and found perfect in an infinity of ways, furnished abundant 

proof not only that S.H.G is the most inspiring and encouraging influence which could possibly be 

bought to bear on me, but that she herself had begun to find me [more] congenial than anyone else, 

 109 Sonia Davis, “Lovecraft As I Knew Him,” in Ibid., 234. 110 Greene’s essay was later published in a 1971 collection of works by and about Lovecraft entitled Something About Cats and Other Pieces, edited by August Derleth. Now writing as Sonia Davis, the essay was entitled simply “Lovecraft As I Knew Him.” 111 Sonia Davis, “Lovecraft As I Knew Him,” in August Derleth, ed., Something About Cats and Other Pieces.  (Freeport, New York: Books for Libraries Press, 1971), 236. 112 Lovecraft quoted by Greene in Sonia Davis, “Lovecraft As I Knew Him,” in August Derleth, ed., Something About Cats and Other Pieces. (Freeport, New York: Books for Libraries Press, 1971), 239. 

                                                                                                                       150 

and had come to depend to a great extent on my correspondence and conversation for mental 

contentment and artistic and philosophical enjoyment.”113   

In the letter, Lovecraft also acknowledged his need for an “active life” and insisted that his 

new residence in “259 Parkside,” an address in Flatbush, Brooklyn was the logical next step.114 

“This more active life,” he added,  “… demands many things which I could dispense with when 

drifting sleepily and inertly along, shunning a world which exhausted and disgusted me, and 

having no goal but a phial of cyanide when my money should give out.”  While “fully prepared to 

seek oblivion whenever cash should fail …. [the] benevolent angel S.H.G. [suddenly] stepped into 

my circle of consciousness and began to combat that idea with the opposite one of effort, and the 

enjoyment of life through the rewards which effect will bring.”115 Still, Kleiner recalled, “There 

was a general feeling [among his friends] that the marriage was not destined to last...”116 

Lovecraft lived in New York City for only two years.   In 1926, he returned to Providence, 

leaving Greene behind in Brooklyn—the two amicably divorcing in 1929.  Lovecraft was initially 

mesmerized by the city.  “I saw for the first time the cyclopean outlines of New-York,” he wrote 

in 1922.  “It was a mystical sight in the gold sun of late afternoon; a dream-thing of faint grey, 

outlined against a sky of faint grey smoke. City and sky were so alike that one could hardly be sure 

there was a city-that the fancied towers and pinnaces were not the merest illusions.”117  Lovecraft 

was also enamored by the history of city, and visited many of its museums and old houses, 

 113 Lovecraft to Lillian D. Clark, March 9, 1924, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., Letters To Family And Family Friends. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2020), 101-107. 114 Lovecraft to Lillian D. Clark, March 9, 1924, in Ibid., 101-107. 115 Lovecraft to Lillian D. Clark, March 9, 1924, in Ibid., 101-107. 116 Rheinhart Kleiner, “A Memoir of Lovecraft,” in August Derleth, ed., Something About Cats and Other Pieces.  (Freeport, New York: Books for Libraries Press, 1971), 221. 117 Lovecraft to Maurice W. Moe, May 18,1922, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., Letters To Maurice W. Moe and Others.  (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2017), 84-100. 

                                                                                                                       151 

including the Bronx cottage of Edgar Allan Poe.118  It was an emotional pilgrimage for Lovecraft.   

“The atmosphere grows on one and finally grips one,” Lovecraft reflected.  “[I]t is so terribly 

vivid-- the ‘forties recalled in every somber detail. The pitiful poverty shows [that] something 

somber broods over the place. I seemed to feel unseen bat wings brush my cheek as a passed 

through a bare, cramped corridor…the house is so pathetically small…and such hideous things 

have been written there. Such was the home of the man to whom I probably owe my every genuine 

artistic impulse and the method I possess. My Master-- the great original whose titanic powers I 

can so feebly seek to copy… Edgar Allan Poe.”119 

It was during his time in New York too that Lovecraft became familiar with the works of 

Friedrich Nietzsche (1844-1900). In his Thus Spoke Zarathustra (1883-1885), Lovecraft, who was 

introduced to Nietzsche’s works by Greene-- explored this idea more thoroughly in the essay 

“Nietzscheism and Realism” (1921). “All human life is weary, incomplete, unsatisfying, and 

sardonically purposeless,” Lovecraft wrote.  “It has always been and always will be; so that he 

who looks for a paradise is merely a dupe of myths or of his own imagination.” 120  While 

Lovecraft’s worldview was pessimistic, it did not cloud his ideas on human compassion. 

“Pessimism,” he added, “produces kindness.”  Indeed, he insisted (in quoting the eighteenth 

century German philosopher Arthur Schopenhauer), “The conviction that the world and man is 

something which had better not have been is a kind of indulgence to fill us with indulgence toward 

one another. It reminds us of that which is after all the most necessary thing in life-the tolerance, 

 118 The structure still stands today near Fordham University. 119 Lovecraft to Maurice W. Moe, May 18,1922, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., Letters To Maurice W. Moe and Others. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2017), 84-100. 120 H.P. Lovecraft, “Nietzscheism And Realism,” in Joshi, ed., Collected Essays: Volume 5: Philosophy: Autobiography & Miscellany (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004), 71. 

                                                                                                                       152 

patience, and regard, and love of neighbor, of which everyone stands in need, and which, therefore, 

everyman owes to his fellow.”121   

Financial worries, however, continued to be a problem, impeding both his marriage and 

ability to write.  Shortly after their marriage, Sonia’s attempt at establishing her own hat shop 

failed. In a letter to his Aunt Lillian in August 1924, explained that following the “disastrous 

collapse of [Sonia’s] independent millinery venture… [there was] something of a shortage in the 

exchequer; so that it seemed advisable for me to investigate whatever commercial prospects of any 

kind might offer themselves-but the result thereof to date have been conspicuously negative.”122 

By the end of the year, Sonia gained employment, but it was in Ohio.  Lovecraft—who declined a 

job offer by Weird Tales as editor (which would have relocated him to Chicago)-- preferred to stay 

in New York City.  Sonia, then, moved to Ohio by herself.  While Sonia did, in fact, send him 

money, Lovecraft ate little—usually a medium-sized loaf of bread, cut into four equal parts with 

Heinz beans and cheese.123  By 1926, Lovecraft-- already small and frail-- had lost forty pounds. 

Lovecraft was also forced to move from Flatbush to Brooklyn Heights, a neighborhood 

that at the time was impoverished and was virtually next to Red Hook, an immigrant stronghold 

of Brooklyn that seemed to cause Lovecraft discomfort. Lovecraft commented on his new 

residence in retrospect stating that “My guess is that its decay had just set in, owing to the spread 

of the Syrian fringe (all unsuspected by me) beyond Atlantic Avenue.”124 Residing in New York 

seemed to heighten his xenophobia.  His wife later recalled that Lovecraft hated crowds and the 

 121 H.P. Lovecraft. “Nietzscheism And Realism” Collected Essays: Volume 5: Philosophy: Autobiography & Miscellany. in Joshi, ed. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004), 71-72. 122 Lovecraft to Lillian D. Clark, August 1, 1924, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., Letters To Family And Family Friends. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2020), 134. 123 Lovecraft to Lillian D. Clark, April 11, 1925, in Ibid.,  134. 124 Lovecraft to Bernard Austin Dwyer, March 26, 1927, in Joshi & Schutlz, eds. Lord of a Visible World: An Autobiography In Letters. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2019), 216. 

                                                                                                                       153 

subway, and that he viewed all foreigners as “mongrels.”  He was especially disdainful of Jews, 

calling them, Sonia wrote, “beady-eyed, rat-faced Asiatics.”  Sonia herself was Jewish, and later 

wrote that despite Lovecraft’s assertions that he was “cured” of his prejudices, he would, 

“whenever we found ourselves in the racially-mixed crowds which characterized New York… 

become livid with rage.  He seemed almost to lose his mind… Soon after our marriage, he told me 

that whenever we had company he would appreciate it if ‘Aryans’ were in the majority.”125   

Lovecraft produced few stories during this time—although he was the ghostwriter for 

magician Harry Houdini’s short story in Weird Tales in 1924 entitled “Imprisoned with the 

Pyramids.”126  There were two stories from his New York days in particular, however, that revealed 

his growing resentment toward the city.  The first was “The Horror at Red Hook,” written in August 

1925 (and not published until 1927).   In a letter to Clark Ashton Smith that October he made his 

feelings clear.  “When you see my new tale,” he wrote, “… you will see what I make of an idea of 

connexon with the gangs of young loafers and herds of evil-lurking foreigners that one sees 

everywhere in New York. I have a nest of devil-worshippers and devotes of Lilith in one of the 

squalid Brooklyn neighborhoods and describe the marvels and horrors that ensured when these 

ignorant inheritors of hideous ceremonies found a learned and initiated man to learn them.”127  The 

other story was “He,” also written in August 1925 (and published in 1926).  The story was clearly 

autobiographical in nature. In the second sentence, the narrator informed the reader that “My 

coming to New York had been a mistake; for whereas I had looked for poignant wonder and 

inspiration in the teeming labyrinths of ancient streets… and in the Cyclopean modern towers and 

 125 Sonia Davis, “Lovecraft As I Knew Him,” in in August Derleth, ed., Something About Cats and Other Pieces.  (Freeport, New York: Books for Libraries Press, 1971), 241-242. 126 The story, which appeared in the May- June-July 1924 issue of Weird Tales, originally credited only Houdini as the author.    127 Lovecraft to Clark Ashton Smith, October 9,1925, in Schultz & Joshi, eds., Downward Spire: The Letters of H.P. Lovecraft and Clark Ashton Smith:1922-1931. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2017), 84. 

                                                                                                                       154 

pinnacles that rise blackly Babylonian under waning moon, I found instead only a sense of horror 

and oppression which threatened to master, paralyse [sic], and annihilate me.”128  

In a letter to a friend that same August, Lovecraft confided that “In order to avoid the 

madness which leads to violence and suicide I must cling to the few shreds of old days and old 

ways which are left to me.”129  By 1926, then, Lovecraft “could no longer tolerate Brooklyn,” 

insisting that “If I could… live in Providence, the blessed city where I was born and reared, I am 

sure, there, I could be happy.”130  His “attitude towards minorities and his desire to escape them,” 

Sonia later insisted, “was one of the factors which” prompted the move.131  Sonia herself was eager 

to move with him and find work.  However, in “a conference with the aunts,” they “gently but 

firmly informed me that neither they nor Howard could afford to have Howard’s wife work for a 

living in Providence.  That was that.  I knew where we all stood.”132  The two continued to 

correspond, but in 1929 they divorced.  “I believed he loved me as much as it was possible for a 

temperament like his to love,” Sonia later wrote.  “He never the mentioned the word ‘love.’  He 

would say, ‘My dear, you don’t know how much I appreciate you.’  I tried to understand him and 

was grateful for any crumbs from his lips that fell my way.”133

## Chapter 6: Lovecraft and “the Modern Masters”

The 1910s and early 1920s proved formative years for Lovecraft.  He returned to writing 

fiction after suffering a mental “break” in 1908, and through amateur journalism, including the 

publication of his own journal, The Conservative, forged key connections with other writers. These 

were years too of rich intellectual development—at least at it related to his engaging with works 

that reinforced his own thinking.  His wife later described him as “non-religious and 

antidemocratic,” and the works he read reinforced that. Still, he never read any of the great works 

of poetry or fiction from the Harlem Renaissance, He did, however, read Nietzsche and Lord 

Dunsany, both of whom helped crystalize his own cosmic worldview.1   It was during this time 

also that Lovecraft began writing an essay on horror entitled “Supernatural Horror in Literature” 

(1927).  In it, he provided a historical survey of the genre, and well as overview of his own thoughts 

about the topic.   

 Lovecraft began writing “Supernatural Horror in Literature” in 1925, at the request of his 

friend W. Paul Cook, who was in the process of establishing his own weird fiction amateur 

magazine, The Recluse.  The essay finally appeared in the summer of 1927 in the magazine’s only 

edition.  Between October 1933 and February 1935, a revised version of the essay appeared in 

serial form in The Fantasy Fan.2  The completed version of the essay was finally included in a 

 1 In her memoir of Lovecraft, Sonia Greene recalled that Lovecraft was also influenced by the works of the English novelist Geroge Gissing (1857-1903), and his novel, The Private Papers of Henry Ryecroft (1903), in particular.  According to Greene, “Howard gave me [a copy of the book] early in the life of our romance.  Throughout, it elucidates much of Howard’s own personality, his attitude toward the masses, and toward life in general.” Sonia Davis, “Lovecraft As I Knew Him,” in in August Derleth, ed., Something About Cats and Other Pieces.  (Freeport, New York: Books for Libraries Press, 1971), 242. 2 The Fantasy Fan was a fan magazine operated by Charles W. Hornig.  

                                                                                                                       156 

Lovecraft collection published by Arkham House in 1939 and entitled The Outsider and Others.  

Then, in 2000, Lovecraft scholar S.T. Joshi published “the first textually sound and annotated 

edition” in book form as The Annotated Supernatural Horror in Literature.3  Overall, the essay— 

some 28,000 words in length—provided a general survey of the origins and course of the horror 

genre.  It also reflected Lovecraft’s own thinking about horror.  Specifically, he articulated a view 

of both the foundation of horror and the exact character of the weird tale.   

According to Lovecraft, “The oldest and strongest emotion of mankind is fear, and the 

oldest and strongest kind of fear is fear of the unknown.”  Indeed, because it was unpredictable, 

the unknown was “a terrible and omnipotent source of boons and calamities visited upon mankind 

for cryptic and wholly extra-terrestrial reasons, and thus clearly [belonged] to spheres of existence 

whereof we nothing and wherein we have no part.”  Thus, the unknown was the foundation for 

horror.  While the unknown might sometimes yield pleasure, it also, Lovecraft noted, dealt pain.  

“Because we remember pain and the menace of death more vividly than pleasure, and because our 

feelings toward the beneficent aspects of the unknown have from the first been captured and 

formalized by conventional religious rituals, it has fallen to the lot of the darker and more 

maleficent side of cosmic mystery to figure chiefly in our popular supernatural folklore.”4   

 This idea of “the unknown” was a key part of Lovecraft’s own horror—especially his later 

writings.  For him, the unknown was not merely a phenomena—i.e. a series of events (good or 

bad) unfolding second-by-second in real time-- but also, potentially, at least, a place (a spiritual 

world, a dreamscape, the subconscious mind, or even the larger universe or cosmos itself).  These 

events and places were mysterious and uncertain, and thus, possibly perilous and evil.  Ultimately, 

 3 S.T. Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1041.  4 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature” in Ibid., 1041-1042. 

                                                                                                                       157 

Lovecraft insisted, true horror literature manifested itself through the weird tale, which represented 

“cosmic fear in its purest sense.”  Indeed, the “true weird tale” was characterized by “something 

more than secret murder, bloody bones, or a sheeted form clanking chains according to rule.”  

Instead, it was marked by (1) “a certain atmosphere of breathless and unexplainable dread of outer, 

unknown forces,” and (2) a hint of “particular suspension or defeat of those fixed laws of Nature 

which are only safeguarded against the assaults of chaos and the daemons of unplumbed space.”5  

As such, Lovecraft concluded, the weird tale—despite all kinds of opposition from “the 

shafts of a materialistic sophistication”— ultimately “survived, developed, and attained 

remarkable heights of perfection.”  Still, he acknowledged, “The appeal of the spectrally macabre 

is generally narrow because it demands from the reader a certain degree of imagination and a 

capacity for detachment from every-day life.” Few, then, were “free enough from the spell of daily 

routine to respond to rappings [sic] from outside.”  It was quite understandable, he added, that “the 

taste of the majority” was for “ordinary feelings and events” since “ordinary matters make up the 

greater part of human experience.”  Fortunately, however, “the sensitive are always with us, and 

sometimes a curious streak of fancy invades an obscure corner of the very hardest head; so that no 

amount of rationalisation [sic], reform, or Freudian analysis can quite annul the thrill of the 

chimney-corner whisper or the lonely wood.”6  For Lovecraft, “the sensitive” of his own day were 

those he called “The Modern Masters”: Arhtur Machen, Algernon Blackwood, Lord Dunsany, and 

M.R. James.  All four, he insisted, possessed “a naturalness, convincingness, artistic smoothness, 

and skillful intensity of appeal quite beyond comparison with anything in the Gothic work of a 

century or more ago.”7  For his own enjoyment, he acknowledged in a 1928 letter, “I turn to that 

 5 Ibid., 1043. 6 Ibid., 1041-1042. 7 Ibid., 1086. 

                                                                                                                       158 

narrow group of special writers (all of them representatives of an ancient rather than modern 

tradition) … By the way, though—don’t fancy that I even dream of competing with these titans 

myself.”8 

Arthur Machen (1863-1947), “the forgotten father of weird fiction,” was a Welsh author 

most known for his 1894 The Great God Plan—a novella that novelist Stephen King later called 

“one of the best horror stories ever written. Maybe the best in the English language.”9   Lovecraft 

himself—ever interested in a romanticized European past complete with “wild domed hills, archaic 

forests, and cryptical Roman ruins”-- appreciated Machen’s heritage, and the author’s development 

of  “an imaginative life of rare beauty, intensity, and historic background.”  Indeed, Lovecraft 

praised Machen’s absorbing of “the medieval mystery of the dark woods and ancient customs,” 

and his championing of “the Middle Ages [generally] in all things—including the Catholic faith.”10  

In his correspondence during the 1920s, Lovecraft often referenced Machen, revealing a deep 

interest in the author’s ongoing work.  Indeed, in late May 1925, while living in New York, 

Lovecraft wrote a friend that he had visited the public library, and read “a new Arthur Machen tale, 

The Shining Pyramid, obtainable there but not removable from the building.”  The book, he added, 

was “fair, though not Machen’s best.”11  In others, Lovecraft called Machen one of his “favourite 

[sic] living authors” and “one of my chief literary admirations.”12 

 8 Lovecraft to Elizabeth Toldridge, May 4, 1929, in Derleth and Wandrei, eds., H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1925-1929, 277 & 282. 9 The novella was originally published in book form (and included another story, “The Inmost Light”). Damien Walker, “Machen Is the Forgotten Father of Weird Fiction,” The Guardian, September 29, 2009, https://www.theguardian.com/books/booksblog/2009/sep/29/arthur-machen-tartarus-press;  Stephen King, Goodreads Interview, November 5, 2014. https://www.goodreads.com/interviews/show/989.Stephen_King#:~:text=And%20is%20it%20really%20%22the,Do %20a%20little%20better.%22     10 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1087. 11 Lovecraft to Lilian Clark, May 28, 1925, in Derleth and Wandrei, eds., H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1925-1929, 12.  12 Lovecraft to Elizabeth Toldridge, February 21, 1929, in Ibid., 277 & 282. 

                                                                                                                       159 

Overall, Lovecraft examined three major works by Machen: The Great God Pan (1894), 

“The White People” (1904), and The Three Impostors (1895). Lovecraft found The Great God Pan 

to be perhaps Machen’s most famous tale—one about “a singular and terrible experiment and its 

consequences.”13 The experiment-- a “trifling reengagement of certain cells” in the brain— would 

“level utterly the wall of sense, and probably for the first time since man was made, a spirit [would] 

gaze on a spirit-world”-- what the ancients called “seeing the great god Pan.”14  Indeed, Mary— 

the subject of the experiment-- awoke from the procedure and her eyes “shone with an awful light, 

looking far away, and a great wonder fell upon her face, and hands stretched out as if to touch the 

invisible.”   Then, suddenly, “the wonder faded, and in its place the most awful terror.  The muscles 

of her face were hideously convulsed, [and] she shook from head to foot; the soul seemed 

struggling and shuddering within the house of flesh.”15  Mary was awake, however, “rolling her 

head from side to side, and grinning vacantly.”  It was a great shame, the doctor who performed 

the experiment, declared. “[S]he is [now] a hopeless idiot.  However, it could not be helped; and, 

after all, she has seen the Great God Pan.”16   

A witness to the experiment was “Mr. Clarke,” who in its aftermath, began writing a 

memoir called “Memoirs to prove the Existence of the Devil.”  Years later, on one “ugly December 

night, black with fog and raw with frost,” Clarke read a newspaper account of a girl, “Helen V., 

who, if she is still alive, must now be a woman of twenty-three.”17  The report told how the young 

orphan girl had appeared some years earlier at a village in Wales.  There, she exhibited strange 

behavior—including “playing on the grass with a ‘strange naked man’ in the woods.”18  Two 

 13 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1087. 14 Arthur Machen, The Great God Pan (Freeport, New York: Books for Libraries Press, 1970), 13, 11. 15 Ibid., 19. 16 Ibid. 17 Ibid., 21-22. 18 Ibid., 25. 

                                                                                                                       160 

children had been harmed during her stay in the village.  One little boy, Trevor—the one who had 

witnessed Helen with “the man in the wood”--  suffered from “violent hysteria” and “at the present 

date … suffers from a weakness of intellect.”19   The other victim was a young girl, Rachel, who 

spent time with Helen in the woods.  One day Rachel entered the woods and disappeared.  Clarke, 

then, began investigating Helen for his memoir.  A man named Villiers—who had a friend who 

had been married to the beautiful but evil Helen—shared more stories to Clarke.  For example, her 

appearance would “cause an artist to paint unthinkable paintings of Witches’ Sabbaths, creates an 

epidemic of suicide among men of her [sexual] acquittance.”20 Later, Clarke learned the truth about 

Helen.  She was the daughter of Mary, who had become pregnant after seeing “the great god Pan.”  

Clarke and Villers tracked down Helen—now living as “Mrs. Beaumont”—and hanged her.  As 

she died, her body was transformed.  “The skin and the flesh, and the muscles, and the bones, and 

the firm structure of the human body… began to melt and dissolve…  I watched, and at last I saw 

nothing but a substance of jelly… for one instance I saw a Form, shaped in dimness before me, 

which I will not farther describe.  But they symbol of this form may be seen in ancient sculptures 

and paintings which survived beneath the lava, too foul to be spoken of… as a horrible and 

unspeakable shape, neither man nor beast, was changed into human form, there came finally 

death.”21   

For Lovecraft, then, “Machen was one of the best of the “living creators of cosmic fear.”22  

Indeed, “No one could begin to describe the cumulative suspense and ultimate horror with which 

every paragraph abounds without following fully the precise order in which Mr. Machen unfolds 

his gradual hints and revelations.  Melodrama is undeniably present, and coincidence is stretched 

 19 Ibid., 27. 20 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1088. 21 Ibid., 81. 22 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror of Literature, 61. 

                                                                                                                       161 

to a length… and the sensitive reader reaches the end with only an appreciative shudder and a 

tendency to repeat the words of one of the characters: ‘It is too incredible, too monstrous; such 

things can never be in this quiet world… Why, man, if such a case were possible, our earth would 

be a nightmare.’”23   

Meanwhile, Lovecraft found Machen’s “The White People”—while “less famous and less 

complex in plot” than The Great God Pan—to be “definitely finer in atmosphere and general 

artistic value…”24  The story centered around a young girl, aged thirteen, who wrote a diary 

detailing how her nurse introduced her to the forbidden magic of a cult of witches.  By the age of 

three the girl had been exposed to various rites by the nurse herself.  At the end of the story, the 

young girl was found dead near a pool in the Welsh wood, having poisoned herself next to a statue 

from the “monstrous mythology of the sabbath.”25  Then there was Machen’s “episodic novel,” 

The Three Impostors, which included “certain tales which perhaps represent the high-water mark 

of Machen’s skill as a terror-weaver.”26  The novel was comprised of several different tales, the 

most famous one being “The Novel of the Black Seal”--  a tale about a “professor having 

discovered a singular identity between certain characters scrawled on Welsh limestone rocks and 

those existing in a prehistoric black seal from Babylon, sets out on a course of discovery which 

leads him to unknown  and terrible things.”27   

 A second “Modern Master” was the great English novelist and short story writer, Algernon 

Blackwood (1869-1951), whom Lovecraft described as “the one absolute and unquestioned master 

 23 Ibid., 63. 24 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1088. 25 Arhtur Machen. “The White People” in The White People and Other Weird Stories (New York: Penguin Classics, 2011), 147. 26 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1089. 27 Ibid., 1089. 

                                                                                                                       162 

of weird atmosphere.”28  Indeed, in one of his early short stories, “The Empty House” (1906), 

Blackwood demonstrated that mastery in the opening lines of the story.  “Certain houses, like 

certain persons,” he wrote, “manage somehow to proclaim at once their character for evil.  In the 

case of the latter, no particular feature need betray them; they may boast an open countenance and 

an ingenious smile; and yet a little of their company leaves the unalterable conviction that there is 

something radically amiss with their being: that they are evil…. and it is the aroma of evil deeds 

committed under a particular roof, long after the actual doers have passed away, that make the 

gooseflesh come and the hair arise.  Something of the original passion of the evil-doer, and of the 

horror felt by his victim, enters the heart of the innocent watcher, and he becomes suddenly 

conscious of tingling nerves, creeping skin, and a chilling of the blood.”29  Thus, while “[l]ess 

intense than Mr. Machen in delineating the extremes of start fear,” Lovecraft observed, Blackwood 

was “infinitely more closely wedded to the idea of an unreal world constantly pressing upon ours... 

amidst whose voluminous and uneven work may be found some of the finest spectral literature of 

this or any age.”30   

Blackwood’s early life was marked by extreme poverty.  “Horror draws its lines deep,” 

Blackwood wrote in his 1923 autobiography, Episodes Before Thirty.  “In my case the horrors 

were, perhaps minor ones, but at the age of twenty-one… they seemed important… They were 

three in number: the horror of loathsome vermin running over my body night after night, the horror 

of hunger, and the horror of living at close quarters with a criminal and degraded mind.”31  As a 

young writer in the late nineteenth century, Blackwood moved to New York City where he worked 

as a reporter for newspapers like the Evening Sun and the New York Times. After a decade of near 

 28 Ibid., 1091. 29 Algernon Blackwood, The Empty House and Other Ghost Stories (London: Eveleigh Nash Company, 1916), 1-2. 30 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1091. 31 Algernon Blackwood, Episodes Before Thirty (London: Cassell and Company, 1923), 1-2. 

                                                                                                                       163 

poverty in New York he returned to England where his interest in paranormal phenomena 

skyrocketed after he joined a secret, occult organization called the Hermetic Order of the Golden 

Dawn.32  In  1906, Blackwood released his first short story collection, The Empty House and Other 

Ghost Stories. The following year, he released his second collection, The Listener and Other 

Stories (1907), which included one of his most popular tales, “The Willows”-- a story where, 

according to Lovecraft-- “art and restraint reach their very highest development and impression of 

lasting poignancy is produced without a single strained passage or false note.”33  

“The Willows” was a tale of two friends—one of which was the narrator-- who were taking 

a canoe trip down the Danube River and experienced a premonition about an impending doom. As 

they journeyed down the river, they were filled with terror by the surrounding willow trees.  “Some 

essence emanated from them that besieged the heart.  A sense of awe awakened, true, but of awe 

touched somewhere by a vague terror.”34 At one point they believed that a man was trying to warn 

them of some danger.  As they camped that night on an island in the river they heard various noises  

and became unsettled, fearing that “[t]he Willows were against us.”35 The narrator then gave a 

description of “huge figures-- immense, bronze-colored, moving, and wholly independent of the 

swaying of the branches.”36 The next morning, they awakened to find that much of their supplies 

had gone missing and their canoe was damaged. The next night the narrator’s friend attempted 

suicide after a discussion about how a human sacrifice might allow one of them to escape “the 

 32 S.T. Joshi. The Weird Tale. (Austin: University of Texas, 1990), 99. Enjoying a wide membership in Victorian England that included Machen and William Butler Yeats, the Golden Dawn emphasized personal development through astrology, tarot divination, and “geomancy” (i.e. the divinations of geographical formations).  Blackwood had an interesting private life, even spending time during World War I as an undercover agent for British intelligence.  In the 1920s, he focused his writing talents on drama and children’s stories. In the 1930’s he became a reader on BBC Radio and later even appeared on television. 33 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror of Literature, 53. 34 Algenon Blackwood. “The Willows” Ancient Sorceries and Other Weird Tales. (New York: Penguin Classics, 2002), 23. 35 Ibid., 29. 36 Ibid., 32. 

                                                                                                                       164 

Willows.”  The next morning, however, they came upon “a body [that] had been swept away to 

midstream and was already beyond our reach.” A feeling of safety, then, replaced fear.  “The 

Willows” had received their sacrifice.  Bizarre, disorienting, and rooted in the emotions of 

uncertainty and fear, the story—especially with its insinuation that “the Willows” represented a 

borderland between worlds-- epitomized the strange and weird tales of the early twentieth century.   

In 1908, Blackwood published John Silence-- Physician Extraordinary, a collection of five 

related tales marred only by what Lovecraft called “traces of the popular and conventional 

detective-story atmosphere.”37  Indeed, Blackwood often wrote strange and fantastic tales—such 

as novels like The Education of Uncle Paul (1909), A Prisoner in Fairyland (1913), and The Wave 

(1916)—over horror ones. Lovecraft considered these novels to be “lesser” works “marred by 

several defects such as ethical didacticism, occasions insipid whimsicality, the flatness of 

benignant supernaturalism, and a too free use of the trade jargon of modern ‘occultism’”38  Still, 

in some of his novels, including Jimbo: A Fantasy (1909) and The Centaur (1911)—both of which 

Lovecraft characterized as “delicate phantasies” --  Blackwood achieved “ a close an palpitant 

approach to the inmost substance of dream, and [worked] enormous havoc with the conventional 

barriers between reality and imagination.”39 It was with his short story collections, then, that 

Blackwood produced his finest horror works.  Indeed, alongside “The Willows,” his 1910 short 

story, “The Wendigo”—included in The Lost Valley and Other Stories (1910)-- was one of his most 

“amazingly potent” horror tales.40  

 37 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1092. 38 Ibid., 1091-1092. 39 Ibid., 1093. 40 Ibid., 1092. 

                                                                                                                       165 

“The Wendigo”—a story Lovecraft called a “marked triumph in craftsmanship”-- was 

primarily about a group of lumbermen who had been working in the forest.41  One night, one of 

the men, Defargo, heard curious noises outside of his tent and left to investigate it.  Sometime later, 

his partner, Simpson, heard Defargo’s voice in the distance. “Oh! Oh! My feet of fire!” Defargo 

screamed.  “My burning feet of fire! Oh! Oh! This height and fiery speed!”42  Simpson tracked 

him for a while with no success and then returned to the camp for assistance.  At camp, some of 

the men related a local story about “the Wendigo”—a spirit or monster—who was rumored to live 

in the woods.  Later, the search party found whom they believed to be Defargo.  “I [have] seen that 

great Wendigo thing,” he said, and then disappeared into the night.43 When the crew returned to 

the main camp, they found the real Defargo frost bitten and dying from exposure. The crew was 

confused and unsure about what they had seen in the forest.  One native member of the crew, 

however, knew what it all meant.  “Old Punk” returned home, covering his “entire journey of three 

days as only Indian blood could have covered it. The terror of the whole race drove him. He knew 

what it all meant. Defargo had ‘seen the wendigo’”44  

 Lovecraft’s third “master of macabre” was Edward John Moreton Drax Pluckett, Lord 

Dunsany (1878-1957).  “Dunsany does not deal much in horror,” Lovecraft wrote in 1929, “but 

weaves a strangely potent fantastic beauty which has its roots in primitive myth and folklore.” 

Dunsany “formed an almost unique element in our literature and to “the inventor of a new 

mythology and weaver a of a surprising folklore.”45  Dunsany was a prolific writer, publishing 

 41 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror in Literature, 52. 42 Algenon Blackwood, “The Wendigo,” in Ancient Sorceries and Other Weird Tales (New York: Penguin Classics, 2002), 166. 43 Ibid., 187. 44 Ibid., 191. 45 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror in Literature, 53. 

                                                                                                                       166 

eighteen short story collections, ten fantasy and science fiction novels, ten plays, and eight poetry 

collections.  His most famous works were his short story collections, The Gods Pegana (1905), 

Time and the Gods (1906) and A Dreamers Tales (1910), and the fantasy novel The King of 

Elfands’s Daughter (1924).  “No amount of mere description can convey more than a fraction Lord 

Dunsany’s pervasive charm,” Lovecraft wrote. “His prismatic cities and unheard-of rites are 

touched with a sureness which only mastery can engender, and we thrill with a sense of actual 

participation in his secret mysteries. To the truly imaginative he is a talisman and key unlocking 

rich storehouses of dream and fragmentary memory; so that we may think of him not only as a 

poet, but as one who makes each reader a poet as well.”46 

 The last writer included in Lovecraft’s “Masters” chapter was Montague Rhodes (M.R.) 

James (1862-1936), an English author and college administrator, who—as Lovecraft put it—was 

“gifted with an almost diabolic power of calling horror by gentle steps from the midst of prosaic 

daily life.”47 From an early age James had an interest in antiquarian studies and enjoyed Medieval 

manuscripts and the Apocrypha. He moved to Kings College, Cambridge in 1882, and ultimately 

spent most of his career there, serving as both provost and vice-chancellor, as well as director of 

the college’s Fitzwilliam Museum.  Over the course of his writing career, James published five 

short story collections: Ghost-Stories of an Antiquary (1904), More Ghost Stories of an Antiquary 

(1911), A Thin Ghost (1919), A Warning to the Curious (1925), and a comprehensive one- volume 

Collected Stories (1936).48 Although an academic, James was, Lovecraft insisted, “a literary weird 

fictionist of the very first rank.”49  In the Preface to his 1911 collection, More Ghost Stories of an 

 46 Ibid., 54. 47 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1091-1092. 48 Most of James’s writings were scholarly, non-fiction works. 49 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1095. 

                                                                                                                       167 

Antiquary, James established “three very sound rules for macabre composition.”50  First, “the 

setting should be fairly familiar and the majority of the characters and their talk such as you may 

meet or hear any day.  A ghost story… in the twelfth or thirteenth century may succeed in being 

romantic or poetical: it will never put the reader into the position of saying to himself, ‘If I’m not 

very careful something of this kind may happen to me!’”  Second, the ghost must “be malevolent 

and odious: amiable and helpful apparitions are all very well in fairy tales… but I have no use for 

them in a fictious ghost story.”  And third, James believed “occultism” and pseudo-science should 

be avoided.  Such approaches, he insisted, “tend to put the mere ghost story (which is all that I am 

attempting) upon a quasi-scientific plane, and to call in play faculties quite other than the 

imaginative.”51     

The most well-known of James’s short stories was “Count Magnus” (1904), from an early 

collection entitled Ghost Stories of an Antiquary (1904). The story—"forming… a veritable 

Golconda of suspense and suggestion”-- was presented as a manuscript discovered half of century 

after its writing and that detailed the experiences of an English writer named Wraxall.52  During 

his travels in Sweden doing research, Wraxall stumbled upon an ancient house owned by the De 

la Garde family.  In researching the family,  he found “particular fascination in the builder of the 

existing, manor house, one Count Magnus, of whom strange and terrible things are whispered.”53 

Magnus had been an evil landowner who treated his tenants with contempt and partook in what 

was called “the Black Pilgrimage”—i.e. a pilgrimage to a certain location to salute the “King of 

Air” (i.e. the devil).54  Waxall became obsessed with the Count’s story, and he began visiting his 

 50 Ibid. 51 M.R. James, More Ghost Stories of an Antiquary (London: Edward Arnold, 1911), v-vi. 52 Joshi, ed., The Annotated Supernatural Horror of Literature, 56. 53 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1097. 54 Ibid., 1096. 

                                                                                                                       168 

crypt.  On his first visit, Waxall found one of the padlocks on the sacrophagus unfastened. After 

his second trip, he recorded in his journal that “I must have been wrong [about the padlock]… I 

see tonight there are two loose.”55 Then, on his third visit to the sarcophagus the last padlock fell.  

“I stooped to pick it up,” Waxall recorded, “and-- Heaven as my witness-- before I had raised 

myself there was a sound of metal hinges creaking, and I distinctly saw the lid shifting upwards. I 

may have behaved like a coward, but I could not for my life stay for one moment. I was outside 

that dreadful building in less than time than I can write-- almost as quickly as I could have said the 

words; and what frightens me yet more, I could not turn the key in the lock.”56  

Ultimately, the “modern masters” had a great and unquestionable impact on Lovecraft.  For 

example, Machen’s influence was linked to the idea that there existed terrible things in the world— 

things too much for the human mind to handle-- and that led to madness. Machen used this horrific 

effect in “The Great God Pan” with its merging of the scientific and the ancient. In his own 

“Dagon” (1917) and “The Call of Cthulhu” (1928), Lovecraft looked toward the reemergence of 

ancient evils while in early stories like “Herbert West-Reanimator” and From Beyond” focus on 

the emergence of scientific inquiry and its consequences.  One of Lovecraft’s greatest works, At 

the Mountains of Madness, combined both literary devices.   

Meanwhile, Blackwood—who looked beyond the realm of science and ancient secrets 

itself into the realm of nature and the very being of objects themselves—often presented, in stories 

such as “The Willows,” an unknown antagonist or force at work.  In the case of “The Willows,” 

the motives of the antagonist were ambiguous—i.e. they could or could not be villainous.  It was 

simply an entity that was a part of nature with no personality of its own, and only doing what was 

 55 M.R. James. “Count Magnus” Count Magnus and Other Ghost Stories. (New York: Penguin Classics, 2005), 77. 56 Ibid., 78. 

                                                                                                                       169 

in its nature. Lovecraft—in stories like “The Colour of Space” (1929)--  also employed that 

approach, and creating a villain too unsettling to describe.57  Like Blackwood, Lovecraft’s 

antagonists were often simply doing what was in their nature.  Unlike Blackwood, he extended 

himself beyond nature, and included antagonists from space, and outside our own nature and 

world. 

Lord Dunsany—whom Lovecraft insisted “influenced me more than anyone else expect 

Poe”-- looked past reality itself, and created instead new worlds, paving the way for the fantasy 

genre.58  Unfortunately for Lovecraft, his Dunsany homages—including “The White Ship” (1919),  

“The Doom That Came to Sarnath” (1920), and “The Quest of Iranon” (1921)-- gained little 

popularity among the reading public. By the end of the 1920s, Lovecraft moved beyond “Dunsany 

fantasy” and made-up universes—except for  The Dream-Quest of Unknown Kadath (written in 

1926 but not published until 1943), and  a cowritten work with E. Hoffman Price called “Though 

the Gates of the Silver Key” (1934). Still, Dunsany’s influence was still present in Lovecraft’s 

horror works, especially in the famed series “The Arkham Cycle” or “Cthulhu Mythos” itself with 

its pantheon of “Elder Gods” or “Old ones” and” Dunsanystic” unpronounceable names.  

Finally, James had a clear impact on Lovecraft as it related especially to technique.  Indeed, 

according to one modern scholar, James displayed “a fascination with the technique of the ghost 

story—specifically with the attempt to render the supernatural plausible in light of the increasingly 

militant materialism and secularism… that was dominating intellectual thought in his day.”59  

James also employed—in his quest “to create verisimilitude, and to overcome the hard-headed 

 57 This was a quality that was also apparent in the works of Machen, specifically “The White People.” 58 Lovecraft to Clark Ashton Smith, July 20, 1923, H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1911-1924, 243. 59 S.T. Joshi, “Introduction,” in M.R. James, The Complete Ghost Stories of M.R. James (New York: Penguin, 2006), viii. 

                                                                                                                       170 

skeptics natural incredulity in the face of the supernatural”—narrative distancing.60  Lovecraft’s 

“Cool Air,” written in 1926, and published in Tales of Magic and Mystery in 1928, mirrored 

James’s technique, and evoked “the clangor and modernity of the metropolis.”61  A modern ghost 

story set in New York-- and involving a narrator who is unable to leave his cold apartment (because 

he dead)—Lovecraft evoked the detail and realism of James, complete with themes of isolation 

and fear of death.   

Writing in 1927 to author Vincet Starrett, Lovecraft again expressed his admiration for 

these “Modern Masters.”  Lamenting the lack of a market “for spectral material above the penny-

dreadful grade,” he acknowledged that “I have never had a volume published, & am not at all sure 

that the merits of my nightmares & fantasies warrant one.”  The public, he noted, preferred “simple, 

understandable ghostliness with plenty of ‘human interest’ & a brisk, concrete, cheerful, & non-

atmospheric style.”  Lovecraft was doubtful of commercial success and expressed humility (and 

even insecurity) when comparing his work to others.  “When I compare my results with Dunsany, 

De la Mare, Machen, Bierce, Blackwood, (at his best, as in The Willows) M.R. James, or any other 

recognized master of the macabre,” Lovecraft concluded, “I lose at once whatever tendency toward 

egotism or complacency I might otherwise acquire…”62   

 60 Ibid., ix. 61 S.T. Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 348. 62 Lovecraft to Vincent Starrett, April 11, 1927, in Derleth & Wandrei, eds., H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1925-1929, 124. 

                                                                                                                       171 

## Chapter 7: The Lovecraft Horror

The last twelve years of Lovecraft’s life coincided with a tumultuous period in American 

history.  Indeed, the decades of the 1920s and 1930s constituted what historian Michael Parrish 

called the “Anxious Decades”—marked, among other things, by the rise (and later, fall) of a 

consumer society, “in which the production, marketing, and individual accumulation of a 

seemingly endless stream of goods and services threatened to become for many the chief 

preoccupation of daily life, a virtual secular religion.”  Meanwhile, Parrish added, “Traditional 

American cultures, anchored to particular ethnic communities, religious traditions, and geographic 

places, faced absorption or dilution in a sea of standardized products and homogenized attitudes 

spread by manufacturers, advertisers, the airwaves, and the silver screen.”1  Ultimately, discontent 

characterized the period—especially the 1920s.  Unsurprisingly, then, the early twentieth century 

French writer Andre Siegried observed in his America Comes of Age (1927), that “The essential 

characteristic of the post-war period in the United States is the nervous reaction of the original 

American stock against an insidious subjection by foreign blood.”2  Most certainly, Siegried’s 

observation summarized Lovecraft’s own thinking.  In fact, other contemporary accounts— 

moving beyond race and consumer culture—underscored the anxiety, discontentment, loneliness, 

and disillusionment that was often reflected in Lovecraft’s horror.  For example, Willa Cather, in 

the “Prefatory Note” of her 1936 essay collection entitled Not Under Forty, insisted that “The 

 1 Michael E. Parrish, Anxious Decades: America in Prosperity and Depression, 1920-1941 (New York: W.W. Norton, 1992), x. 2 Andre Siegried quoted in David J. Goldberg, Discontented America: The United States in the 1920s (Baltimore: The Johns Hopkins University Press, 1999), xi. 

                                                                                                                       172 

world broke in two in 1922 or thereabouts,” while mid-twentieth century historian Henry May 

called the World War I era “the first years of our own time.”3 

Lonely and ill at ease among the diverse population of New York City, Lovecraft returned 

to Providence, Rhode Island in March 1926.4  “Hooray!!” he wrote a friend later that month.  “Long 

live the State of Rhode-Island and Providence Plantation!!!... an ultimate Paradise to be regain’d 

at last.”5  Indeed, he insisted, New England living was a necessity.  He needed it, he wrote, “in 

some form or other.  Providence is part of me-- I am Providence.”6 Still, Lovecraft’s experiences 

in New York made him a changed man. According to one modern scholar, “Only by leaving 

Providence was Lovecraft able to discern the regions centrality of his writing.” And so, it remained 

the center of his work as the two sides of his personality: his nihilistic view on the world and his 

love for the past in all its aspects.  Together, they combined to serve as the mainspring of 

Lovecraft’s cosmic, new stories—works that proved the most enduring. Overall, the period 

between 1926 and his death in 1937, were years of continued popular and commercial struggling 

and the writing of some of his most prominent and enduring stories—stories such as “The Color 

Out Of Space” (1927), “The Call Of The Cthulhu” (1928), “The Dunwich Horror” (1928),  “At 

the Mountains of Madness” (1936), and his only two novels, both published posthumously, The 

Dream-Quest of Unknown Kadath (1943) and The Case of Charles Dexter Ward (1943) -- that 

quietly but assuredly transformed American horror.   

 3 Henry F. May, The End of American Innocence: A Study of the First Years of Our Own Time (Chicago: Quadrangle, 1964). 4Lovecraft and Greene divorced in 1929—although, unbeknownst to Greene (who remarried in the 1930s) -- Lovecraft never signed the divorce papers. 5 Lovecraft to Lillian D. Clark, March 27, 1926, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., Letters To Family And Family Friends. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2020), 574. 6 Lovecraft to Lillian D. Clark, March 29, 1926, in Ibid., 582. 

                                                                                                                       173 

Most certainly, Lovecraft never fully experienced commercial success in his own lifetime, 

dying a relatively obscure writer in March 1937.  All his publications (except for the cheap, 1936 

paperback, The Shadow Over Innsmouth) were in pulps—and even there, he often was met with 

rejection.  Indeed, his novella, “At the Mountains of Madness”—now regarded as one of his most 

important works—was rejected as a serial by Weird Tales when written in 1931.7  There were at 

least two main reasons for his lack of critical success.  For one, while horror by the 1920s was 

more generally acceptable as a genre, the publication of “horror” works outside of the pulps was 

still mostly reserved for “established” literary figures, such as Edith Wharton, Henry James, and 

Virginia Woolf.  Among most literary critics of the day, then, horror was still in “‘poor relation’ 

to mainstream literature.”8  “The purpose of shocking—of frightening or horrifying—is in a 

literature a meretricious one,” literary critic Winfield Townley Scott wrote in 1944.9  Echoing 

another critic, journalist Elliot Paul, Scott added that he refused “to regard weird tales, no matter 

who writes them, as great literature: ‘there is enough horror in real life without dragging it in from 

outside.’”10  Meanwhile, in his “Tales of Marvellous [sic] and Ridiculous” (1945), Edmund Wilson 

insisted that “The only real horror in most of these fictions is the horror of bad taste and bad art.”11 

The other reason was that Lovecraft was verbose, and his writing style pre-modern.  “It is 

difficult to believe that Howard Phillips Lovecraft was a literary contemporary of Ernest 

Hemingway,” novelist Robert Bloch later observed.  “We have not the brisk, terse, casual, and 

often simple style that typifies modern American, and to a lesser extent, modern English fiction.”  

Instead, Bloch insisted, Lovecraft possessed “a soberly erudite and precise style, a modernization
