 7 It was not published until 1936 by Astounding Stories magazine. 8 S.T. Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: Four Decades of Criticism. (Athens, Ohio: Ohio University Press, 1980), 21. 9 Winfield Townley Scott, Exiles and Fabrications. (Garden City, New York: Doubleday, 1961), 52. 10 Ibid. 11 Edmund Wilson, Classics and Commercials: A Literary Chronicle of the Forties. (New York: Farrar, Straus, 1950), 288. 

                                                                                                                       174 

of a style in vogue two centuries ago.”12  Many contemporaries disagreed with Bloch, arguing 

instead that Lovecraft’s style was “a cartload of bananas.”13  Lovecraft himself, Edmund Wilson 

added, “was not a good writer.  The fact that his verbose and undistinguished style has been 

compared to Poe’s is only one of the many sad signs that almost nobody any more pays any real 

attention to writing.”14  It was difficult, then, for a writer such a Lovecraft—who, as admirer 

Stephen King even acknowledged, was “a terrible dialogue writer”-- to transition from the world 

of pulp magazines to one of book contracts and large publishing houses.15  Despite all of his 

creative talent, Lovecraft—because of the reluctance of major publishers to embrace an “amateur” 

horror writer-- lacked the editorial input of someone like Maxwell Perkins of Scribner’s, who 

helped make accessible a whole generation of young writers, including Thomas Wolfe, F. Scott 

Fitzgerald, Marjorie Kinnan Rawlings, and Ernest Heminway.16   

The last years of Lovecraft’s life also resulted in some of his most profound writings-- 

works that ultimately ushered in a new phase of American horror.  Indeed, Lovecraft “was the 

Copernicus of the horror story,” author Fritz Leiber, Jr., later reflected.  “He shifted the focus of 

supernatural dread from man and his little world and his gods, to the stars and the black and 

unplumbed gulfs of intergalactic space.”  To achieve this, Leiber added, Lovecraft “created a new 

kind of horror story and new methods for telling it.”17 To achieve this, Lovecraft introduced 

 12 Robert Bloch quoted in S.T. Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: Four Decades of Criticism. (Athens, Ohio: Ohio University Press, 1980), 21. 13 Elliot Paul quoted in Winfield Townley Scott, Exiles and Fabrications. (Garden City, New York: Doubleday, 1961), 52. 14 Edmund Wilson, Classics and Commercials: A Literary Chronicle of the Forties. (New York: Farrar, Straus, 1950), 288. 15 Stephen King, On Writing. (New York: Scribner, 2000), 181. 16 Indeed, in 1936, Lovecraft’s novella The Shadow Over Innsmouth—written in late 1931-- was published “as a crudely bound booklet issued by William L. Crawford’s Visionary Publishing Company.”  It was his only work to be published in book form during his lifetime.   Unfortunately, the paperback was poorly marketed and cheaply made—and thus sold only a few copies.  Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 807. 17 Fritz Leiber, Jr., “A Literary Copernicus,” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: Four Decades of Criticism (Athens, Ohio: Ohio University Press, 1980), 50. 

                                                                                                                       175 

elements of Cosmicism—a literary philosophy grounded in the belief that there was no divine 

presence or plan, and that Man was generally insignificant in relation to the larger universe. 

Lovecraft’s “gods” were not gods at all but previous inhabitants of Earth who like humans are 

unaware of their origins and have more in common with aliens. If anything, the dark ideals that 

came out in Lovecraft’s writing were directly influenced by contemporary intellectual trends.  

Just as Lovecraft found influence in his four “modern masters”, he also looked to 

philosophy for inspiration and the creation of a literary world view. There were two philosophic 

strands that resonated with Lovecraft.  One was Materialism-- the belief that everything is 

composed of matter and that all processes of the universe are results of material interactions.  The 

other was Nihilism, a philosophical approach that denied meaning or purpose in the world.  Both 

philosophies were already staples of Lovecraft’ worldview long before the bulk of his literary 

works were written.  Indeed, “the human race is but a thing of the moment,” Lovecraft declared in 

1916. “[T]hat its existence on this planet is extremely recent, as infinity is reckoned; and that its 

possible existence in all the expanse of illimitable space is but a matter of yesterday. Space and 

time have always existed and always will exist. And we are able to see that not only humanity, but 

all other forms of organic life as well are mere innovations. Our human race is only a trivial 

incident in the history of creation. It is of no more importance in the annals of eternity and infinity 

than is the child’s snow-man in the annals of terrestrial tribes and nations. And more: may not all 

mankind be a mistake-an abnormal growth-a disease in the system of nature-an excrescence on the 

body of infinite progression like a wart on a human hand? Might not the total destruction of 

humanity, as well as of all animate creation, be a positive boon to nature as a whole?”18  

 18 Lovecraft To the Kleicomolo, August 8, 1916, in Joshi & Schutlz, eds., Lord of a Visible World: An Autobiography in Letters. New York: Hippocampus Press, 2019, 85. 

                                                                                                                       176 

Unsurprisingly, Lovecraft’s philosophy did not coincide with the prevailing Christian 

worldview of most popular literature in Europe and the United States.  Indeed, writing in 1918 

about “What I Have Against Religion,” Lovecraft—identifying himself as an “agnostic”--  insisted 

that the very crux of all agnosticism… [is] that the Judeo-Christian mythology is NOT TRUE.”19  

While he was not necessarily hostile to Christianity—“The Good effects of Christianity are neither 

to be denied, nor lightly esteemed, though candidly I will admit that I find them overrated,” he 

once wrote—his embrace of materialism and nihilism (and agnosticism) resulted in an innate 

pessimism.20  The transition from youth to middle age, he wrote in 1920, led the thinker to realize 

“there is no such thing as ideal happiness and justice, and ceases to strive after illusions so empty 

and unreal… One should realize that all life is merely a comedy of vain desire, wherein those who 

strive are clowns… The utter emptiness of all the recognized goals of human endeavor is to the 

detached spectator deliciously apparent—the tomb yawns and grins so ironically!”21 

While much of Lovecraft’s worldview was rooted in his early interest in scientific inquiry, 

he was also deeply inspired by philosophy, a point made in his short autobiographical essay “A 

Confession of Unfaith” (1922). “The most poignant sensations of my existence are those of 1896, 

when I discovered the Hellenic world, and of 1902, when I discovered the myriad suns of worlds 

of infinite space,” he wrote.22 Lovecraft was exposed to both philosophy and science at an early 

age and by his thirteenth birthday “was thoroughly impressed with man’s impermanence and 

insignificance, and by my seventeenth, about which time I did some particularly detailed writing 

on the subject I had formed in all essential particulars my present pessimistic cosmic views.”23  

 19  Lovecraft to Maurice W. Moe, May 25,1918, in Ibid., 65. 20 Ibid. 21 Lovecraft to Reinhardt Kleiner, March 7, 1920, in H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1911-1924, 111. 22 H.P. Lovecraft. “A Confession of Unfaith.” In Joshi. Collected Essays: Volume 5: Philosophy: Autobiography and Miscellany. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004), 147. 23 Ibid. 

                                                                                                                       177 

For Lovecraft, the First World War “only confirmed all the views I had begun to hold.”24 “The 

Peace Conference, Friedrich Nietzsche, Samul Butler (the modern), H.L. Mencken and other 

influences have perfected my cynicism; a quality which grows more intense as the advent of 

muddle life removes the blind prejudice whereby youth clings to the vapid ‘all’s right with the 

world’ hallucination from sheer force of desire to have it so.  As I near thirty-two I have no 

particular wishes, save to perceive the facts as they are.” wrote Lovecraft only a year before his 

depressing tenure in New York-- a time period in which his pessimism would have reached 

extremes due to his lack of employment, material positions and even food. On his return to 

Providence in 1926, Lovecraft’s work gained a nihilistic edge which seemed to take inspiration 

from the Godless universe which haunted the philosophical works of Friedrich Nietzsche (1844-

1900).  

Nietzsche was born in Saxony in modern day eastern Germany to a devout, Lutheran 

family. Growing up in Nuremburg with various health issues he nevertheless was an excellent 

student who would spend a year as philosophy student at the University of Bonn before transferring 

to the University of Leipzig to study classical philology (the scholarly study of the ancient Greek 

and Roman world. After receiving a professorship at the university of Basel-- where he taught 

classical philology-- Nietzsche became close with the famed composer Ricard Wagner and wrote 

his first book, a study of the Apolline and Dionysiac tendencies in art entitled The Birth of Tragedy 

(1872). By 1880, Nietzsche’s bad health led to his resignation from his professorship and he began 

a life of travelling and writing.  It was during this decade, then, that he completed his greatest 

 24 Ibid. 

                                                                                                                       178 

works including The Gay Science (1882), Thus Spoke Zarathustra (1883), Beyond Good and Evil 

(1886), On the Genealogy of Morals (1887), and The Twilight of the Idols (1889).  

All these works were in a way a precursor for what was to be Nietzsche’s four-part work, 

The Revaluation of All Values (of which only The Antichrist: A Criticism of Christianity) was 

completed and published (1895). The work discussed the dismantling of the Christian value 

system; a system he saw as weak ethical system that “has waged a deadly war upon the higher 

type of man.”25 The other planned works would have discussed the creation of a new value system 

that may have in some looked back toward the Hellenic world for its values. Nietzsche had felt 

that “Christianity destroyed the harvest we might have reaped from the culture of antiquity…”--a 

viewpoint Lovecraft embraced due to his love of the Hellenic world and his dissatisfaction with 

Christianity.26 Nietzsche  spent the last years of his life hospitalized or the under care of his sister, 

who become his curator and editor, spent the rest of her life promoting his works as antisemitic 

polemics that represented National Socialist ideology.   

Lovecraft, the, read Nietzsche before the Second World War, and the postwar scholar 

Walter Kaufmann’s re-evaluation of his writings.   Nevertheless, Lovecraft did not mention the 

racial ideals that mainstream culture had wrongly interpreted from Nietzsche works.   Lovecraft’s 

interest was strictly of a political or philosophical nature-- as demonstrated in his essay “Nietzsche 

and Realism” (1921), which discussed Lovecraft’s political preference of an aristocracy at the 

time.  Nietzsche, then, was specifically examined in relation to the concept of the Ubermench or 

“superman”- a man who is who able to let go of Judeo-Chrisitan ethics to become someone who 

is able to affirm life and revaluate all values among other things. Ironically, Lovecraft mentioned 

 25 Friedrich Nietzsche. The Antichrist: A Criticism of Christianity. (New York: Barnes and Noble, 2006), 5.  26 Ibid., 72.  

                                                                                                                       179 

the Übermensch in a negative fashion in order to dismantle the possibility of such a race of beings 

ever existing. “I doubt,” he wrote, “whither it would be possible to create any class strong enough 

to sway permanently a vast body of inferiors, hence I perceive the impracticability of 

Nietzscheanism and the essential stability of even the strongest governments.” 27  

In a letter to a friend in 1931, Lovecraft noted that “With Nietzsche, I have been forced to 

confess that mankind as a whole has no goal or purpose whatever but is a mere superfluous speck 

in the unfathomable vortices of infinity and eternity”---a comment reflecting Lovecraft’s reading 

of Nietzsche’s nihilistic views in works like The Antichrist.28  Still, Lovecraft insisted, “I do not 

sallow him whole. His ethical system is a joke-or a poet’s dream, which amounts to the same thing. 

It is his method, and his account of the basic origin and actual relation of existing ideas and 

standards, which make him the master figure of the modern age and founder of unvarnished 

sincerity in philosophical thought”29  

While Lovecraft, then, accepted Nietzsche’s Godless universe with purposeless lives and 

meaningless religions, he was unable to accept that there is any ethic system that can be created to 

replace these systems. It was with these concepts that Lovecraft would full much of his later cosmic 

art.  Indeed, stories such as “The Call of Cthulhu”, The Colour Out of a Space” and “At the 

Mountains of Madness” demonstrated that in the “Dark Prince of Providence’s” literary world 

there was no purpose to existence and that there was no deity to look to for solutions.  Even the 

“gods” mentioned the Cthulhu Mythos were nothing more than former inhabitants of earth looking 

to regain control of the planet. There were no ethical solutions in Lovecraft’s worldview-- 

 27 H.P. Lovecraft. “Nietzsche and Realism .” In Joshi. Collected Essays: Volume 5: Philosophy: Autobiography and Miscellany. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004), 72. 28 Lovecraft to Maurice W. Moe, April 5, 1931, in Derleth and Wandrei, eds., H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1929-1931, 362. 29 Ibid. 

                                                                                                                       180 

existence was meaningless and ultimately led to madness. Lovecraft’s “borrowing” of Nietzsche’s 

nihilistic approach to the dismantling of ethical and religious systems to show the meaninglessness 

of existence became one of the main attributes of Lovecraft’s developing cosmic art-- an approach 

that began to show fruits in one of the first stories written within a this “cosmic” frame, “The Call 

of Cthulhu.” 

Without question, Lovecraft was impacted by the intellectual tide during which he lived.  

Indeed, the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries were marked by new ways of thinking 

about Man and the world.  By 1900, American thinking was deeply rooted in Progressive Era 

assumptions—i.e. “to a considerable degree man could make and remake his own world.”30  In 

works like Dynamic Sociology (1883), Psychic Factors of Civilization (1897), and Outlines of 

Sociology (1898), Lester Frank Ward, a professor of sociology at Brown University,  insisted that 

social evolution was grounded not in natural law, but in “man’s intellectual capacity… to shape 

environmental forces to his own advantage.”31  Meanwhile, in his Will to Believe and Other Essays 

(1897), William James encouraged readers to “Believe that life is worth living, and your belief 

will help create the fact.”32  For Walter Lippmann, in Drift and Mastery (1914),  civilization, then, 

was “just this constant effort to introduce plan where there has been clash, and purpose into the 

jungles of disordered growth.”33  Man had now arrived at such a state of knowledge that he could 

—as Lippmann’s title suggested—establish “order” or “mastery” over what had previously been 

“drift.”34   

 30 George E. Mowry, The Era of Theodore Roosevelt and the Birth of Modern America, 1900-1912 (New York: Torchbooks, 1958), 37. 31 Lester Frank Ward quoted in Ibid., 21. 32 William James quoted in Ibid., 19-21. 33 Walter Lippmann, Drift and Mastery (New York: Mitchell Kennerley, 1914), 269. 34 George E. Mowry, The Era of Theodore Roosevelt and the Birth of Modern America, 1900-1912 (New York: Torchbooks, 1958), 18. 

                                                                                                                       181 

This optimistic view of early twentieth century reformers was not embraced by everyone.  

Indeed, there was a long tradition of nihilism—i.e. the rejection of moral principles and the 

embrace of a view holding that life lacked meaning or value—in American fiction.35  Most 

certainly, the First World War (1914-1919) helped shatter the optimism of the Progressive 

movement and ushered in “a postwar literature of disillusionment.”  According to historian David 

M. Kennedy, that disillusionment was rooted not so much in the war itself as it was a generational 

conflict.  “An older generation,” Kennedy wrote, “attempted to carry intact into the twentieth 

century traditional nineteenth-century definitions of morality, the rationality of human nature, and 

the civic utility of ‘high culture.’  Against them were pitted younger intellectuals attracted by 

notions of absurdity, irrationality, and the possible subversive implications of art.”36  Lovecraft 

himself expressed this attitude in a letter in 1916, insisting that “the human race is but a thing of 

the moment; that its existence on this planet is extremely recent, as infinity is reckoned; and that 

its possible existence in all the expanse of illimitable space is but a matter of yesterday... Our 

human race is only a trivial incident in the history of creation. It is of no more importance in the 

annals of eternity and infinity than is the child’s snow-man in the annals of terrestrial tribes and 

nations.37 

 35 The term “nihilism” was first popularized in Europe earlier in the nineteenth century. Philosophers such as Friedrich Nietzsche and Albert Camus were associated with the Nihilistic Movement.   36 David M. Kennedy, Over Here: The First World War and American Society (New York: Oxford University Press, 1980), 224. 37 Lovecraft to the Kleicomolo, August 8, 1916, in Joshi and Schutlz, eds., Lord of a Visible World: An Autobiography in Letters. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2019), 85.  Unsurprisingly, Lovecraft had little personal regard for Christianity.  Writing to a friend in the summer of 1918, Lovecraft, an agnostic, explained that “the very crux of all agnosticism… [is] namely that the Judeo-Christian mythology is NOT TRUE.”  Still, Lovecraft was not hostile to Christianity.   “The Good effects of Christianity,” he added, “are neither to be denied, nor lightly esteemed, though candidly I will admit that I find them overrated.” 37 Lovecraft to Maurice W. Moe, May 25,1918, in Joshi & Schutlz, eds., Lord of a Visible World: An Autobiography In Letters. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2019), 65. 

                                                                                                                       182 

Lovecraft’s return to Providence in 1926 rejuvenated his creative energy.  During his time 

in New York, he had written and published little.  Back home in Rhode Island, he again became 

very prolific—ultimately writing some of the best horror stories ever written and creating a distinct 

Lovecraft horror.  Overall, there were three key elements to Lovecraft’s time in Providence as both 

a writer and a weaver of tales. First, he possessed what one Lovecraft scholar called the gift of 

“narrative power”—i.e. to tell a tale with emotional force, stylistic heft, and content command.38   

Specifically, he employed gradual revelation, atmospheric detail, and complex sentence structures 

(complete with methodically chosen adjectives) that drew the reader into the disturbing, dark and 

vast environment of his tale.  For example, in “The Picture in the House,” published in the National 

Amateur in early 1921, Lovecraft insisted—in the very first paragraph—that “the true epicure in 

the terrible, to whom a new thrill of unutterable ghastliness is the chief end and justification of 

existence, esteems most of all the ancient, lonely farmhouses of backwoods New England; for 

there the dark elements of strength, solitude, grotesqueness, and ignorance combine to form the 

perfection of the hideous.”39   Indeed, Lovecraft himself insisted that the purpose of the artist was 

“to rouse strong emotion by action, ecstasy, and astonishment.”40  

 Lovecraft also introduced elements of Cosmicism—a literary philosophy grounded in the 

belief that there was no divine presence or plan, and that Man was generally insignificant in 

relation to the larger universe.  For Lovecraft, then, “nothing was so essentially terrifying to the 

human spirit as a dislocation in space and time.”41  Writing to Weird Tales editor Farnsworth 

Wright in 1927, Lovecraft acknowledged that “all my tales are based on the fundamental premise 

that common human laws and interests and emotions have no validity or significance in the vast 

 38 T.O. Mabbott, “H.P. Lovecraft: An Appreciation,” in Joshi, eds., H.P. Lovecraft: Four Decades of Criticism, 44. 39 Lovecraft, “The Picture in the House,” Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 124. 40 Lovecraft quoted in Ibid. 41 Ibid., xvi. 

                                                                                                                       183 

cosmos-at-large…  To achieve the essence of real externality, whether of time or space or 

dimension, one must forget that such things as organic life, good and evil, love and hate, and all 

such local attributes of a negligible and temporary race called mankind, have any existence at 

all.”42  The implications of this for horror were profound. Indeed, in his class short story “The Call 

of Cthulhu” (1928), Lovecraft—in the first sentences—insisted that “The most merciful thing in 

the world, I think, is the inability of the human mind to correlate all its contents.  We live on a 

placid island of ignorance in the midst of black seas of infinity, and it was not meant that we should 

voyage far.  The sciences… have hitherto harmed us little; but some day the piecing together of 

dissociated knowledge will open up such terrifying vistas of reality, and of our frightful position 

therein, that we shall either go mad from the revelation or flee from the deadly light into the peace 

and safety of a new dark age.”43  

 Then, there was his creation and development of a “pseudomythology”—especially that of 

what Lovecraft disciple August Derleth later called the “Cthulhu Mythos,” and that Lovecraft 

himself called the “Arkham Cycle.”44 While Lovecraft was “a significant New England 

regionalist”— almost all his stories at least started in New England—he was a creator of vast new 

worlds of the imagination.45 The “Cthulhu Mythos” itself included twenty-three short stories, 

including “Dagon” (1917), one of the earliest, and “The Haunter of the Dark” (1936), the last.  

Together, they formed a pantheon of “Great Old Ones” and “Outer Ones” that included Azathoth, 

Cthulhu, and the Mi-Go.  Yet, these “deities” and races were “never portrayed as gods pure and 

simple, even though they are worshipped as such by their human devotees.”46   Indeed, in At the 

 42 Lovecraft quoted in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, xi. 43 Lovecraft, “The Call of Cthulhu,” in Ibid., 355. 44 Lovecraft to Clark Ashton Smith, August 31, 1928, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., Downward Spire: The Letters of H.P. Lovecraft and Clark Ashton Smith:1922-1931. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2017), 163. 45 S.T. Joshi, ed., The Complete Cthulhu Mythos (New York: Barnes & Noble, 2015), ix.  46 Ibid., x. 

                                                                                                                       184 

Mountains of Madness (1931), Lovecraft revealed the gods to be nothing more than “myths”— 

merely barrel-shaped extraterrestrials who have come from the depths of space to colonize this 

planet.”47 Lovecraft also created “an entire fictional New England topography, with such cities as 

Arkham, Kingsport, Dunwich, and Innsmouth; a growing library of ‘forbidden’ books, such as the 

Necronomicon of the mad Arab Adbul Alhazred, which provided glimpses of the cosmic entities 

and their encounters with human being, and a cadre of sober, scientific protagonists whose 

encounters with the unimaginably bizarre unhinged their minds…”48 

 Specifically, there were five key works that constituted that mythos: “The Call of Cthulhu” 

(1928), The Case of Charles Dexter Ward (1943), “The Dunwich Horror” (1929),  At the 

Mountains of Madness (1936), and The Shadow Over Innsmouth (1936).  First, the most famous 

story that emerged from Lovecraft’s return to Providence was “The Call of Cthulhu”—written in 

1926 and initially rejected by Weird Tales.  The story—disjointed and ambiguous—was comprised 

of three “manuscripts” in the notebooks of the narrator’s recently deceased Uncle Angell, a 

professor of semantic languages at Brown University. In one manuscript, the uncle described 

different strange incidents, including a sculptor sculpting a “horrendous figure.”  In the second 

manuscript, the uncle detailed  a police raid on a cult in New Orleans, and how after their raid, one 

of the cult members told local authorities that the “Old Gods” were waiting to be resurrected in 

the city of R’lyeh “for the spells of mighty Cthulhu, for a glorious resurrection when the stars and 

the earth may be ready for Them.”49   

In the third and final manuscript the narrator himself continued his own investigation into 

the cult of Cthulhu.  Specifically, he learned of an Australian ship that came across an “island” 

 47 Ibid. 48 Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, xi-xii. 49 Lovecraft, “The Call of Cthulhu,” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 367. 

                                                                                                                       185 

that was “adrift” in the sea.  One of the crew members—the second mate-- sent a story of his 

experiences to the narrator who then rewrote it.  According to the second mate, the “island” was 

home to the city of R’lyeh and the old god Cthulhu, and the ship and crew somehow awakened 

him, resulting in much of the crew’s death.  Later, after the narrator learned that the second mate 

had been murdered, he feared for his own life—believing that followers of Cthulhu would now 

seek him out to destroy.  “What has risen may sink,” the narrator concluded. “And what has sunk 

may rise. Loathsomeness waits and dreams in the deep, and decay spreads over the tottering cities 

of men. A time will come-- but I must not and cannot think! Let me pray that, if I do not survive 

this manuscript, my executors may put caution before audacity and see that it meets no other 

eye.”50 

Writing to a friend in October 1926, Lovecraft noted that “Little Farny rejected Grandpa’s 

Cthulhu on the ground that it was too slow and obscure for his zippy morons… If I could get plenty 

of good revision to do, I’d never write another tale for these cheap commercial caterers.  It ruins 

one’s style to have a publick [sic] of tame-souled half-wits hanging over one’s head as he writes.”51  

The following summer, he again submitted the story to Wright. “In accordance with your 

suggestion I am re-submitting The Call of Cthulhu, though possibly you will still think it a trifle 

too bizarre for a clientele who demand their weirdness in name only, and who liked to keep both 

feet pretty solidly on the ground of the known and the familiar.”   Lovecraft added that he doubted 

that his writing “would ‘go’ very well with [Wright’s] sort of readers… The general trend of the 

yarns which seem to suit the public is that of essential normality of outlook and simplicity of point 

of view…” Indeed, he insisted, “The weird element in such material does not extend far into the 

 50 Ibid., 379. 51 Lovecraft to Frank Belknap Long, October 26, 1926, in H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1925-1929, 79. 

                                                                                                                       186 

fabric—it is the artificial weirdness of the fireside tale and the Victorian ghost story, and remains 

external camouflage even in the seemingly wildest of the ‘interplanetary’ concoctions.”52   

This time, Wright—pressured by Lovecraft friend and fellow writer Donald Wandrei-- 

accepted the submission.  “I am very glad to hear that you have found Cthulhu available for use,” 

Lovecraft wrote Wright in July 1927.53  To a friend, however, Lovecraft confided “Oh, by the 

way… [I’m] returning to the almost forgotten and detestably tradesmanlike [sic] world of 

scribbling—pipe the accompanying line from Little Farny! Wandrei sure did wake him up!”54 

“The Call of Cthulhu” appeared in Weird Tales in February1928 and was met with immediate 

praise from Lovecraft loyalists such as Robert E. Howard, who wrote in Weird Tales that spring 

that “Mr. Lovecraft’s latest story… is indeed a masterpiece which I am sure will live as one of the 

highest achievements in literature. Mr. Lovecraft holds a unique position in the literary world; he 

has grasped, to all intents, the world outside our paltry ken. His scope is unlimited, and his range 

is cosmic.”55  

While “The Call of Cthulhu” was making its way to publication in 1927 and 1928, 

Lovecraft completed his longest work of fiction, The Case of Charles Dexter Ward—a short novel 

he dismissed as a “cumbrous, creaking bit of self-conscious antiquarianism,” and thus never 

prepared it for public consumption.56  Indeed, it did not appear in print—serialized in Weird Tales 

in abridged form--  until 1941, some four years after Lovecraft’s death.  It was not published in 

full until 1943, and the Arkham House collection, Beyond the Wall of Sleep.  Overall, the novel 

 52 Lovecraft to Farnsworth Wright, July 5, 1927, in Ibid., 149-150. 53 Lovecraft to Farnsworth Wright, July 16, 1927, in Ibid., 154. 54 Lovecraft to Frank Belknap Long, July 6, 1927, in Ibid., 153. 55 Robert E. Howard quoted in S.T. Joshi, David E. Schultz, and Rusty Bruke, eds., A Means to Freedom: The Letters of H.P. Lovecraft and Robert E. Howard (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2017), 7. 56 Lovecraft quoted in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 490. 

                                                                                                                       187 

provided a detailed account of a young man, Charles Dexter Ward, who resided in “a private 

hospital for the insane near Providence.” Ward suffered from a “dark mania involving both a 

possibility of murderous tendencies and a profound and peculiar change in the apparent contents 

of his mind.”57   His trouble began when he became fixated on a distant ancestor named Joesph 

Curwen-- “a very astonishing, enigmatic, and obscurely horrible individual” from Salem, 

Massachusetts in the late seventeenth century.58 Curwen had left Salem during the witch trials in 

1692, and relocated to Providence, where, for fifty years, he seemed not to age. An entrepreneur 

“engaged in shipping enterprises,” Curwen soon gained a reputation in his new home as a “close-

mouthed “chymist [alchemist].”  In reality, however, he was a murderer and experimenter in 

necromancy (including the use of “essential saltes” needed for the resurrection of a body from the 

dead).59   The community soon learned of Curwen’s strange dealings, and raided his home.  

Curwen was then killed in the attack.   

Over two hundred years later, Curwen’s descendant, Ward, learned of the “chymist’s” 

history—and his remains—and sought out the “saltes” needed to resurrect his body.  Ward 

acquired them and raised Curwen from the dead.  Curwen then murdered Ward and assumed his 

identity.  It was Curwen, then, who was the resident at the mental hospital in Providence.  In the 

end, Ward’s physician, Dr. Willett, discovered the truth, and was able to recite an incantation 

which killed Curwen once and for all-- “Joesph Curwen now lay scattered on the floor as a thin 

coating of fine blueish-grey dust.”60  

 57 Lovecraft, The Case of Charles Dexter Ward, in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 490. 58 Ibid., 498. 59 Necromancy involved magical spells to communicate with the dead. Ibid., 501-502. 60 Ibid., 593. 

                                                                                                                       188 

In September 1927, shortly after completing The Case of Charles Dexter Ward, Lovecraft 

wrote a small, two-page “essay” entitled “History of the Necronomicon” (and not published until 

late 1937).   The “essay” was fictious and intended as a hoax, one modern scholar observed.61  

Indeed, the Necronomicon was a fictional book of Lovecraft’s imagination that found its way into 

many of his Cthulhu stories and provided—as its title suggested—a brief history of the “Great Old 

Ones” of the Cthulhu mythos (and even of the book itself).   Indeed, the last line of the “story” 

included a not-so-subtle reference to some of Lovecraft’s own influences.  “It was from rumors of 

this book (of which relatively few of the general public know),” Lovecraft concluded, “that R.W. 

Chambers is said to have derived the idea of his early novel The King in Yellow.”62    

The third major work in the “Arkham Cycle,” then was “The Dunwich Horror,” published 

in Weird Tales in 1929.  “I have managed to snatch the time to write one new tale,” Lovecraft 

wrote the previous August.  “It covers 48 pages, so that Wright would probably classify it as a 

‘novelette.’ I haven’t yet submitted it to him. The title is ‘The Dunwich Horror,’ and it belongs to 

the Arkham Cycle. The Necronomicon figures in it to some extent.”63  The story primarily focused 

on the character of Wilbur Whatley and his unseen twin brother—both the offspring of an albino 

mother and a father of alien origins in the fictional community of Dunwich.  Suspicion had grown 

around Wilbur due to his physical abnormalities (and odd appearance) and he was kept at home 

where his grandfather became his teacher.   As a youth, then, Wilber became interested in studying 

the Necronomicon and using it to open a portal for Yog-Sothoth, an “elder God” and father of the 

Whatley twins.  While attempting to steal copies of book from (the fictional) Misakatonic 

University in (the fictional town of) Arkham, Massachusetts, Wilbur was mauled to death by a 

 61 Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 621. 62 Lovecraft, “History of the Necronomicon,” in Ibid. 63 Lovecraft to Clark Ashton Smith, August 31, 1928, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., Downward Spire: The Letters of H.P. Lovecraft and Clark Ashton Smith:1922-1931. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2017), 163. 

                                                                                                                       189 

guard dog.   Wilbur’s twin and invisible brother-- who was now no longer under the guardianship 

of Wilbur -- escaped his confinement in the Whatley house, and wreaked havoc and horror 

throughout Dunwich.  Meanwhile, a librarian at Misakatonic University became interested in the 

Whatley family—especially after Wilbur’s death—and learned of Wilbur’s work to unleash Yog-

Sothoth.  Aided by two of his colleagues, the librarian stopped both the twin and Yog-Sothoth.  

A fourth major work of the Cthulhu mythos was At the Mountains of Madness—the second 

longest work of Lovecraft’s career and one that marked the zenith of his attempts to fuse weird 

fiction and cosmic art.  The story took place in Antarctica far from Lovecraft’s usual New England 

setting.  It involved, though, a Miskatonic-sponsored expedition that included science faculty from 

the New England university.  During an advance, field operation, a smaller team—led by a biology 

professor named Lake-- discovered soapstone fragments that were primordial in nature, as well as 

ancient and fossilized organisms that were nearly eight feet long and “baffle[d] all conjecture as 

to origin.” Some on the team insisted that the frozen fossils resembled “the Old Ones”-- a race of 

beings mentioned in the dreaded Necronomicon.64   

Meanwhile, at expedition headquarters, geologist William Dyer—the leader of the entire 

research endeavor—became concerned about the safety of Lake’s group following a loss of contact 

(shortly after the fossil discovery).  Dyer’s group ultimately made it to the last contact location, 

and to their horror found that all the members of Lake’s team were either missing or dead.  Lake 

himself was missing-- along with the fossil remains.  Dyer and a graduate student named Danforth 

then set out by airplane to the surrounding mountains ranges in such for any survivors and the 

missing specimen. There, they came upon an enormous and ancient stone city. The architecture 

 64 Lovecraft, “At the Mountains of Madness” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 739. 

                                                                                                                       190 

was exquisite, with walls painted with bass reliefs that revealed “a vanished epoch of their creation 

and contained a large proponent of evident history.”65 

According to the reliefs, the city had been made by “Shoggoths,” who were slaves of the 

“Old Ones,” and were fifteen feet tall and made of protoplasm.  Over time, the Shoggoths 

developed a will of their own to fight their masters, and joined with other races, including the 

Fungi from Yuggoth and the Cthulhu, until the “Old Ones” were forced back to their original 

Antarctic settlement.  Dyer and Danforth then found the remnants of decapitated “Old Ones” and 

six-eyed penguins that seemed to be livestock. Hearing “the wide-ranged piping-Tekeli-li! Tekeli-

li!”—and understanding that they were encountering the Shoggoths-- the two men ran for their 

lives.66  Lovecraft scholar S.T. Joshi later called At the Mountains of Madness an “extraordinarily 

evocative tale… the best of Lovecraft’s short novels.”67  While the tale was indeed his masterpiece, 

it was—to Lovecraft’s dismay and great disappointment-- initially rejected by Weird Tales. “Yes,” 

Lovecraft wrote a friend in 1931, “Wright ‘explained’ his rejection of the ‘Mountains of Madness’ 

in almost the same language as that with which he ‘explained’ other recent rejections to Long and 

Dereleth. It was ‘too long,’ ‘not easily divisible into parts’-- -and so on.”68 

And fifth, there was The Shadow Over Innsmouth, one of Lovecraft’s last “great” tales.  

Written in 1931, it too was rejected by Weird Tales (twice) and was not published until 1936 as a 

“crudely bound booklet issued by William L. Crawford’s Visionary Publishing Company.”69  The 

narrator was a student taking an antiquarian tour of New England.   During his travels, he heard 

 65 Ibid., 767. 66 Ibid., 801. 67 Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 723. 68 Lovecraft to J. Vernon Shea, August 7, 1931, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., Lord of a Visible World: An Autobiography In Letters. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2019), 341. 69 Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 806. 

                                                                                                                       191 

about the old and mysterious town of Innsmouth and decided to visit.  As he walked the streets of 

the lonely and decrypt town, the narrator met an old man named Zadok, who provided him with a 

history the town—including how it had once been shipbuilding center. However, in the mid 

nineteenth century—after the formation of strange organization called the Esoteric Order of 

Dagon—the town began to experience decline.  The leader of the order was a merchant named 

Marsh, who had spent time with of ancient tribe from the Caroline Islands who offered human 

sacrifices to “The Deep Ones,” a race of fishlike humanoids living in the sea.  Over time, then, the 

people in Innsmouth worshiped and bred these creatures.  Fearing he was in danger, the narrator 

tried to leave the town, which resulted in a chase scene that was unusual for a Lovecraft story. The 

narrator made his escape to nearby Arkham and only to discover that he was related to Marsh and 

that soon he will make his transformation into the sea.  Instead of suicide, the narrator accepted 

his fate: “We shall [all] swim out to the brooding reef in the sea and dive down through the black 

abysses to Cyclopean and many columned Y’ha-nthlei and in that lair of the Deep Ones we shall 

dwell amidst wonder and glory forever.”70  While The Shadow Over Innsmouth was another 

worthy addition to the “Arkham Cycle,” Lovecraft believed it would face difficulty finding a 

publisher.  “No,” he wrote a friend in late 1931, “I don’t want to offer ‘The Shadow Over 

Innsmouth’ for publication, for it would stand no chance of acceptance…  I have been paying too 

much attention to the demands of the markets and the opinions of others.”71 Derelth did try to 

submit the work for publication in Weird Tales, but it was rejected because of its length.   

In the early 1930’s, then, Lovecraft’s cosmic era reached its zenith. “The time has come,” 

he wrote in early 1931, “when the normal revolt against time, space, and matter must assume a 

 70 Lovecraft, “The Shadow Over Innsmouth” in Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 858. 71 Lovecraft to August Derleth, December 10, 1931, in Schultz & Joshi, eds., Essential Solitude: The Letters of H.P. Lovecraft and August Derleth:1932-1937, (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 419-420. 

                                                                                                                       192 

form not overly incompatible with what is known as reality--  when it must be gratified by images 

forming supplements rather than contradictions of the visible and mensurable universe. And what, 

if not a form of non-supernatural cosmic art, is to pacify this sense of revolt-- as well as gratify the 

cognate sense of reality.”72 Lovecraft understood that this type of horror was “vastly [different] 

from most of the weird literature we have had so far… If I live much longer, I may try my hand at 

something of the sort-- for it is really closer to my serious psychology than anything else on or off 

the earth. In ‘The Color out of Space’ I began to get near it-- though ‘Dunwich’ and ‘The Whisperer 

[in Darkness]’ represent a relapse.”73  

Lovecraft, of course, continued writing stories outside of the “Arkham Cycle.”  For 

example, in 1927, he published “The Colour Out of Space”— a science fiction and horror hybrid 

that he called an “atmospheric study”-- in Amazing Stories.74 Founded in 1926 by Hugo 

Gernsback, Amazing Stories was a rival of Weird Tales. Frustrated with “Little Farny” for his 

initial rejection of “The Call of Cthulhu” in Weird Tales, Lovecraft submitted “The Colour Out of 

Space” exclusively to Amazing Stories, which immediately accepted the story.  “I have just 

finished one new tale which I’ll send you in my next letter,” Lovecraft wrote a friend that March.  

“This one has little flashes of the quasi-poetic but is for the most part realistic; with a homely 

country setting “west of Arkham.” Something falls from the sky and terror broods. The thing is 

told by an old man forty years after, and the title is ‘The Colour Out of Space.’”75 Still, by the early 

1931, his literary output had slowed tremendously. Indeed, after 1931, he only wrote eight stories. 

 72 Lovecraft to Frank Belknap Long, February 1931, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., Lord of a Visible World: An Autobiography In Letters. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2019), 327. 73 Ibid. 74 Lovecraft to Clark Ashton Smith, March 24, 1927, in H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1925-1929, 114. 75 Lovecraft to Bernard Austin Dwyer, March 26, 1927, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., Letters To Maurice W. Moe and Others. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2017), 261. 

                                                                                                                       193 

Two pieces written during the last years of his life were not even stories at all.  For example, 

“The Book” and “The Evil Clergyman” were both fragments from 1933 that found their way into 

print only after Lovecraft’s death. That same year he co-authored (with E. Hoffmann Price) 

“Through the Gates of the Silver Key,” which was later published in Weird Tales in 1934. This 

left only four pieces between 1932 and 1937 of which Lovecraft wrote as full-length stories.  After 

1933, Lovecraft only formally wrote two pieces of fiction, “The Shadow Out of Time,” published 

in Astounding Stories in June 1936, and “The Haunter of the Dark”-- his last published work during 

his lifetime--  printed in Weird Tales in December 1936.76  

There were at least three reasons for this decline in productivity.  One reason was his 

growing insecurity following the rejection of At the Mountains of Madness by Weird Tales. In fact, 

Weird Tales was not the only magazine to reject his work during this period.  Strange Tales also 

rejected four of his stories, and even an effort to publish a book of Lovecraft’s stories ended in 

disappointment.  Lovecraft—humble but always confident in his abilities—was devastated by 

these rejections and began to express self-doubt.  “It is curious,” he wrote in 1934, “how the 

rejection of At the Mountains of Madness slowed up my whole writing career… The more I stand 

off as a critical nonparticipant the more avoidable faults I see in my stuff.”77  In another letter 

Lovecraft confessed that “the hostile reception” to At the Mountains of Madness “did more than 

anything else to end my effective fictional career.  The feeling that I had failed to crystalize the 

mood… robbed me in some subtle fashion of the ability to approach [writing]… with the same 

degree of confidence and fertility.”78   Lovecraft—believing that “[t]he peculiar faculty which 

 76 “The Haunter of the Dark” was a sequel to “The Shambler from the Stars” by a young friend and correspondent of his named Robert Bloch, who one day achieved worldwide fame through his novel Psycho (1959). 77 Lovecraft to Elizabeth Toldridge, August 31, 1934, in Derleth and Wandrei, eds., H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1934-1937, 24. 78 Lovecraft to E. Hoffmann Price, February 12, 1936, in Ibid., 224. 

                                                                                                                       194 

Blackwood and Dunsany possess simply isn’t mine”-- also became disinterested in his career and 

only completed eight other stories after 1931.79  In a letter to a friend in the spring of 1936, 

Lovecraft reflected on his own writing, insisting there was “(a) lack of general ability (my stuff 

never was much good…[and] (b) too much reading of pulp fiction, whereby I acquired mental 

patterns fatal to genuine expression…”80  As such, the few works he did write in the 1930s, were 

often discarded and Lovecraft did not even bother to submit them to a publisher .  Indeed, one 

story, “The Dreams in the Witch House,” written in early 1932,  was submitted for him to Weird 

Tales by friend and fellow writer August Derleth (and was published in July 1933).81  Another 

story, “The Shadow Out of Time” (1936), was also discarded by Lovecraft, and only published 

after another friend, Donald Wandrei, submitted it to Astounding Stories.  Ultimately, At the 

Mountain of Madness was published and serialized in Astounding Stories in early 1936—one year 

before Lovecraft’s death.  “[I]t is some consolation,” Lovecraft acknowledged, “to have the damn 

thing printed at last—as a posthumous effort, if nothing more.”82 

Second, in July 1932, there was the death of his seventy-six-year-old aunt, Lillian Clark, 

with whom he lived in Providence (near the John Hay Library at Brown University). “The 

suddenness of the event,” Lovecraft wrote, “is both bewildering and merciful—the latter because 

we cannot yet realize, subjectively, that it has actually occurred at all.”  To another friend he 

confided that “[t]he vacuum created in the household is easy to imagine, since my aunt was its 

presiding genius and animating spirit.  It will be impossible for me to get concentrated on any 

project of moment for some time to come—and meanwhile there intervenes the painful task of 

 79 Ibid. 80 Lovecraft to E. Hoffman Price, March 16, 1936, in Ibid., 230. 81 Joshi, H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 859. 82 Lovecraft to E. Hoffmann Price, February 12, 1936, in H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1934-1937, 224.  

                                                                                                                       195 

disturbing my aunt’s effects... whose familiar arrangements… I dread to disturb.  The family is 

now reduced to my younger aunt—living a mile from here—and myself.”  The following year, 

Lovecraft movedtz into an apartment with that aunt, Annie Gamwell, at 66 College Street.  There, 

he continued to struggle financially, receiving only a small income from the few stories he 

published.  

And third, Lovecraft seemed ready to move on from fiction by the mid-1930s.  In a letter 

from March 1936—just one year before his death—Lovecraft admitted that “the fact [is] that 

fiction is not the medium for what I really want to do.”  He was not sure what that medium was 

but speculated that “perhaps the cheapened and hackneyed term ‘prose-poem’ would hint in the 

general direction.”83  Regardless, he believed he needed “another 9-year vacation from this type 

of thing—like my silence of 1908-1917.  It will be time to begin again when I have forgotten about 

all magazines—pulp and genuine—and reading publics, and have returned to the complete state 

of isolation and spontaneous self-suiting which I possessed in 1905 and 1917… in each of which 

years I began a series of tales which virtually wrote themselves.”84  In a letter to a friend in early 

1937, Lovecraft admitted that he had not written anything since November 1935. “Other tasks 

press heavily upon me, my correspondence utterly swamps me, & my health has of late been very 

poor.”  His brief hiatus from writing, though, apparently renewed his interest in fiction.  “I had 

hoped that I might arrange for some fiction-writing leisure this winter,” he added, “but the 

prospects now seem very slim… However, whenever I do get at yarn-spinning again, I shall no 

doubt drag in the primal pre-human entities of Earth’s youth as often as of yore.”85 

 83 Lovecraft to E. Hoffman Price, March 16, 1936, in Ibid., 230. 84 Ibid. 85 Lovecraft to Arthur Widner, February 20, 1937, in Ibid., 416. 

                                                                                                                       196 

Ultimately, of course, that “yarn-spinning” was never realized.  Indeed, in February 1937, 

Lovecraft was diagnosed with terminal cancer of the small intestine, and his subsequent decline 

was rapid.  Amazingly, Lovecraft continued to keep correspondence during this last month of his 

life.  In late February—perhaps sensing that the end was near-- he wrote a friend and reflected 

upon fear and death.  “Regarding the element of fear,” he wrote, “I don’t think I share your 

immunity… I’m not especially set on living forever, although I’d dislike meeting a messy or 

disintegrative end.  I don’t bear pain well, & dodge it whenever possible.  However, I endeavor 

not to do my yelling out loud.”  In his infancy, Lovecraft recalled,  “I was afraid of the dark… but 

my grandfather cured my of that by daring me to walk through certain dark parts of the house… 

After that, dark places held a certain fascination for me.”  It was in dreams, he poignantly added, 

“that I have known the real clutch of stark, hideous, maddening, paralyzing fear… I don’ have 

such dreams now—but the memory of them will never leave me.”86    

On February 28, he wrote another friend noting that he was “completely knocked out with 

an acute intestinal trouble following 2 mos. of dragging grippe.  Don’t know how long or serious 

an ordeal I’m in for but Dr. Dustin plans to call in a stomach specialist Tuesday. Am in constant 

pain, take only liquid food, and so bloated with gas that I can’t lie down. Spend all time in chair 

propped with pillows and can read or write only a few minutes at a time. Taking 3 medicines as 

well.” A proposed book deal, he added, looked “as if all bets were off.  I have no idea what is 

ahead of me—and any systematic effort seems fantastically remote.”87 Ten days later, he was 

admitted to Jane Brown Memorial Hospital in Providence. “As he neared the end of his life,” 

according to later newspaper reports, “he turned his scholarly interest to a study of his own 

 86 Lovecraft to Harry O. Fischer, late February 1937, in Ibid., 416-417.  87 Lovecraft to Wilfred Blanch Talman, February 28,1937, in Ibid. 419-420. 

                                                                                                                       197 

personal condition and daily wrote minutely of his case for his physician’s assistance… and ended 

only when he could no longer hold a pencil.”88  By March 10, Lovecraft could no longer write and 

was fed by intravenous.  Then, early on the morning of March 15, 1937, Lovecraft died.  He was 

only forty-six years old.  

Lovecraft’s funeral was held on Thursday, March 18, in the chapel of Horace B. Knowle’s 

Sons on Benefit Street.  While some regional papers published his obituary, he was still largely 

unknown—even in Providence—and his funeral was a small affair.  Indeed, the local, Providence 

newspaper merely described him as a “Student and Writer of Fiction about Supernatural Things.”89 

He was buried near his parents and aunt at the nearby Swan Point Cemetery, located only a few 

blocks from his boyhood home at 598 Angell Street.  In the end, Lovecraft’s career revolutionized 

the horror genre by looking toward the future with a new emphasis on cosmic horror (which was 

modern) while still writing in an intense, dense style that owed more to the eighteenth and 

nineteenth centuries than it did to his own twentieth century.   While Lovecraft was indeed largely 

unknown in life, he nevertheless had loyal fans, and large correspondence with literary friends, 

including Frank Belknap Long, Reinhardt Kleiner, August Derleth, Donald Wandrei, and R. H. 

Barlow.   It was with this group of friends that Lovecraft’s legacy was begotten.  Indeed, his works 

quickly found their way into print through their diligence and thus influenced new generations of 

horror readers and writers from Robert Bloch to Stephen King.    

Writing near the end of his “Supernatural Horror in Literature” in 1927, Lovecraft 

philosophized on the future of horror and speculated that there would not be any “startling 

mutations.”  Indeed, he added, “while we may justly expect a further subtilisation [sic] of 

 88 “H.P. Lovecraft, Author, Is Dead,” Providence News-Tribune, March 16, 1937. If Lovecraft in fact kept such a journal, it no longer exists. 89 Ibid. 

                                                                                                                       198 

technique, we have no reason so think that the general position of the spectral in literature will be 

altered.  Whatever universal masterpiece of tomorrow may be wrought from phantasm or terror 

will owe its acceptance rather to a supreme workmanship than to a sympathetic theme.  Yet who 

shall declare the dark theme a positive handicap?  Radiant with beauty, the Cup of the Ptolemies 

was carven of onyx.”90   

 90 Lovecraft, “Supernatural Horror in Fiction,” in Joshi, ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1098. 

199 

### Conclusion:  The Legacy of H.P. Lovecraft

“When Lovecraft died,” author Stephen King once observed, “his work was born.”1   

Indeed, after Lovecraft’s death in 1937, the horror genre—and Lovecraft’s own influence-- 

continued to expand.  A major reason for Lovecraft’s newfound popularity was the introduction of 

his works to the book publishing world through Arkham House Publishing.  Arkham House was 

created in Saux City, Wisconsin in 1939, by August Derleth and Donald Wandrei.  The goal of 

Arkham was to serve as a publishing house for weird fiction, and to finally produce hardcover 

collections of Lovecraft’s stories—something that had never been achieved in his own lifetime.  In 

1939, Arkham House released its first Lovecraft collection, The Outsider and Others, which 

included thirty-seven stories and the essay, “Supernatural Horror in Literature,” and totaled 533 

pages.  In 1943, it was followed by a second volume, Beyond the Wall of Sleep, which included 

ninety-nine stories, poems, and essays, totaling 458 pages. Despite disputes over copyrights, 

Arkham House introduced Lovecraft’s work to a whole segment of readers who never read pulp 

magazines. By the 1960s, the publishing house was releasing paperback collections of his works, 

as well as collections of Selected Letters from Lovecraft’s vast correspondence.  His influence 

extended into the late twentieth and the early twenty-first centuries with writers as varied as 

Stephen King, Peter Straub and Thomas Ligotti.  In the early 2000s, Lovecraft reached a level of 

critical respect unheard of for a pulp writer by having his work published in Penguin Classic and 

Library of America editions.  

 1 Stephen King, “Lovecraft’s Pillow,” in Houellebecq, H.P. Lovecraft: Against the World, Against Life, 20.  

                                                                                                                       200 

  The last decade or so of Lovecraft’s life was marked by the cementing of key friendships, 

including friendships with fellow writers Donald Wanderlei, August Derleth and R.H. Barlow. 

Whatever enjoyment Lovecraft gained beyond his writing seemed to be in his correspondence and 

his frequent visits to and from old and new friends, mainly made through new connections made 

with readers and writers of Weird Tales, still the primary source of his published work. While 

Lovecraft had spent time as a failed ghost writer for writers and celebrities like Zelia Bishop, 

Adolphe De Castro, and on one occasion Harry Houdini. Throughout this vast correspondence, 

Lovecraft often reflected on good literature and best practices in writing. Sometimes his advice 

was very pragmatic.  For example, a key to effective writing, he told a friend in 1929, was the 

“complete swearing-off of the cinema and of cheap magazines. You can’t bury that stuff too deeply 

out of sight and memory for your own artistic good!”2  At other times, he was much more technical, 

insisting that writers had to transcend a “fivefold problem”: 

1. To get the facts of life. 2. To think straight and tell the truth. 3. To cut out maudlin and extravagant emotion. 4. To cultivate an ear for strong, direct, harmonious, simple, and graphic 

language. 5. To write what one really sees and feels.3 

Ultimately, his advice—and more importantly, his stories—inspired a whole new generation of 

horror writers. 

Robert Hayward Barlow (1918-1951) spent most of youth in Fort Benning, Georgia.  In 

the early 1930s, when he was only thirteen, Barlow began his correspondence with Lovecraft about 

weird fiction. The two began writing each other after Barlow wrote to Weird Tales in 1931, 

 2 Lovecraft to Zealia Brown Reed, August 28, 1929, in H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1929-1931, 15.  3 Ibid. 

                                                                                                                       201 

expressing his admiration for Lovecraft’s stories.  When the fan letter—which included a request 

for an autograph-- was forwarded to him, Lovecraft-- unaware of Barlow’s young age—responded 

in appreciation, writing that he hoped Barlow would enjoy his newest work, “The Whisperer In 

Darkness” to be published in Weird Tales that September.4 “As for an autograph-- something of 

very little value indeed as connected with an obscure nonentry like myself-- if the following 

signature won’t do,” Lovecraft wrote.  “I’ll be glad to sign any old thing expect cheques on my 

largely non-existent bank account & other documents prejudicial to my life, liberty, & pursuit of 

tolerable contentment! The demand for autographs has not so far been such as to force me to 

procure a multiple writing apparatus or use a rubber stamp!”5   

  Lovecraft trusted Barlow enough to have him prepare in typescript, Lovecraft’s two short 

unpublished novels The Dream-Quest of Kadath and The Case of Charles Dexter Ward.  It was 

also during this period—i.e. the early 1930s-- that Barlow officially invited Lovecraft to visit him 

and his family in DeLand, Florida.  “As I’ve said, consult the convenience of yourself & family in 

setting a-or no-date for advent,” Lovecraft wrote Barlow in April 1934.  “I needn’t reiterate how 

honored I feel by your hospitable invitation.”6  Ultimately, Lovecraft visited the Barlows in Florida 

twice—in both the summers of 1934 and 1935.7  Barlow himself later wrote about the visits, and 

their conversations about the “fantastic tales which he wrote and which I wanted to write.”8   

According to Barlow, Lovecraft was “more important as a man who had the integrity to ignore the 

machine age and its frenzied leveling-out-to rubble of life’s rich irregularities, who had the courage 

 4 Lovecraft to R.H. Barlow, June 25, 1931, in Joshi & Schultz, eds., O Fortunate Floridian: H.P. Lovecraft’s Letters to R.H. Barlow. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2016), 3. 5 Ibid. 6 Ibid. 7 Barlow, in turn, visited Lovecraft in Providence in 1936, staying for over a month. 8 H.P. Lovecraft. “The Wind That Is in the Grass: A Memoir of H.P. Lovecraft in Florida.” in Joshi and Schultz, eds., O Fortunate Floridian: H.P. Lovecraft’s Letters to R.H. Barlow. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2016), xxx. 

                                                                                                                       202 

to study and think and converse and write, and to stimulate others to study and think and converse 

and write, in accordance with the deeper traditions of a more orderly age.”9  

Donald Wandrei (1908-1987), meanwhile, was a writer of considerable skill, specifically 

in poetry-- despite his later and more high-profile association with Arkham House and pulp 

fiction.10 Wanderlei was a major fan of weird fiction and while still a teenager began to correspond 

with horror writer Clark Ashton Smith. Wanderlei and Smith bonded on their mutual love of 

Lovecraft, whom Smith insisted was “great-- his horror stories are totally unlike those of anyone 

else.”11 By 1926, Lovecraft and Wandrei had begun corresponding and sending each other 

manuscripts. Lovecraft and Wandrei also spent much time trading works of fiction between each 

other.  Wandrei often sent Lovecraft volumes of early science fiction, and Lovecraft reciprocated 

by sending Wanderlei books by Lord Dunsany.  The two also promoted each other’s literary 

pursuits.  Indeed, Lovecraft praised Wandrei’s short story. “The Twilight of Time”—later published 

as “The Red Brain” in Weird Tales in 1927-- and promoted it to editor Farnsworth Wright.  

Meanwhile, Wandrei was instrumental in the eventual of “The Call of Cthulhu” in Weird Tales.   

Wanderlei continued his fierce promotion of Lovecraft after the Rhode Islander’s death in 1937, 

and was a key curator of Lovecraft’s legacy, specifically through the publishing house, Arkham 

House. 

Then there was August Derleth (1909-1971), a pulp writer from Sauk City, Wisconsin.  At 

the age of seventeen, his story “Bat’s Belfry” (1926) was published in Weird Tales.  Shortly 

thereafter, Derleth initiated correspondence with Lovecraft, writing him in July 1926 about an old 

 9 H.P. Lovecraft. “The Wind That Is in the Grass: A Memoir of H.P. Lovecraft in Florida.” in Ibid., xxxiii. 10 Wanderlei published his first book of poetry Ecstasy and Other Poems in 1928. Lovecraft was influenced by Wanderlei’s poetic works, Sonnets of the Midnight Hours (1927), as evident in Lovecraft’s own collection of sonnets, written in 1929-1930, and entitled Fungi from Yuggoth. 11 “Clark Ashton Smith to Donald Wanderlei, December 11, 1924.” Minnesota Historical Society.  

                                                                                                                       203 

letter that Lovecraft had written for Weird Tales years earlier.  “In this letter,” Derleth wrote, “you 

mention two tales, The House of Sounds by MP. Shiel and The Pale Ape.  Your note on these tales 

caught my fancy at once and I would like to know if their stories are procurable in the United 

States, and where, and at what price.”12  Over the course of the next eleven years, Lovecraft— 

whose first impression of Derleth was that he “promises to develop into something of a fantaisiste 

[sic]”-- wrote to him over three hundred and eighty letters.13  The two regularly traded manuscripts 

and often argued good-naturedly about books and writing.  

In 1937, a few days after Lovecraft’s death, Barlow arrived in Providence to find 

Lovecraft’s Aunt Anne in mourning. It was at that point that among Lovecraft’s private papers,  a 

document entitled “Instructions in Case of Decease,” was found.  It declared Barlow—perhaps 

Lovecraft’s closest friend in the 1930s-- his “literary executor.” Anne was able to get the paper 

legalized by affidavit shortly afterwards, and Barlow was given first choice of Lovecraft’s 2,000 

volumes, and received ownership of all his manuscripts and magazines.14 Barlow felt that 

Lovecraft’s papers belonged in a public archive and so donated them to the John Hay Library at 

Brown University. Over the next two years, Barlow published two issues of a small magazine, 

Leaves (1937-1938), highlighting Lovecraft’s work, and then, in 1938, published a small edition 

of Lovecraft’s Notes and Commonplace Book (1938), a small book in which he kept with his story 

ideas. 

 12 Derleth to H.P. Lovecraft, July 30, 1926, in Schultz & Joshi, eds., Essential Solitude: The Letters of H.P. Lovecraft and August Derleth: 1932-1937. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 27. 13 Lovecraft to Clark Ashton Smith, October 12, 1926, in Derleth and Wandrei, eds., H.P. Lovecraft: Selected Letters, 1925-1929, 77. 14 H.P. Lovecraft. “Instructions in Case of Decease”, in Joshi ed., Collected Essays: Volume 5: Philosophy: Autobiography & Miscellany. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2004), 237-240. 

                                                                                                                       204 

Controversy, though, soon erupted over control of Lovecraft’s works and legacy.  Barlow 

had not seemed to inform others in Lovecraft’s circle of friends that he had received the rights of 

a literary executor, and it could of course be argued that he didn’t have to inform anyone of 

anything anyway. Derleth from the time of Lovecraft’s death had formulated plans to publish 

Lovecraft. At first Derleth attempted at finding a publisher for Lovecraft’s stories but had very 

little luck. It was at this point that Derleth and Wanderlei decided together that the most practical 

way to publish Lovecraft’s work was with the creation of their own publishing company which 

they then called Arkham House containing a little nod to Lovecraft’s fictional city. Wanderlei, 

specifically felt that Barlow had stolen Lovecraft’s manuscripts and books and began to spread 

rumors to that effect, even causing Barlow’s friend Clark Ashton Smith to send a two sentence 

letter informing Barlow that he wanted no further contact with him.15  While Barlow did his best 

to assist Derleth and Wanderlei with the first Lovecraft imprint, Derleth successfully pushed 

Barlow out of the partnership.  Barlow, then, moved to San Francisco in 1939 where he completed 

Poems for a Competition (1942) and View from a Hill (1947).  Supported by both the Guggenheim 

and Rockefeller Foundations, Barlow moved to Mexico in 1943, where eventually he became the 

chairman of the Anthropology Department at Mexico City College.  It was there, in 1951, that the 

thirty-three-year-old Barlow, a homosexual fearful of being revealed by a disgruntled student, 

committed suicide.16   

With Barlow out of the picture, Wanderlei and Derleth moved to publish Lovecraft’s work 

in book form. After both Charles Scribner’s Sons and Simon & Schuster rejected their Lovecraft 

 15 H.P. Lovecraft. “Introduction.” In Joshi and Schultz, eds., O Fortunate Floridian: H.P. Lovecraft’s Letters to R.H. Barlow. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2016), xix. 16 In 2003, a volume of Barlow’s poetry and stories were published as Eyes of the God: The Weird Fiction and Poetry of R.H. Barlow.  

                                                                                                                       205 

proposal, the two decided to pay for a small press to publish the work—including his vast 

correspondence-- themselves.17 Both men obtained a wealth of material by reaching out to 

Lovecraft’s friends asking for letters, manuscripts and even old amateur magazines where much 

of Lovecraft’s early work was published.  In a spark of genius, Derleth also began submitting many 

of Lovecraft’ early stories-- originally only published  in amateur journals-- to Weird Tales, where 

they were now eagerly embraced and published.18 Eventually, with the help of subscribers and 

much of their own finances, The Outsider and Other Stories found bookshelves as the first volume 

from Arkham House-- and the first Lovecraft work to be properly published-- in 1939. While the 

collection only contained thirty-seven Lovecraft stories, this first Arkham publication truly offered 

up some of Lovecraft’s most memorable, including “The Outsider,” “The Tomb,” “Dagon,” “The 

Picture in the House,” “The Call of Cthulhu,” “The Dunwich Horror,” and Lovecraft’s beloved At 

the Mountains of Madness. 

Arkham House continued publishing under Derleth’s leadership over the next thirty years 

publishing not only Lovecraft but other well-known horror writers as well. Derleth’s collection, 

Someone In The Dark was the second publication by Arkham House because he was “keeping the 

Arkham House imprint in the public eye while other Lovecraft works were in preparation.”19  

Then, in 1943, a second Lovecraft collection called Beyond the Wall of Sleep was released.  The 

decade of the forties was a struggle for Arkham House as they grew from a company dedicated to 

publishing Lovecraft to a small printing house that published both established and younger horror 

writers. In 1945, they published Robert Bloch’s The Opener of the Way as well as the companies 

first deep delve into the fantasy genre: the novel Evangeline Walton’s Witch House. By the 1950’s 

 17 August Derleth. Arkham House: The First 20 Years:1939-1959. (Sauk City: Arkham House, 1959), 3-4. 18 Derleth made sure that all payments for these stories when to Lovecraft’s Aunt Anne. 19 August Derleth. Arkham House: The First 20 Years:1939-1959. (Sauk City: Arkham House, 1959), 5. Arkham House also began publishing other Lovecraft associates, Clark Ashton Smith’s Out of Space and Time (1942). 

                                                                                                                       206 

after some financial issues Arkham House was releasing works by such acclaimed young authors 

as Ray Bradbury. 

 However, times were still difficult for Arkham House and the horror genre in general. Pulp 

magazines and dime store novels were still the primary way to read such genre works. Works by 

Lovecraft and his contemporaries were still out of fashion. This can be seen by observing that 

perhaps the most well-known horror writer of the 1940’s and 1950’s was Shirley Jackson, who had 

more in common as a writer with the gothic novels of Henry James or Edith Wharton than with 

“weird” or “cosmic” fiction. Cinema had long been growing more popular and now with film code 

fully in use, horror films were becoming more popular with the youth population. The arrival of 

comic books, including the popular EC Comics who began to specialize in horror comics was sure 

to have had a direct influence on the fate of pulp magazines which before had taken much of their 

readership from the youth population. The literary world was changing due to the changing media 

of the time; however, Lovecraft’s legacy benefited from such an occurrence, especially as the 

1960’s came into view. However, a controversy surrounded Lovecraft’s legacy during this time, 

primarily because of Derleth. 

In the mid-1940s, Derleth also released the somewhat controversial The Lurker at the 

Threshold in which he based the stories on fragments and notes written by Lovecraft and used this 

work to promote his ideas on “The Cthulhu Mythos.”. Shortly after Lovecraft’s death, in a small 

magazine called River, Derleth stated that “Bit by bit it grew, and finally its outlines became 

distinct, and it was given a name” the Cthulhu Mythology: because it was in “The Call of Cthulhu 

that the myth-pattern first became apparent.” And in the same article also said how the formula 

was “remarkable for the fact that, though it sprang from the mind of a professed religious 

unbeliever, it is basically similar to the Christian mythos, particular in the regard to the expulsion 

                                                                                                                       207 

of Satan from Eden and the power of evil.”20 Previously, with the use of Lovecraft’s letters a basis 

for Lovecraft’s worldview was stated and Derleth at this time seemed to be imprinting his ideals 

on Lovecraft’s work with prior consultation of Lovecraft’s views. Now, with the passing of 

Lovecraft and his ability to publish his works without a copyright at the time, more than likely due 

to the fact that not many companies were attempting at publishing him, Derleth could imprint his 

ideas on Lovecraft’s notes and sketches for unwritten stories. It was not until 1959, that Derleth 

even stated that that stories were written after Lovecraft’s death, a fact that caused much confusion. 

Ironically enough it was because of Derleth, that “The Cthulhu Mythos” became probably the most 

popular reference to Lovecraft.  

By Derleth’s death in 1971, compilations of stories by Derleth primarily on the “mythos” 

including The Mask of Cthulhu (1958) and The Trial of Cthulhu (1962) were published creating a 

industry that continues to this day with various writers publishing “Cthulhu” stories or what would 

be called “Fan Fiction” today. However, some of Lovecraft’s friends like the young author Fritz 

Lieber stated in the late 1940’s that “I believe that it is a mistake to regard the entities as parts of 

Christan demonology, or to attempt to divide them into balancing Zoroastrian hierarchies of good 

and evil.”21 Despite more aware Lovecraft readers, The “Cthulhu Myhtos” became one of the more 

popular aspects of the Lovecraft legacy.  

Lovecraft’s work began to find notice in the 1960’s in industries that he would  never have 

expected if he had been alive. These industries were specifically in the realms of radio and cinema. 

Much of this spark in Lovecraftian interest was lightly connected with the works of up-and-coming 

horror authors who while not specifically writing in Lovecraft’s style had their work associated 

 20 August Derleth, “H.P. Lovecraft, Outsider.” River 1 No., 3 June 1937, 8. 21 Fritz Lieber “A Literary Copernicus.” in August Derleth, ed., Something About Cats and Other piece. (Sauk City: Arkham House, 1949), 294.  

                                                                                                                       208 

with Lovecraft and Edgar Allan Poe. The American Independent Film Director Roger Corman 

(1926-2024) while searching for a horror film which could be recognized instantly by name and 

yet be in the public domain was good draw for an audience and prevented him from paying 

royalties to an estate. Corman put his bets on the works of Edgar Allan Poe and with the help of 

the acclaimed actor Vincent Price (1921-1993) began what is known as his “Poe Cycle.”   

Horror cinema had become by the 1960s commercially satisfiable for motion picture 

production companies. In the 1930s during the tail end of Lovecraft’s life and career, films such 

as Dracula (1931) and Frankenstein (1932) had made the genre commercially viable especially in 

the age of the “talkie.” In Europe established Directors such as Carl Theodore Dryer, most famous 

for his film The Trial of Joan of Arc made the Avant grade Vampyr (1931) showing that even among 

the intellectual community horror cinema was in vogue. However, after 1934 due to the Hays code, 

a system created by the Motion Picture Producers and Distributors of America to regulate 

guidelines that a film must abide by, horror films could not effectually use much of its tools since 

they were considered in some instances profane. During the 1940’s much of horror cinema, except 

for the films of Val Lewton best known for producing the film Cat People (1941), had comedic 

aspects to them and were not as artistically credible as in the past. This, however, may have led to 

a crucial transformation in the industry, the rise of independent filmmakers. Directors such as John 

Cassavetes and Roger Corman began to produce, direct and distribute their own films. While 

Cassavetes produced mainly dramas, Corman worked in genre film giving young directors such as 

Martin Scorsese and Francis Ford Coppola to work in the film industry. Horror was commercially 

viable and so Corman made such films.  

Another important idea that can be attributed to Corman was the usage of horror writers  

as screenwriters for his films. Many of the writers were members of  “The Southern California 

                                                                                                                       209 

Group” and were among the best in the genre at the time. Perhaps the most well-known of the 

group was Richard Matherson (1926-2013) whose first work I Am Legend is among one of the 

most important horror works of the mid-20th century with its combination of both the gothic and 

science fiction genres that while not “cosmic” were definitely influential. Other works of
