importance included The Incredible Shrinking Man (1956), A Stir of Echoes (1958), Hell House 

(1971), and What Dreams May Come (1978). H wrote the adaptations of four of the Corman films 

The House of Usher (1960), The Pit and the Pendulum (1961) Tales of Terror (1962) and The 

Raven (1963). 

Another pivotal member of the group was Ray Russell (1924-1999), a writer who was not 

as well known as Matherson but whose central work had a deep influence on some of the more 

famous horror trends of the next decades. Russell spent much of his career as the executive editor 

of Playboy magazine and transformed the magazine to include stories and articles by such writers 

as Ray Bradbury and Kurt Vonnegut along with his friends Richard Matherson and Charles 

Beaumont. Russell is perhaps most known today for his religiously themed gothic novel The Case 

Against Satan (1962) which much like a work of Lovecraft’s brings a situation from the past, in 

this case an exorcism into a modern context. The work is quite similar to one of the biggest book 

sellers of the next decade, Willam Peter Blatty’s The Exorcist (1971) which created a new genre 

of religiously themed horror novels and films. Russell would collaborate with Charles Beaumont 

on Corman’s adaptation of The Premature Burial (1962).  

Charles Beaumont (1929-1967) was perhaps the most mysterious of the group best known 

today for his story “Perchance to Dream” (1958) which he adapted into a well-known Twilight 

Zone episode and for his early death from what was more than likely a combination of Alzheimer’s 

and Picks disease. Matherson, Russell, and Beaumont wrote for The Twilight Zone, much of the 

                                                                                                                       210 

time adapting their own stories for the screen, making their names well known to T.V. audiences. 

Beaumont wrote one of the Poe adaptions for Corman, The Haunted Palace, it was among the first 

adaptations of a Lovecraft story, in this case the novella The Case of Charles Dexter Ward. Because 

of the former films in the cycle being Poe works, and their placement in the public domain, the 

title of the film was changed to reflect a Poe work for continuity’s sake, though the plot of the film 

is recognizable as a Lovecraft work. Interestingly enough, this incident did not seem to be noticed 

by August Derleth, who saw himself as the owner of Lovecraft’s work. At this time Lovecraft’s 

work was also adapted to radio with “The Dunwich Horror” as early as 1949. Derleth seemed to 

gain momentum from Lovecraft’s newfound visibility in the visual arts publishing three volumes 

of Lovecraft’s selected letters as well as the collections The Dunwich Horror and Others (1963), 

At the Mountains of Madness and Other Novels (1964) and, Dagon and other Macabre Tales 

(1965) as well as a volume called Collected Poems (1963). New writers were also beginning to 

find inspiration from the “Cthulhu Mythos” included J. Ramsey Cambell who became one of the 

better horror writers to emerge from these fan fiction origins. Derleth died on July 4, 1971, leaving 

behind a complicated legacy. Derleth popularized Lovecraft while attempting at transforming 

Lovecraft’s radical worldview to his much more conservative ones. After Derleth’s death, critical 

study of Lovecraft became a major scholastic venture also for the first time major publishing 

companies such as Ballantine books were able to publish his work. This was an important factor 

in Lovecraft’s transformation into the mainstream since S.T. Joshi believes that if Derleth had 

waited longer before publishing Lovecraft himself, mainstream companies may have published his 

work first changing the whole course of weird literature in 20th century.22 Alas, this would not be 

 22 S.T. Joshi. I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 1034. 

                                                                                                                       211 

but a critical study of Lovecraft was attempted before the juncture of Derleth’s death leaving at 

least a basis for future scholastic concerns.  

As far back as the 1940’s Lovecraft had begun to gain critical study. An important magazine 

that helped in this regard was the creation of The Acolyte, a fan magazine that nevertheless became 

an important platform for critical work about Lovecraft. During the first few years after Lovecraft’s 

death most of the critical study around him came from amateur magazines.  In the small magazine 

Scienti-Snaps a man named J. Chapman Miske’s wrote a biographical article named “H.P. 

Lovecraft: Strange Weaver” in which he nearly foresaw Lovecraft’s reputation in the future by 

saying that “Lovecraft was a eccentric to the point of being born ‘out of due time’. Not freakish, 

simply different, by temperament, tastes, and, to certain degrees, actions…Lovecraft is dead, but 

the strange patterns he wrote will always be appreciated by a small but intelligent group.”23  

In 1944, an early horror treatise called “A Treatise on Tales of Horror” was written by 

Edmund Wilson and published in The New Yorker. Wilson was not a major fan of weird fiction or 

pulp magazines in general. Wilson stated that “Lovecraft was not a good writer. The fact that his 

verbose and undistinguished style has been compared to Poe’s is only one of the many sad signs 

that almost nobody any more pays real attention to writing.” but then states surprisingly that 

“Lovecraft’s stories do show at times some traces of his more serious emotions and interests. He 

had a scientific imagination rather similar, though inferior to that of early Wells.”24 Wilson’s work 

had a small influence on Lovecraft’s reputation among the mainstream creating an animosity 

toward him for the next thirty years among serious critics.  

 23 J. Chapman Miske. “H.P. Lovecraft: Strange Weaver.” Scienti-Snaps 3. No.3. Summer 1940, 9. 24 Edmund Wilson. “A Treatise of Horror” The New Yorker. May 14, 1944, 72.  

                                                                                                                       212 

While In America, many of Lovecraft’s works fell out of print, In Europe Lovecraft was 

gaining popularity with celebrities as famous as the director Jean Cocteau stating that “Mr. 

Lovecraft, is an American who invents a terrifying world of space-time.”25 The Swiss scholar Peter 

Penzoldt also mentioned Lovecraft in the well-known horror survey The Supernatural in Fiction 

(1952). Even Lovecraft’s former wife Sonia Greene wrote a short memoir that was published in 

the Providence Sunday Journal as “Howard Philips Lovecraft as His Wife Remembers Him.” 

Lovecraft was also influenced late twentieth century horror cinema with at least two 

adaptations of Lovecraft’s work occurring in the 1980’s. These included Stewert Gordons 

Reanimator (1985) and From Beyond (1987). However, Lovecraft’s work has occurred more as 

an influence than as adaptation in most modern day cinema. Christoper Sharett in his work “The 

Haunter of the Dark: H.P. Lovecraft and Modern Horror Cinema” (2015) describes how 

Lovecraft’s views are a little to nihilistic and influenced by the ideas of Nietzsche, Schopenhauer 

and, Oswald Spengler to have a pure influence on cinema, however; many films have recycled his 

plot points and ideals in more commercial terms.26 An example of this is Howard Hawks’s seminal 

horror-science fiction film The Thing from Another World (1951) which while using as its source 

the John W. Campbell story “Who Goes There?” was actually based on for all purposes Lovecraft’s 

masterwork At The Mountain of Madness (1931).27 The 1982 remake of The Thing  by John 

Carpenter was even more influenced by the ideas and atmosphere of Lovecraft’s stories. This 

makes perfect sense when considering the fact that John Carpenter is a renowned Lovecraft fan 

 25 Jean Cocteau. “Books of 1954: A Symposium.” Observer. December 26, 1954, 7.  26 Christoper Sharrett. “The Haunter of the Dark: H.P. Lovecraft and Modern Horror Cinema” Cineaste Vol 41, No. 1 (Winter 2015), 23. https://www.jstor.org/stable/26356385?searchText=Lovecraft&searchUri=%2Faction%2FdoBasicSearch%3FQuery %3DLovecraft&ab_segments=0%2Fbasic_search_gsv2%2Fcontrol&refreqid=fastlydefault%3Ad1d1e4eb4eadcd481be6989bab76af46 27 Ibid., 24. 

                                                                                                                       213 

and created a cinematic homage to Lovecraft called In The Mouth of Madness (1994), a direct 

homage in title to Lovecraft’s work as well as in the usage of “The Old One’s” as the villains of 

his film. There have been at least two adaptions of Lovecraft’s works in television shows: 

specifically, his work “The Dreams In The Witch-House” which was adapted into an episode of 

the showtime show Masters of Horror in 2006 and Guillermo Del Toro’s Cabinet of Curiosities 

in 2022.   

In the early 2000’s, Lovecraft’s work officially became critically respectable and was 

recognized as an important cultural figure. Penguin Classics, among the most respectable 

publishing agencies, published three volumes of Lovecraft’s work between the years of 1999 and 

2004, annotated by Lovecraft scholar S.T. Joshi. The company Dell also released two volumes 

between the years 1997 and 1999. In 2005, The Library of America released a copy of Lovecraft’s 

Tales with an introduction by horror writer Peter Straub which went on to sell 25,000 copies within 

a few months. The Modern Library also released an annotated version of At the Mountains of 

Madness and “Supernatural Horror in Literature.” Lovecraft was greeted by “a majority of 

reviewers [who] welcomed his ascent into the company of Melville, Fitzgerald and Faulkner.”28 

Lovecraft and the horror genre had finally found its recognized plate in the classic literary 

establishment.  

History is—in part, at least—an inquiry into lives and their journeys. Some lives are tied 

together in a common goal on a long thread through time. In this case, this has been the journey of 

many writers in the horror genre. In the beginning the horror genre was marked by gothic subtexts 

that emerged from folkloric images. These themes were developed through the late seventeenth 

 28 S.T. Joshi. I Am Providence: The Life and Times of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 1041. 

                                                                                                                       214 

century and early eighteenth century as writers like Mary Shelly in Europe and Washington Irving 

in the United States bought interesting new subtexts to the genre by adding satirical and 

philosophical themes. In the mid nineteenth century, the genre had perhaps its most important 

writer emerge: Edgar Allan Poe. Poe added new elements to horror literature by creating the “weird 

fiction” subgenre as well as emerging as perhaps the most famous American poet of the era 

reaching aspects of commercial and critical success unknown for not only a horror writer but an 

American author. Other writers like Nathainel Hawthrone and Ambrose Bierce in the mid 

nineteenth to the late nineteenth century continued to write in the gothic and “weird” traditions 

giving the genre a respectable and a philosophical meaning.  

The media surrounding the horror genre transformed as well, and it had both positive and 

negative effects. Magazines such as Penny Dreadfuls began to commercialize genre fiction as well 

as the horror genre gaining many new readers but losing critical favor. Cinema also led to more 

people being interested in the horror genre for its commercial aspects. Out of this new culture 

emerged the pulp magazine, an entity that published genre fiction cheaply to gain a wide and varied 

public. From the pulps emerged interesting and sometimes great writers such as Robert E. Howard 

and Clark Ashton Smith. Lovecraft emerged from the pulp magazines and attempted to merge his 

philosophical ideals and meticulous compositions into the “weird” horror genre. While unheralded 

during his lifetime, his work emerged in the twentieth century due to its combination of both the 

modernist and traditional tendencies of his work, influencing the way horror was written 

afterwards. Yes, horror could still be commercial, but it could also be taken as a serious genre with 

critical acclaim and artistic tendencies. While Poe’s work shows that such a merging of serious 

literary ideals and genre fiction could be practiced, it was with Lovecraft that a writer emerged 

                                                                                                                       215 

with such well formulated work that its ideals became contagious among all creators of art, 

eventually becoming part of the serious literary tradition. 

     Lovecraft died in 1937 and was laid to rest in his beloved Providence, where “the spires 

of Providence ascend against the ancient skies” and where the story of his life can still be found 

among its landscape-his spirit lingers over its century old stones.29  Today, his home in Providence 

where he lived with his mother and spent much of his adolescence and early adulthood can still be 

seen on 598 Angell Street- Two establishments where Lovecraft spent much of his time, the 

Providence Athenaeum and the Ladd Observatory--  where he spent his adolescence gazing at the 

stars and falling in love with the distant cosmos--  are still visited by inquiring minds. Much of the 

landscape seen in Lovecraft’s stories can be found among the city’s architecture.   Indeed, the 

Industrial National Bank Building—still the tallest building in Rhode Island--was described in 

Lovecraft’s last story “The Haunter of the Dark” as the “red Industrial Trust beacon [that] had 

blazed up to make the night grotesque.”30 The building now lies vacant towering like a ghost over 

downtown waiting to be rediscovered as Lovecraft had waited for rediscovery decades after his 

death.31   

 Even during Lovecraft’s three-year residence in Brooklyn, New York, Providence never 

strayed far from his thoughts.  Indeed, in 1924, during his troubled stay in New York, Lovecraft 

composed the poem “Providence,” and submitted it for publication in the local paper, The 

Brooklynite: 

 29 H.P. Lovecraft “Providence.” in Joshi, ed., The Ancient Track: The Complete Poetical Works of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 303.  30 Lovecraft, “The Haunter In The Dark” in Joshi ed., H.P. Lovecraft: The Complete Fiction, 1001. 31 While there are no museums in the city that carry his name, the oldest indoor shopping mall in America, the Arcade, includes a store named Lovecraft Arts and Sciences that is dedicated to not only Lovecraft but weird literature in general. At nearby John Hay Library (at Brown University), where many Lovecraft’s original manuscripts are kept, a memorial plaque was placed on the centennial of his birth celebrating his life and influence. 

                                                                                                                       216 

Where bay and river tranquil blend, 

And leafy hillsides rise, 

The spires of Providence ascend 

Against the ancient skies. 

Here centuried domes of shining gold 

Salute the morning’s glare, 

While slanting gables, odd and old, 

Are scatter’d here and there. 

And in the narrow winding ways 

That climb o’er slope and crest, 

The magic of forgotten days 

May still be found to rest. 

A fanlight’s gleam, a knocker’s blow, 

A glimpse of Georgian brick— 

The sights and sounds of long ago 

Where fancies cluster thick. 

A flight of steps with iron rail, 

A belfry looming tall, 

A slender steeple, carv’d and pale, 

A moss-grown garden wall. 

A hidden churchyard’s crumbling proofs 

Of man’s mortality, 

A rotting wharf where gambrel roofs 

Keep watch above the sea. 

                                                                                                                       217 

Square and parade, whose walls have tower’d 

Full fifteen decades long 

By cobbled ways ’mid trees embower’d, 

And slighted by the throng. 

Stone bridges spanning languid streams, 

Houses perch’d on the hill, 

And courts where mysteries and dreams 

The brooding spirit fill. 

Steep alley steps by vines conceal’d, 

Where small-pan’d windows glow 

At twilight on a bit of field 

That chance has left below. 

My Providence! What airy hosts 

Turn still thy gilded vanes; 

What winds of elf that with grey ghosts 

People thine ancient lanes! 

The chimes of evening as of old 

Above thy valleys sound, 

While thy stern fathers ’neath the mould 

Make blest thy sacred ground. 

Thou dream’st beside the waters there, 

Unchang’d by cruel years; 

A spirit from an age more fair 

That shines behind our tears. 

                                                                                                                       218 

Thy twinkling lights each night I see, 

Tho’ time and space divide; 

For thou art of the soul of me, 

And always at my side!32 

A few miles northeast of Brown University lies Lovecraft’s final resting place at Swan Point 

Cemetery. Only recently—since 1977-- did Lovecraft, who originally had his name attached 

to an obelisk along with his mother in the family plot, receive his own headstone through 

donations from fans. His gravesite now stands as a place of pilgrimage for those who love the 

literary arts. And on his gravestone-- for those who visit to see-- reads his immortal epitaph--

“I am Providence.”   

 32 H.P. Lovecraft “Providence.” in Joshi, ed., The Ancient Track: The Complete Poetical Works of H.P. Lovecraft. (New York: Hippocampus Press, 2013), 303-305.
